Che Ways of many Waters
Collects the dashing ballads and verses of sailor-
men’s and wharf-men’s life which E. J. Brady has
contributed to The Bulletin and the Sunday
Times, Sydney. It is the first attempt on the
part of an Australian verse-writer to chant the
life on the sea and the margins of the sea ; though
there is one horse-poem— the story of “ How Jack
Bowlin Steered ‘Jones.’” The volume is bound
in buckram, with gilt top, in a size that will go
into a pocket without squeezing j and the prioe is

THE RED PAGE.
If I Could Paint,
AND were still an Australian, I would not
worry too much about old masters, sunset
studies, or even horses ; you can study and
enjoy real sunset effects any day and almost
anywhere—in University Park for preference and
convenience ; and Australia is already .smothered
by the horse. Sunlight and scenery endure, and
there are plenty to paint them, but types and
classes are passing away or changing rapidly in
Australia. I’d be prouder of a picture like
“ Breaking the News ” than of a hundred exquisite
alleged studies in “the nood” —which, as they
are, are rather less satisfactory than a study in the
latest Paris fashion would be. The most beautiful
studies in the nude are of children ; but go to
Manly Beach any summer day for the real thing—
with the moving grace and laughter thrown in.
My ambition would be to paint Australia as it
is, and as it changes: pictures that Australians
could look through—and through a mist of tears,
perhaps—back into their pasts : pictures that Aus-
tralians could look through and onward to a
brighter and nobler future. Pictures that the
“ careless ” joker might stand in front of chuck-
ling comfortably to himself, and feel less lonely
and cynical in his heart than careless jokers are
apt to feel. Pictures that might soften hard eyes
and mouths, and so ease hard hearts. Pictures
showing the best and noblest sides of human
nature, so that the world might keep its faith in
it, and love it, or, failing that, be more charitable
and tolerant towards it. Pictures showing the
worst side of humanity, the poverty, misery, and
squalid vice, that men might hate the greed and
selfishness that cause it all. Pictures that would
bring hot tears to the eyes and fire the hearts of
men. And pictures that would make men laugh
and be cheerful in our time.
And if I went to Europe it would be to learn to
paint Australia—not old-world scenes : those who
are old-world-bom can do that better than we. A
Dutch tavern-scene looks picturesque to us—pro-
bably on account of the costumes; but a real
Dutch tavern-scene mightn’t be any more pic-
turesque than our own haggard threepenny-bar.
Pictures of our threepenny-bars might set men
wishing for a different style of pub. and a different
fashion in drinking. The narrow bar—for most
things made by men are narrow and haggard in
this thin, faded time of ours; no seat, except per-
haps a ledge under the window (for seats also, like
verandahs, have narrowed and disappeared : it is
only intended that men should stand in front of a
bar, and drink as long as they can pay for it, and
then go away ; the dmgy little “ parlors ” are for
the accommodation of “ molls ” and their acquaint-
ances). The unpicturesque, unredeemed guzzling
on one side ; and undisguised “ business ” on the
«*ther.
And the humorous side of it, “Breaking the
Law, ” for instance : A group representing or sug-
gesting as many sorts and conditions of ordinary
sinners as possible, by a side-entrance of a cross-
street hotel on Sunday or after hours; one
stooping down to listen or speak through the
keyhole, or giving the knock; make a study
of. his face the face of a born leader in
mischief or otherwise. The atmosphere of
school-boyish guiltiness and watchfulness, with
something of the uneasiness born of mature cau-
tion or experience. The two or three interested
oasuals—without the knowledge of the “lay of
the country; ” or, maybe, without the pluck to
battle for themselves—hanging about the opposite
corner with a poor show of neutrality, and ready
either to stroll on with an assumption of indiffer-
ence at the sight of a policeman, or to slip across
and in after the bolder spirits as soon as a breach
is made. The population of the alley in sym-
pathy, and aiding and abetting, maybe, with
advice and signals. All human in our times.
And, as a companion picture, the bar
lighted by a dim point of gas, or in the dusk
behind drawn blinds. An air as of school-boys
safe in hiding and enjoying stolen fruits after an
orchard-robbing expedition, yet alert and ready
for a surprise ; the irrepressible wink and the soul-
ful grin of the jokers. The one or two dead-
earnest, matter-of-fact characters —generally small
contractors or bosses, who take everything (drink
included) as seriously as they do their jobs and
the “men.”
All human.
The Saturday afternoon drunk who gets drunk
by accident—as we all do, if we do it at all—who
has lost his mates, or is temporarily parted from
them, and has passed the point when he’ll either
go home with the bulk of his wages or make a
night of it; see him with his back to a support,
solemnly arguing or putting the case to himself,
and striking off the main points (for or against)
with one forefinger across the other. The boozers’
boat on Saturday night. And—well, it’s a pity
that there is so much humor, wit, and sympathy
in drinking. Isn’t it? . . . But change to
Faddy’s Market, Saturday night—a world of
types, pathos, and tragedy there. The usual
haggard-featured mother going from one entrance
to another of the markets; a boarding-house
keeper with two or three grown-up, white-shirted,
stand-oip-and-turn-down-collared (mother does all
their linenJherself) straw-hatted, cigarette-smoking
sons (boarding at 'home and paying 12s. or 15s. a
week), apeing men-about-town in King-street to-
night. She has the ordinary load of Sunday-
dinner-stuff on one arm, and a great, peevish brat
on the other, two or three trailing behind, or hang-
ing on her skirts. She turns sharply, at last, goaded
to it, and, in a shrill half-shriek : “Be quiet, blast
yer ! (that I should say such a thing) where am I to
get hokey-pokey money from?” I’d like to paint her
and the children:—and the ‘ ‘ nan-nan ” sons hurrying
past in the background. I’d call it “Nit! there’s
Mother.” Where was she to get hokey-pokey
money from ?
Then, in the face of Mother Grundy and to the
advancement of Australia—the slums—there are
miles of ’em now —and the poor of Sydney. The
interiors of brothels (where little girls keep the
door when “ Aunty ” is at the pub., or too drunk),
and Chinese dens (for European and Australian
types as well as Chinese—the variety of types and
feminine beauty would surprise you a bit, I
reckon.) The patrol of the prostitutes at night in
Elizabeth, Bathurst, College, and Hunter-streets :
splendid types, some of them, and there is plenty
of street light to allow of effect. Our civilisation
has advanced so that these are no longer shy of
light and crowds.
The drovers, the teamsters, the shearers, the
station hands, the cookies, the diggers—and the
acts and scenes of their lives—what place have
these m our galleries ? I would paint the shearing
shed as it is ; the swagman tramping through the
heat, and the swagman tramping through the
rain (paint, but not hang in Australia); the
swagmen’s camps and the selectors’ homes; the
bush-women : the drovers riding home with their
dogs and pack-horses after a long trip—on wiry,
ungroomed stock-horses and burned brown, and
ragged and dusty, and with the patient weariness
of 500 miles of dry country in their eyes. .
The selector—take a scene at random—” The Last
Tree on Our Selection.” A great gum grubbed
out and that moment fallen; the cloud of dust
from the branches : the hard, caked clay flying
from the butts of the roots ; the great tap-root
cut through; the rush of dogs into the head of
the tree after a colony of ’possums long left un-
disturbed in the hollows of the branches. The
selector standing back, leaning on his axe and
wiping his forehead. . . Or a long-ago abandoned
goldfield in the rain, and with the spirit of the
past haunting it. . . . Two old diggers work-
ing the old field over again, pegging out old claims
anew (as they would have been pegged out in
the first place had' diggers been able to see
through the ground) jabbing down shafts and
following workings with their index fingers on the
table—Gulgong, or any of these in the Roaring
Days. We have the dresses, tents, cradles, ready
to our hand in crude but faithful old water-colours
done on the spot. And wild and strange and
mixed and ” enough for anything such a
scene would look. Do you know that men wore
hats with ribbons to them, and capes, and other
outlandish things, those days in Australia ? And
well-greased ringlets and earrings were common.
And stockmen wore strapped trousers. Do you
know what strapped pants are ? We’ll rip the
side and ’tween-leg seams of a pair of pants, take
the back out altogether and put a new one in of a
different colour, that will give you some idea——
no, it’s not that ; get on ahorse, draw a chalk-line
in curves all round the spaces of contact between
you and the saddle and horse, get a woman to cub
out and sew neatly over the spaces enclosed, a
length of good saddle tweed, taking care that the
colours are something in harmony. Then you’ll
have a pair of strapped trousers, and if you’ve got
good legs and the trousers fit close as riding pants
should, very natty they’ll look. What were they
for? Why, for riding in, of course. The old
poverty-advertising ashamed patch carried to
extremes and made fashionable. Would be a
grand thing now-a-days when a well-made pair of
pants go behind prematurely, through too much
thinking.
Then, again: The Rescue Party “packing
water ” and fighting out across the plains in the
furnace-blast of the terrible drought. The “Death
of Thirst ” for the sake of Wool, Tallow, Hides
& Co.
“ The Ride for the Doctor.” Take an ordinary
plain bush girl—the brave mother-sister of the
family, riding (man’s saddle, perhaps—shabby old
apology for a riding skirt) on a rough track
through the lonely bush, under dark brooding
ridges, on the “old grey horse ’’—broken-winded,
but settled down to it and running bullock-like ;
the girl’s face drawn and haggard through anxiety
and want of sleep, and all but hopeless—whiter in
the ghostly daybreak, but brave; and in it the
fixed purpose of unconscious heroism. Thin lips
dry and set, great tears of fear, pain and weari-
ness dropping from the wide-open eyes that seem
to have lost the power of ever closing in sleep
again, fixed on the distant ridge beside which lie
the town, hope and the doctor. Thirty miles from
home. Paint that picture ! Or “The Bush
Doctor ”in the hut. . . . “The Crisis.” . . . The
parting between the bushman and the dootor after
the danger has passed.
And a picture that should be painted. Plain
girl again: “Sweet ugly woman of the past.”
The only one with a trace of refinement in a rough
bush family, too proud or too sensitive to live at
home, she studies at night-school; she is slave to
the brats, cows, “poddy calves” and selection
generally all day, and eventually she is appointed
teacher to some out-back State-school, or two half-
time schools maybe, and “ boards” in such homes
as I have sketched, where every sight, sound,
smell —every word almost—is repellent to her
nature. This girl, by a slab school-house in a
dusty patch in the eternal, God-forgotten, body-
mind-and-soul-destroying scrub, “ marking time ”
before a row of half-savage children, of whom the
youngest might be as tall as herself. Paint her in
school ; paint her at home—and the surroundings.
Paint her kneeling by her bed, or in school
after hours, arms thrown out on the desk, aching
head down, in utter loneliness, hopelessness and
weariness of life.
The angry diggers and the soldiers face to face
on Lambing Flat, and the Commissioner on the
stump speaking to the diggers after the first shot
had been fired ! . . . Way back of it all the Roll-
Up, on Ballarat. “ The Night - before Eureka ”;
the diggers kneeling under the flag. The day-
dawn tight at Eureka Stockade. Thomas Kennedy
marching from Creswick at the head of his men,
through the night-storm, flourishing his sword
and singing a wild rebel song—marching to battle
and fighting in imagination, no doubt, for
his own country, and striking, indirectly, the
grandest blow that was ever struck for Australia.
The meeting called “to strengthen the hands of
the Government,” and the doom of bad govern-
ments written in stern faces. The scene outside
the courthouse where justice was done.
And, for scenery, the lung-tilling depths and
distances of hundreds of unknown places like
McDonald's Hole and Capertree Valley. “Grim
grey coasts and sea-boards ghastly.” The
Darling River rising from far-off Queens-
land rains ; drought blazing down to a narrow
dark band of soakage between the water and
ashen banks. The yellow water rising in the
branohes of trees, and over high level < wY>r?. the
grass will sprout fresh and green—phantom or
unreal-looking sheets of water sheading through
the broiling scrubs. ... A camp on the sea
beach, jagged edges and gums softened in the
moonlight, mountain streams running down and
spreading over the sands to the sea—all the
world far and faint—faint and far, mates dream-
ing, smoking, and yarning lazily round camp-fire.
And—rest and peace for awhile.
HENRY LAWSON .
Gladstone as Orator.
Lecky’s new edition of “ Democracy and
Liberty ” is prefaced by a long introduction,
which appreciates Gladstone interestingly.
Some extracts come by way of Literature.
Lecky compares Gladstone with Burke.
“ Both of the two orators,” he says
Carried into political questions a passion seldom
round among statesmen, and also a range and
versatility of knowledge that far surpassed that of
their contemporaries, but Gladstone was incom-
parably superior to Burke in the power of moving
great masses of men, dominating in Parliamentary
debate, catching the tone and feeling of every
aud tence he addressed, and carrying an immediate
issue. On the other hand, the texture of
his intellect was commonplace. The subtleties
and ingenuities of distinction in which he was in-
exhaustibly fertile were nearly always the mere
subtleties of debate. His long and involved sen-
tences and the extreme redundancy of his lan-
guage scarcely impaired the effect of his speeches
when they were set off by his clear, musical and
powerful voice, and by his admirable skill in
enunciation and emphasis, but to the reader they
will often appear intolerably verbose.
Of course the real gist of the matter (says
Literature ), and with it the true secret of Mr.
Gladstone’s success, is to be found in those
parts of the description which concern the
unrivalled physical gifts of the orator. Ora-
tory, in fact, considered as a practical, and
distinguished from an aesthetic art—oratory
regarded purely as an instrument of imme-
diate persuasion—will always depend, to the
extent of some four-fifchs of its efficiency,
on physical qualities, its moral and intellec-
tual elements being represented in the pro-
portion of about one-tenth each. When, in-
deed, it is oratory addressed to “ the masses,”
as all Mr. Gladstone’s temporarily effec-
tive efforts were, a rigorous investigation
of it would be found to yield something like
the following analytical report:—“ Personal
magnetism of the orator—a combination of
vocal, facial, gestural, and emotional appeals
to the senses of his audience, 95 per cent,;
moral earnestness, or the semblance of it,
6 per cent.; reason—a trace.” We do not
pledge ourselves to the exact applicability of
this analysis to all Mr. Gladstone’s greatest
popular performances ; in some of them the
second and third ingredients may have been
present in somewhat higher proportions, but
what we do say is that any given speech of
his—say in Mid Lothian in 1879 —would have
been equally effective if its composition had
been precisely as above stated. Of the
external characteristics of the great orator
Mr. Lecky has made a curiously minute
study, and he gives its results with an
occasionally refreshing bluntness. No one,
as he truly says, could stand before a good
portrait of Gladstone without feeling that
“ he was in the presence of an extraordinary
man.”
Yet the greatest painter could only represent
one of the many moods of that ever changing and
most expressive countenance. Few men have had
so many faces, and the wonderful play of his
features contributed very largely to the effective-
ness of his speaking. It was a countenance
eminently fitted to express enthusiasm, pathos,
profound melancholy, commanding power, and
lofty disdain: there were moments when it could
take an expression of intense ounning, and it often
darkened into a scowl of passionate anger. In
repose it did not seem to me to be good. With its
tightly-compressed lips, and fierce, abstracted
gaze, it seemed to express not only extreme deter-
mination, but also great vindictiveness—a quality,
indeed, by no means wanting in his nature, though
it was, I think, more frequently directed against
classes than individuals. He had a wonderful eye
—a bird-of-prey eye—fierce, luminous, and rest-
less. “When he differed from you,” a great
friend and admirer of his once said to me, “there
were moments when he would give you a glance as
if he would stab you to the heart. ”
And, in illustration of these ocular peculiari-
ties of his, Mr. Lecky cites the following
somewhat comic anecdote related to him by
the late Sir Edgar Boehm :
He told me that he was once present when an
altercation between him and a Scotoh professor
took place, and that the latter started up from the
table to make an angry reply when he suddenly
stopped as if paralysed or fascinated by the glance
of Gladstone, and Boehm noticed that the pupil of
Gladstone’s eye was visibly dilating, and the eye-
lid round the whole circle of the eye drawing back
as may be seen in a bird of prey.
This peculiar property in Mr. Gladstone’s
eye can hardly, perhaps, have been of much
use to him in addressing large popular audi-
ences, but there is no doubt that-, for all
hearers who were near enough to see the
play of his features, that expressive mobility
of countenance which Mr. Lecky describes
did much to enhance the effect of his wonder-
ful voice. But even all these explanations
of Mr. Gladstone’s persuasive power still
leave a considerable margin to the unex-
plained ; and this, in our opinion, may best
be filled up by the suggestion that what
most critics treat as a defect of his oratory —
as of course it is, sesthetically considered —
its extreme verbosity, was, from the
practical point of view, a main factor in
its success. Those who have watched the
effect upon large mixed audiences of
the combination of vocal and elocutionary
charm with an inexhaustible fluency of speech
will readily appreciate this. A speaker with
a powerful and melodious voice, who never
hums and ha’s, never pauses for a word,
never leaves a sentence unfinished, but flows
on unbrokenly and perpetually like the river
before the eyes of Horace’s rustic —in omne
volubilis cevam —begins, after the lapse of
a quarter of an hour or so, to exercise a sort
of hypnotic influence over the mass of his
hearers, who end by yielding to his per-
suasions, an assent which they believe to be
intellectual, but which is in large measure a
response to a purely sensuous appeal.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Good-bye.
A star in the west
Hangs low o’er a hill, tree-crowned.
The moon in the east
Stares down at us full and round.
A curlew’s low cry
Echoes, and is answered again.
A wind in the trees
Murmurs, and dies on the plain.
Wait, stand here ;
This is the end, dear, of all.
A bat flits by ;
At our feet a cricket’s shrill call.
Blame you? Not I.
You are right, so the world says, you know.
“You love me ! ”
Repeat it again and again, perhaps it is so.
“ Believe you ! ”
O, yes, I believe, the warm touch of your hand
That trembles in mine ;
Would make me believe, but—l don’t understand
You love me?
Yet this touch of suspicion has grown
You love me !
And distrust me at once. I’m stupid, I own.
Your lips, dear,
Are mine, and your eyes ; love is sweet.
But your trust
I must have to make the perfect complete.
’Tis not given,
The world intervenes. Daren’t you face evil
breath ?
Oh, my love,
With a love for a love that is braver and stronger
than death.
Good-bye, then !
You shiver and tremble; the wind has grown
chill,
The star in the West
Has sunk now behind the low hill.
Good-bye—ah, Good-bye !
We must part. Had you trusted me, sweet
But it’s over.
Ah, Rirl! for a love that is lost, for a trust incom-
plete !
A. LEO WATTS.
Provence.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In old Provence I long to stray,
All for the old love’s sake,
For there in far-off times a lay
I sang to you at close of day
Beside the garden lake.
Sad in the summer twilight air—
O, dusk of summer eves! —
Around sat knight and lady fair;
I saw them not for you were there,
My love, beneath the leaves.
I sang to you of perished things,
Of love that sighs alone,
We seemed to hear death’s mournful wings
Sweep as he passed the trembling strings,
So deep and sad their tone.
No other love could be for me,
No other love for you ;
But you were born of high degree,
And I but lord of minstrelsy
Who lodge ’mid forest dew.
Wasted by time we fell asleep
With hearts unsatisfied ;
But love like ours must sow and reap,
And in the dark of death’s still keep
For ever may not bide.
And so beneath this lofty lime
We add unto our joy
The sweetness of that buried time
When you were young and heard the rhyme
Of wandering minstrel boy.
Tas.
JAMES HEBBLETHWAITE.
A Bulletin correspondent :
The generosity of Vie. Govt, towards the laborer is
princely A mob of men put on to a railway job in
Goulburn Valley district were paid a price that yielded
just 12s. a man for a fortnight’s hard work under a
blazing 105' sun. Despairing of making tucker with the
pick they hired a plough, and the extra earnings just
paid’ for the implement and the horse. Then the men
‘‘chucked it,” and joined the Melb. unemployed, and are
scornfully accused of not wanting work. The official
mind deduces from the unceasing clamor for work the
conclusion that the “ lower orders ” should be thankful
for work without wages.
“ C.S.” gossips about the “ Geisha ” :
The “ Chon Kino,” or “Chon Newke,” music in “ The
Geisha” reminds me of the old Japan days. “Chon
Kino,” or “ Newke,” is a sort of rhythmic swaying and
posturing of strong suggestiveness, and, considering the
general disrobing into which the performance developes,
the hearing of the air at a Sydney swell show makes any-
body in the “ know ” sit up considerably. “ Chon Kino ”
is now tabu in Japan ; but time was when the seeker after
naked truth in, say, Nagasaki could order his Chon Kino
and invite a few friends to assist —it being a mere detail
that, the number of said friends always happened to cor-
respond with that of the performing ladies. The details are
too blushful for print in any Brunker-bossed community.
Often Chou Kino was followed by dumb-show scenes of
an eye-startling character. The Geishas of 20 years ago,
by the way, were analogous to the old Greek hetairai ;
and pre-marital continence was hardly ever expected, but
for adultery the punishments were diabolical : as, for in-
stance, the burial-alive of the lady up to the chin in
quicklime and the administering of thirst-provokers and
subsequent copious draughts of water. The male culprit,
if not slain, was dealt with in a way that effectually pre-
vented repetition of the offence. On the coast of the
Inland Sea the erring pair used to be lashed, naked and
back to back, on a wooden cross weighted at the foot,
and the affair was then pitched into the sea and left,
with the heads above the surface, to float around until
the offenders perished.

The Legend.
IT was in the summer of 1778, Wash-
ington, with his army ever active,
determined to oppose the British
general, Sir Henry Clinton, who, having
evacuated Philadelphia, was retreating
through Hew Jersey on Hew York. The
forces met on 28th June at Monmouth.
La Fayette conducted the attack. The
heat was so intense that some refused to
fight, and many fell dead from exposure
to the sun’s rays. Both sides fought
desperately. As the day waned, and the
flagging spirits of La Fayette’s soldiers
were at a low ebb, a strange sight aroused
them. Marching with drum and fife
was an old man, Jeremiah Wynton, and
his son, and grandson. The old father,
with silvery flowing locks, sturdily strode
through the field. The son with ban-
daged head fifing lustily, the youth,
catching the spirit of his grandsire, rolled
out with him the soul-inspiring rattle of
the drum. Father, son, and grandson
had accompanied the troops as volunteers,
and were ever in the forefront of the
fight. The fierce light of battle was in
their eyes, they knew no fear, and many
a wild cheer arose, as on that fateful
day Yankee Doodle was heard as never
was heard before or since.

swimming carnival at Fitzroy Docks. The members of that body

their doing so in the presence of a mixed company.”

SATURDAY, APRIL 8, 1899.
Swamping the Council.
Australia is still waiting for New South
Wales before the Federal question can get
any further forward. The Federal session
has come and gone and left things as they
were. Either through treachery, or in-
capacity, or through the natural slowness of
a man who labors under much fat, or through
a desire to be painfully constitutional in all
his methods, or through deep policy, Premier
Reid again deliberately wasted his oppor-
tunity, and when the new session opens a
few days hence the Enabling Bill has to be
started again from the beginning. It was to
all intents and purposes a foregone con-
clusion that the Legislative Council would
block the measure ; it has all along been
notoriously provincialist, and the resignation
of four enthusiastic federalists— Barton,
Lees, and the two O’Connors —to contest
Assembly constituencies at the late general
election left the Upper House even more
unanimously provincialist than it was be-
fore. And when Premier Reid sent up the
Federal Enabling Bill without first filling
with reliable Federalists the thirteen or
fourteen vacancies in that much-reduced
body, he delivered the measure into the
hands of its sworn enemies. When a
mangled fragment of it came back to him
after many days—a fragment that had been
run over by the wheels of controversy, and
chewed by the teeth of prejudice, and blown
to and fro by the winds of the Old Men’s
scorn—the result was only what might have
been expected. And the Old Men, who
howled so joyfully when they sent back the
mangled fragment, have thereby given
Premier Reid a most valuable advertise-
ment. For if he likes to keep firm he must
win ; with the majority which he and
Barton can raise in the Assembly there is
no other possibility in the case. But in
addition he has a chance to pose as the
valiant fat man who fought against great
opposition and overcame a big constitutional
difficulty in advancing the cause of union,
and he gets the laurels and the limelight and
the other profits. If the Enabling Bill had
gone through in peace Reid would only have
been the light porter who humped the parcel
along. As it is he has a chance to appear as
a conqueror—which is something consider-
ably higher than a porter.
* * * *
The Council has certainly furnished its
enemy with a great opportunity. Its action
helps to consolidate the league between Reid
and Barton. Together they have such a
majority that if Reid demands the right to
appoint 20 or 30 or 40 new M.’sL.C. the
Lieutenant-Governor must either accept or
leave himself without a Ministry, for the
scraps which make up the Haynes and Lynß
and M‘Laughlin party can’t possibly form a
Government with a workable majority.
Reid can figure as the strong man, the
master of the situation, the politician who
downed the House of Tory Obstruction and
gave the law to the vice-regal figurehead, the
boss Democrat and defender of the rights of
the people—and the Council has given him
all this free advt., and put all this cheap
and easy victory within his reach. No
man can gain the credit of saving the
country unless it has first been lost, and the
Council has a stupid and futile habit of try-
ing to lose the country, whereby it furnishes
an admirable opportunity for its enemy to
plunge in and make a sensational rescue.
* * * *
But however the Federal crisis came about
there is no shadow of doubt that Premier
Reid is doing the right thing now. Every
real Democrat, and every real Federalist,
and every honest anti-Federalist will support
him in his course of action. If the matter is
put fairly the question at issue is one
to which a self-governing community can
give only one answer. Whatever his
past record may be, and whatever his
motives may be, George ReID is now
fighting for the self-governing rights of the
people—nothing more. The New South
Wales Assembly, which backs Reid, de-
mands that the vexed question of union or
disunion shall, as speedily as is con-
venient, be submitted to the people,
that the majority of those voting shall
decide, and that those who don’t take
enough interest in the matter to vote at all
shall be left out of the calculation—as they
always are, and that Federalism and Pro-
vincialism shall have a perfectly equal show.
The Council, which doesn’t support Reid,
demands that the majority shall not neces-
sarily decide. And the Council party raised
a feeble clamor that the Assembly should be
dissolved, and that the Reid Ministry should
ask a fresh mandate from the people before
proceeding to swamp the Council—that it
should ask the majority of the people (at the
election) to decide whether the majority of
the people should be allowed to decide (at
the referendum); but the suggestion is mere
burlesque. If it was proposed that the
Parliament should, on its own responsibility ,
make New South Wales a part of the Com-
monwealth, then it would be a fair thing to
demand that Parliament should be dissolved
at the last moment so as to give the people a
final opportunity to say whether they were
taking any Commonwealth or not. The
same rule would apply if the Parliament
was going to do anything else of supreme
importance on its own responsibility.
But the present case is utterly unlike
any previous one in Australia’s his-
tory, and the old rules don’t apply. Par-
liament doesn't propose to do anything,
except get home as soon as possible, and
have a rest, and get the cheque for its salary
regularly. The Federal question is left to
the people. And to dissolve Parliament
in order to give the majority a chance to
decide whether it believes or doesn’t believe
that the majority should decide is ridiculous.
All the country wants to say about the
Federation scheme will be said on Refer-
endum Day.
* * * •
So long as it is accepted that the electors
are the last court of appeal, then there is no
room for argument between the House
which wants to leave matters to the decision
of the majority and the one which doesn’t.
The only thing to do is to swamp the latter
in one act—or else give up the theory of
popular government. Even if it requires
40 new nominations to bring the result
about, that doesn’t matter. The essential
point is that this time there should be no
mistake. The provincialist party’s majority
in the Council ran as high as 15 during the
recent debates, and there are probably a
half-dozen, or perhaps a dozen, more ancients
who could be brought down in ambulances
on an emergency to vote that the present
state of things is good enough for them, and
that because they are too old to change no
one else should be allowed to change either.
If the Reid Government, in its desire to
adhere to a mouldy precedent about the
relative sizes of the two Chambers, appoints
a batch of new members which will just .
enable it to miss its mark by a hair’s-breadth
after all, the situation will be ridiculous as
well as dangerous. The country in general
doesn’t care how large the Council is, and
even if it is increased to 100 members
nobody is likely to object much. The un-
paid gentlemen roll up so reluctantly that
on all ordinary occasions, even with a
House of 100 members, it is hardly likely
that more than 30 would be present. The
country doesn’t pay its Councillors, so 100
of them would cost practically the same as ;
the present 57. Also, 100 couldn’t well be
more stupid than 57. The only real diffi-
culty seems to be about seating accommoda-
tion on the rare occasions when there is a
very large muster, and even then a few cane
chairs could be borrowed from somewhere,
or the last arrivals could camp on the floor.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Kingston Appeals to the Dry Countree.
South Australia has a general election in a few
weeks, and all the present signs are that Pre-
mier Kingston will come back safely to take
another turn of office. Kingston already has
a record of continuous office such as no other
S.A. Premier ever approached ; if he can hang
out till 16th June he will have held his billet
for six consecutive years. He is almost the
first real Premier that South Australia has
possessed ; his predecessors, who scuffled in
and out of the Ministerial jobs every few
months, and were regarded as having put up
an amazing record if they hung on for two
years, were mostly myths. Kingston’s record
up to date as a democratic statesman rests
mainly on the facts that he created the State
Land Bank, passed a measure authorising the
State to acquire large landed properties for
closer settlement, restricted colored immigra-
tion, gave the franchise to women, passed a
conciliation law, and a useful Factories
Act, and a law for the protection
of homes from creditors, enacted the graduated
land-tax and the absentee-tax which are
steadily grinding up the huge land monopolies
of S. A., and did all in his power to help the
cause of Australian unity. Also, he did his
utmost to carry a radical reform of the Upper
House, and promises to renew the struggle next
session if he and his portfolio still hang
together. He asks for a renewed lease of
power on the further grounds that he favors
elective Ministries, the referendum, State fire
insurance, a State bank of issue, and most of
the other reforms that the democratic party
aims at; that he has lived honestly up to his
programme in the past, and done more for the
good of the masses than most of his predeces-
sors put together ; and that though his finance
hasn’t been good it has been at least an im-
provement on that of his immediate predeces-
sors. And the S.A. democracy can’t do better
than take another spell of Kingston. There is
no one to put in his place who looks one-tenth
as promising as the fighting man of many mixed
nationalities who has steered S.A. through the
last few calamitous years. Considering w'hat
awful years of drought and dust they have
been, the Kingston regime has been in hard
luck all along. It has struck almost
the leanest period in S.A.’s history; and
yet, despite all the prophecies of inevitable
ruin from the party of “ Turncoat Bill ”
Cor ky and the great bore Ward, it has pro-
duced about as good results as the other crowd
did in much fatter days. And, after all, re-
sults count for a great deal more than the
gruesome prophecies of “Jeremiah ” Copley.
The Westralian Bubble.
From “ A Man who has Come Back
Easterners hear few candid reports about Westralia.
Distance has lent a spurious enchantment to the so-called
“Golden West.” But boom-times have passed, and
Westralia is suffering: a bad recovery. Yet it may seem
strange that a territory of 23 million acres, in the hands
of 170,000 people, should feel such a depression. Respon-
sible Govt, was ushered in with rich discoveries of gold,
increased population and capital, and no party could have
had better opportunities than had the Forrest crowd.
As events prove no party could have made a worse mess.
Of course no one expected the abnormal state of affairs
accompanying’ the mining development to last—except
boomster Forrest, who spent public money in a manner
reckless beyond conception. Westralia has come to the
end of its tether. With high cost of living and scarcity
of employment the candle burning at both ends does not
last long. So the West at the present time is about the
very worst place to go to.
However, Forrest will be the last man to believe that
W.A. has been grossly mismanaged, and is likely to suffer
still more keenly the reaction from an abnormal state of
things. Everlastingly he trots out the gold-yield as an
argument in support of the province’s prosperity. But
the prosperity of a country ought to he represented bv
the individual prosperity of the people, not by the special
prosperity of the few mines. The W.A. gold-yield is mag-
nificent and ought to be sufficient to redeem the country,
but the people there are under the disadvantage of having
the mining industry worked by companies nearly all
foreign. The result is the gold-yield is all exported, as
well as the bulk of the wages obtained in winning it;
Owing to the cost of living, miners will hot bring theif
wives to W.A. All very well to talk about the province’s
future from a mining standpoint. No doiibt there is A
future for the mines, but under present auspices there is
none for the province.
There is a Considerable export 6f timber to Europe and
South Africa. W.A. cart be justly proud 6f its timber re-
sources, but the timber industry, like the mining in-
dustry, is controlled by foreign capital. With two or
three exceptions, the profits all go to London. The
timber is shipped in the log, and, beyond the cost of falling
and handling, the province reaps little benefit. Hundreds
of acres of the finest tree-country are devastated yearly,
but a year’s revenue from the whole industry is only about
equal to the value of one good boat-load of jari ah. As
to the agricultural industry, no one seems to know much
about it. Owing to the fact that nearly all the food sup-
plies have to be imported some attention has been paid
to farming. But it is highly questionable whether there
is any land with adequate depth of soil; even the best
wheat-land needs manuring. After all it is not a great
task to feed 170,000 people, and as for agriculture it can
be carried on more profitably in the east and in Maori-
land. The pastoral industry is run by native labor, and
is so remote that its influence in the labor-market is’ not
felt. The question has been often asked, who are the
pastoralists ? The butchers’ ring. The bulk of the meat
consumed is imported by the butchers’ ritig—a political
combination—which is protected to the extent that it
costs from 3s, to 4s. to land a sheep in W.A;; arid £4 td
land a bullock. Like everything feise the nlcht-trade is
controlled by a few. Let Atiy ohe else’try’tb chip in arid
see how long he will last;
Putting aside the mining industry— and the British
capitalist is having a well-earned rest on the money he
has already sunk—it in ay be asked what are the legiti-
mate Outlets for Capital V Perth has been overbuilt, but for
t’othersiders who have had to pay the gropers exorbitant
rents, or get what Forrest calls a stake in the country—
a bit of sand and a humpy—the overbuilding is not an
unmixed evil, for it must materially reduce the cost of
living. Landlords have been getting 30 per cent, on
their outlay, but as there were over 600 houses empty in
Perth the other day, they will have to be content with
much less. It is certainly not an agreeable prospect to a
class of people whose daily prayer is “ God bless John
Forrest.” As for the Premier, he has been so long in
power that he has really come to believe he is there by
divine right.
The Premier has really to thank the Electoral Act
for his long-continued reign. Ostensibly the W.A.
electoral qualification is 12 months residence in the pro-
vince, but in reality it takes on the average three years
to get on the roll. After living a year in Westralia, the
would-be elector must reside six months in the one elec-
torate before he can start to apply. The franchise may
be wide enough, but there are so many ways for dis-
qualification, especially in an unsettled' country, that
three years is the average period for qualification for a
voice in Parliamentary representation. There is wide
but impotent dissatisfaction with Forrest and his policy
culminating in the Coolgardie water-scheme with its
four-million debt strangling a mere handful of people.
But the Sandgroper’s prayer is ever “ God bless Sir John.”
It is a peculiar country, but there is a day of reckoning
coming. Someone will have to pay the piper. If that
somebody is the old resident he won’t care to stand it
all, and the t’othersider won’t stand taxation for ever with-
out representation.

Holman’s History.
Labor-member Holman turned up the other
day at Bulli (N.S. W.) to misinform the people
about the proposed Federal Constitution, and
to garble a lot of facts in the provincialist in-
terest. The following is a most interesting
hunk of the speech of that fluent and in-
accurate young man, quoted from the report of
the admiring Telegraph :
There were two distinctly opposing kinds of Federation,
the one provided that in the federated community the
majority should rule, and there was no more reason why
the majority should not rule in a Federation than
in a single State. Switzerland, Canada, and Ger-
many were prosperous examples of Federation. Under
the specious guise of talk about a majority of
States, a minority of the people might impose their
will on a vast majority. These two kinds of Federation
were as utterly distinct from one another as the Poles
were asunder. The one kind exalted a nation ; the
second crushed and degraded it. The second kind of
Federation had only been tried once, and that was in t.he
United States of America, and it was the most gigantic
failure associated with the American name, America had
a record of a century and 16 years of failure. The men
who drew up the Draft Constitution for tlie Australian
Commonwealth had shut their eyes wilfully to all the
successful examples, and had blindly copied the gigantic
failure of America.
Pity the Labor*party’s gushful orator doesn’t
learn as well as talk, and get to know his book
a little better. For the proposed Australian
Federal Constitution is to a great extent a copy
of that of Switzerland, which Holman holds up
as a shining example, and presents only a casual
resemblance to that of the United States,
which he disapproves. The matters in which
the proposed Australian Constitution departs
from strict majority rule are the equal repre-
sentation of the States in the Senate, and the
necessity for a majority of States as well as a
majority of people to concur in any change of
Constitution. And this compares with the
system in some other Federations as follows
United States : States have all equal representation in
the Senate. Constitution can be altered with the assent of
two-thirds of the House of Representatives, two-thirds of
the Senate, and three-fourths of the States.
Germany : States have unequal representation. Amend-
ments of the Constitution require an absolute majority of
the Reichstag, and the assent of two-thirds of the
Bundesrath.
Switzerland : States have equal representation in the
Senate. Constitution can be altered by vote of a bare
majority of the Legislature, a majority of the people
voting, and a majority of the States. Also a majority of
the people voting and of the States can alter it without
reference to the Legislature.
Australia (proposed): States to have equal representa-
tion in the Senate. Constitution to be alterable by the
assent of a majority of the Legislature, a majority of the
people voting, and a majority of the States.
The makers of the Australian Constitution
actually made a close copy of the system which
Holman barracks for and which Holman’s
party holds up as the model constitution of the
earth ; and carefully avoided the worst evils of
the American system which Holman objects to.
And Holman, it would appear, deliberately
turns the position upside down, and curses the
Convention for doing the very thing it avoided
and for not doing what it did, and is a ridiculous
blind leader of the eyeless multitude in general.
“The Bulletin’s” Casual Prophet.
Methusalem writes in a spirit of prophecy
trimmed with beer :
Everything was perfectly lovely. The important pro-
vince of Two Ales had at last definitely decided to bestow
the boon of Federation upon the rest of Mixedalia. The
Two-Aleians had made sporadic attempts to do this be-
fore, but on each occasion some far-seeing citizen had
discovered —fortunately in time—that Federation meant
annihilation and disintegration to the State of Two Ales,
and the idea was dropped enthusiastically. The obstacle
all along had been a very simple one. When the sugges-
tion was first formulated, the Two-Aleians had been
under the natural impression that Federation merely
meant the recognition of Two Ales as the leading dog ot
the pack. Therefore Two Ales came forward and joy-
fully proposed that everybody should federate with her.
Then it transpired that the insignificant other provinces
entertained the preposterous notion that Federation
meant “ pool the meat and every dog gets a bite.” And

GAMMA.


when the Two-Aleians realised that it was actually pro-
posed to put them on an equality with everybody else,
the unanimity with which they were not playing in any-
body's yard was touching to behold. After that, at peri-
odical intervals, Two Ales arose, with a charming air of
novelty, and said that really Mixedalia ought to, federate,
you know. And then her neighbors, who didn’t particu-
larly want to unite, and would probably never have
thought of it if it hadn’t been for Two Ales, agreed
wearily, and proceeded to help at constructing Bills. But
the Bills, like all bills, were never satisfactory, for those
stupid States would persist in trying to divide things all
round, instead of handing over the management of every-
thing to Two Ales. And so the latter always retired with
the grace of a hippopotamus emerging from a mud bath.
However, in the year 1916, and after 17 Bills had been
successfully squelched by the frenzied advocates of
Federation in Two Ales, the -matter was beautifully
settled, and everything, at last, was lovely. The other
States had knuckled under, and the last Bill formally re-
cognised Two Ales as second only to Paradise. The
Federal capital was to be situated on the tropical islet of
Pinchgut, in the centre of Kidney Habor. Two Ales was
to have sole control of all the rivers of Mixedalia. The
total revenue was to be paid in to Two Ales to spend exactly
as she pleased. Nohody else was to get anything, and
Two Ales was to get everything. And, at this point, the
Two-Aleians expressed a modified amount of satisfaction
with the concessions, and decided that Federation might
go ahead. And the 18th Bill was about to be passed with
acclamation when a patriotic citizen named Squibbs dis-
covered that it would be suicidal for Two Ales to
Federate. He pointed out that everybody from the sur-
rounding provinces would be obliged to flock into Two
Ales, and that no provision had been made for making
them pay for the extra amount of air which would there-
fore be breathed by the strangers. Mass meetings were
at once held to protest against the “sacrifice” of the
Two Ales air, a petition was drawn up against the Bill,
and Mr. Squibbs was presented with a purse of sovereigns
for his patriotic action in saving Two Ales from the
dangers of Federation. Then the Bill was hung up in
order to have another (the 1275th) amendment added.
Victorian Charity.
Significant item from Melbourne Age :
On 21st January last Constable Cahill, of Royal Park,
arrested a young woman named Annie Smith, whom he
found camping in a culvert in Royal Park, and he charged
her with vagrancy. The girl told the magistrates next
morning that she came from Geelong, and had no friends.
Mrs. Goldspink, of the Abbotsford Convent, offered to
take care of her, and Smith, who expressed her willing-
ness to go with Mrs. Goldspink, was discharged. On
Wednesday evening the police were notified that Smith
and another woman had escaped from the convent by
scaling the 12ft. wall which surrounds the buildings and
dropping to the ground on the other side. The Carlton
police kept a lookout for the women, and on Thursday
evening Constable Cahill found the escapers in the cul-
vert in which he had first arrested Smith, and which is in
a deep gully at the rear of the Zoological Gardens. The
women had just then arrived, and were making prepara-
tions for a stay under the shelter of the bridge. They
were both lodged in the Carlton lockup on charges of
vagrancy. At the local court on Saturday Smith stated
that she left the convent “because she didn’t like it.”
The other accused, a middle-aged woman named Annie
Hayden, stated that she had “ been there for nine
months, and she didn’t like it either ; but she had been
unable to get away before.” The Bench sent each accused
to gaol for 12 weeks.
The Bulletin invites the attention of a logical
Government to these two cases. The charitable
institution at Abbotsford is the place where the
big laundry is. Said laundry, as a “ charitable
institution,” is exempted from the operation
of the Factories Act. There is no minimum-
wage there—in fact, there is no wage at all.
The inmates can be worked at the unceasing
grind of the wash-tub and the mangle for
practically any hours that the management
pleases. And it is, to all intents and purposes,
a prison—the only way out is apparently by
scaling a high wall at night, and when one
of the prisoners escapes the police proceed
to run her down and run her in. Also, it seems
to be an unregulated prison where the confinee’s
sentence has no definite end. Annie Smith
was arrested in the first place because she had
no money—a hideous crime that might surely
be expiated by, say, a month’s confinement.
She did three months’ confinement (and laun-
dry-work) in the Abbotsford institution, and
then, probably thinking it was a case of im-
prisonment for life, she bolted. The police
were informed by her kind bosses, and she was
run in for vagrancy—as she got no wages slie
was naturally just as much of a vagrant as she
had been before. The other woman (also,
apparently, a vagrant) had done nine months of
her indefinite sentence, and as they both got 12
weeks more, that makes six months in one case
and a year in the other. Six months seems a
long term for having no money, and yet if
Annie Smith had remained in the charitable
laundry it seems possible that her sentence
there would have run to years. The other
woman had already done nine months there,
and was apparently not nearly through.
The question is: Under what possible law
are women, for the smallest offences, sen-
tenced to indefinite confinement in anybody’s
wash-house, with no stipulation as to the
kind of treatment meted out to them while
there, and no wages, and with the understand-
ing that they will get a severe sentence for
breaking out, no matter how long a term they
may have served before their escape ? The
arrangement may be charity, but it looks very
like chattel slavery. Will some politician
move for an official inquiry into this and similar
institutions, and for a return showing the
offences for which prisoners were sent there,
and how long each one has been there ? In the
case of a woman who was incapable of scaling
a 12ft. wall, it seems quite possible that the
sentence would spread out like that of the
legendary Prisoner of Chillon. And his sen-
tence wasn’t aggravated by laundry-work.

An Advt. for Digger Dick.
A little crop of crimes, mostly small ones, and
offences and misdoings has arisen in Maoriland
as the immediate result of the Old-Age Pension
scheme, and they are being worked for all they
are worth by the Stagnant Party. The first
death among Digger Dick’s pensioners arose
through the butchery of an old man with an
axe, and another pensioner is in gaol charged
with the offence on his own confession. There
is no positive evidence that the second old
man’s mind was deranged by the sudden receipt
of bis <£lB a year, and that be was driven to
murder by Digger Dick’s pension—but it cer-
tainly seems that he was. There has been a
species of insurrection in Hawera Old Men’s
Home, where the pensioners put on airs, and
stood the institution on its head, and were
finally expelled ; and the first-pensioner at the
Costlev Home (Auckland) got out and had such
a drunk as that cheerless receptacle for the
aged hasn’t seen or heard of for a long, long
time. The Tory press is grieved in its in-
most soul about the immorality of granting
pensions to the aged because of these
few drunks and episodes—which don’t amount
to much after all among 5000 or 6000 pensioners
—and reckons that the money should be stopped
to save the aged from being Lost. Yet, after
all, the Tory organs needn’t be so badly troubled.
Every sudden blessing drives somebody or other
mad until the public gets used to it. When
the American and West Indian slaves were
released, respectable niggers, who might other-
wise have lived to a useful old age, went mad
with excitement and suicided, and fossils of
great tonnage said that their blood was on the
heads of the abolitionists, and that their deaths
showed conclusively that slavery should be re-
established for the nigger’s own protection.
But it wasn’t re-established, and the spasm
passed, and now no nigger is so astonished to
find himself free that he finds it needful
to go mad over it. Also, there have been
cases when men became dangerous lunatics
through the joy of getting out of gaol
after 20 years of confinement, but that doesn’t
prove that everybody should be in gaol.
There have even been instances when men got
their souls saved at religious meetings, and
were so pleased at the thought that they were
not going to hell that they became dotty and
slew the whole family with an axe—also the
dog. This may show that everybody should go
to hell—but it isn’t generally considered in
that light. What strikes The Bulletin most
about the little crop of offences which has
attended the introduction of the pension
system in M.L. is this : Maoriland is without
doubt the most prosperous of the Seven Pro-
vinces. And yet even in Maoriland there were
people so miserable that the joy and astonish-
ment of having 7s. 6d. a week for certain made
some of them get drunk, and some of them
start a small insurrection in their benevolent
asylum, and drove one so completely off his
aged dot that the result was homicide. When
these venerables get used to their bloated
affluence they will simmer down and become
ordinary members of society again the
average human isn’t usually driven mad
by 7s. 6d. a week. But the depth of woe
from which some of them were rescued
may be judged by their wild excitement at
even the small measure of relief which Seddon’s
scheme affords them. And the Maoriland Tory
party wants to keep these old wrecks in their
former state—the state wherein 7s. 6d. a week
is so unusual that the sight of it endangers
their sanity. The Bulletin reckons that that
aged individual who went crazy after getting
his first instalment and axed his mate is about
the best advt. Seddon’s policy has had up to
date.
A Double Event.
The Anti-Federal party’s demand that N.S.W.
shall not federate unless Queensland comes in,
even if Victoria, Tasmania, S.A. and Westralia
are all willing, combined with its loud assur-
ance that Queensland doesn’t care a straw for
Federation, is a very interesting double event.
The situation, if carefully reasoned out, goes
into these four propositions : (1) Queens-
land, according to the Anti-Federal crowd,
doesn’t really want to federate now. (2) If
the other provinces federate, however, it is
difficult for it to stand out, as it wants the free
run of the southern market for its sugar ;
therefore, the shortest way to bring Queensland
in is to explain that the other provinces are
going to federate, anyhow. (3) If the other
provinces federate, Queensland wants to be in
at the beginning so as to have a voice in the
fixing of the capital, and to get it as near its
border as possible; therefore, again, the
shortest way to bring Queensland in is to ex-
plain that the other provinces are going to
federate in any case, and if it wants to have
the capital near its border it must join now.
(4) Therefore, the anti-federal party, fearing
that Queensland will join now, and thus spoil
a useful anti-Bill argument, proposed to assure
that province that there is no reason why it
should come in now—no reason at all; that
N.S.W. will wait for it, no matter how long,
and the other provinces will wait for N.S.W.,
and, therefore, all Australia will wait till
Queensland is ready; that it won’t gain any-
thing by federating now, nor lose anything by
indefinite delay, and that it may as well delay
as not. And, having first stipulated that the
standing-out of Queensland will wreck the
whole movement, it sends its John Norton
and others to froth at Brisbane and persuade
Queensland to stand out—and wreck the move-
ment. Also, having stipulated that it is im-
possible for N.S.W. to accept the Federal
scheme if Queensland doesn’t do likewise, the
Sydney anti-Federalists and their daily organ
yell incessantly that there isn’t a chance of
Queensland accepting—not the smallest possible
ghost of a chance; therefore, for the N.S.W.
electors to vote “Yes” is sheer waste of time
and energy, and they may as well save them-
selves the trouble —in short, that the Queens-
land proviso has damned the Bill utterly. The
proposed stipulation that N.S.W. can’t federate
without Queensland gives theanti-Billiouscrowd
two chances; it may persuade Queensland to
reject the Bill on the ground that there is no
hurry—Australia will wait till Queensland is
ready. And it may persuade N.S.W. to let it
go by default on -the ground that Queensland is
sure to reject it, anyhow.
A Woman in a Bath-tub.
A woman is stirred to remonstrate on a subject
of which she presumably knows something:—
Dear Bulletin.— About Dr. Brigham Brown’s remarks
on the unwashedness of women, I want to say that,
though I am a mere woman, I have a great regard for
cleanliness. I may have a natural dislike for the use of
water, but you must remember that, in common with
many other women, I come of a line of “ tubbers,” and I
have an acquired or inherited liking for water. All the
women I know are exquisitely clean. Not clean in the
ordinary way of a man who stands under a shower for one
rninule and hurriedly gets out and scrubs himself with a
towel. My women are cleah in a more elaborate and
dainty fashion ; they have marvellous loofahs, brushes,
and great yellow sponges. Most women of my acquaint-
ance take Turkish baths regularly, and swimming baths
also ; one woman whom I know rises at 5 every morning
and goes to a swimming bath with her husband.
In passing, let me say that Bananaland girls don’t bathe
half as much as Victorian girls, nor do they wash them-
selves as often. Another fact: all the women I know
take cold baths during the winter, whereas several of my
men-friends either go without or take warm baths. Also
I think the Australienne in her heart very irreligious,
though she may assume a virtue she has not. No woman
ever tounded a religion. Man goes and gets religions re-
vealed unto him, and then he shows em to woman, and,
if she follow 'em, nine times out of ten it is for the sake
of the man, not the religion. On another of Dr.
Brown’s assertions : Most women don’t regard them-
selves as the female of a vegetable John. Even in a
healthy woman, sexuality isn’t always on top. John
may be a man, but we don’t regard him as
such. To us he is simply a purveyor of vegetables.
We don’t regard him as our animal equal. He’s not a
very fetching animal anyway. Also, which is best,
licentiousness or temperance in all things? I think most
women are temperamentally more moral than men. I ve
known women of all classes and kinds, and I’ve met two
exceptions. Messadina was a fool to these. It s just
this way; if moral women were not in a majority the
bottom would drop out of our civilization. I don t say it
would beanyloss. Ourmoralitymay beacquired or innate,
but it’s all there. Of course, if Brigham Brown reasons
from a narrow Australian experience he may make out
some kind of a case in the matter of the vegetable John.
In Australia some women do marry John. And very few
men marry Miss John. The sufficient reason is that Miss
John isn’t here. But go among the islands, or to the
Southern States of America, or to Asia, and you will find
six men more or less married to colored women for one
woman more or less wedded to colored men. If Brown,
M.D., will get out of his local rut and look at the experi-
ence of countries where man has a chance to show his
craving after the brown and black and yellow daughters
of the land, he will perhaps learn something that will
surprise him. Even in a healthy woman, as I said,
sexuality isn’t always on top. Most of the sex-problem
novels, and practically all the sex-problem plays, are
written by men. Nearly every book in this world which
verges on the indecent is written by a man. Ninety per
cent, of the recorded offences against decency are done
by men. If you ask any doctor in charge of a large
hospital as to the condition of his patients on arrival, he
will tell you that five-sixths of the worse offenders against
cleanliness are men. Man has been insisting for a long
time on woman going under the shower —because he
wants to teach her to wash in the same perfunctory
fashion as he does himself. —Yiva Decadent.
The Suffield Case.
Following is a statement of the amounts col-
lected by The Bulletin on behalf of Thomas
Suffield, sentenced by Judge Windeyer to
ten years’ penal servitude on Bth April, 1891,
after a mockery of a trial, and in circumstances
which caused the greatest doubt of his guilt,
on the charge of assaulting a woman, he
being convicted by the uncorroborated and self-
contradicted evidence of one witness —the
woman herself.

no success.

The dirge of Brisbane Telegraph :
Yesterday- a first meeting was held of persons who in-
tend to form a league for the defence of Southern Queens-
land against the threatened invasion and ruin of all our
industries and commerce by the scheme of Federation
proposed by Melbourne politicians. If numbers and
warmth of feeling foretell events, this league will open
the eyes of persons who are trying secretly- and corruptly
to hand this country over to her destroyers. The next
step, it was resolved, should be to summon by circular a
large number of manufacturers, merchants, mechanics,
and others to a meeting at which the movement will be
thoroughly organised, with a view- of distributing infor-
mation through all the chief centres of population, so that
the electors may understand how great is the danger
hanging over them, arid that the referendum may meet
its deserved fate. The names handed in were those of
our best-known public men, and the publication of some
of them, which will take place in a few days, may cause
a little surprise. Plenty of money was at once promised,
so that the sinews of war are ready. A paid secretary
will bp appointed at once, and other paid officers, if
necessary, to make a thorough canvass and otherwise
carry out the purposes of the league.
If Queensland’s “ best known public men” are
in the anti-Billious camp, that camp will smell
like the Khalifa’s city of Omdurman before it
was disinfected. Queensland’s best-known
public men were mostly among the overdrafters
at the old Q.N. Bank, and the fragrance of
that institution can still be noticed as far
south as Launceston when a north -wind blows.
At a Yic. Court, the other day, a young fellow
pleaded guilty to a charge of “ improper rela-
tions ” with a damsel somewhat under the age
(16 years) of consent. The amorous pair were
anxious to stop the case by getting married, but
the girl’s mother refused her consent, and the
offender was duly committed for trial at next
Criminal Sessions. The protesting parent, in
cross-examination, admitted that she was her-
self living with a colored person (Argus re-
port), so it may be that she has conscientious
objections to any lawful union of the sexes.
Which suggests the necessity for a Court of
Matrimonial Advice to settle such questions as
arise in this case. Then the willing “victim”
could have the Court’s authority to get married
contrary to the wish of a mother who sets her
an example of free love with a blackman ?
During the first eight months of 1898 three
post-offices in the Austrian province of Dal-
matia (only three post-offices out of dozens)
cashed money-orders from Maoriland to the
amount of £ll,OOO. The money came from
Austrian gum-diggers. Not one in ten of the
Austrians was a permanent settler ; their ob-
ject in almost every case was to work their
hardest at gum-digging, to live on 4s. a week,
and to take their money back to their beloved
Dalmatia. On a low estimate they took
£lOO,OOO a year out of M.L. ; they paid prac-
tically no taxes ; they furnished the smallest
possible amount of employment; they made no
improvements in the country ; they were aliens
who couldn’t be called on to fight for it in case
of need ; and they deprived many Maorilanders
of work. As a rule, Australasia has a welcome
for every clean white man who means to settle
in the country, but the mere transient exploiter
calls for rigid exclusion.
When does the N.S.W. Government propose
to wake up and do something decisive to the
registry-office swindle —which is, at present,
about the most heartless fraud in the province 1
A multitude of the billets which the registry-
end puts on his list, and which he pretends
are in his hands exclusively, are simply copied
out of the paper of that morning, or yesterday,
or the previous week. Said fiend charges as
much as he thinks the hungry applicant can
pay (ss. is generally about the lowest figure),
and then sends him to find the place is
long since filled, or that he is only one
among perhaps 100 applicants. Also, he
doesn’t in the least mind sending half-a-dozen
applicants to look after one billet and charging
them ss. or more apiece. Sometimes his alleged
job is absolutely bogus, and never even existed
in the daily paper. If he really finds a job for
an applicant lie takes the largest part or the
whole of the first week’s wage for his fee.
Occasionally he is in league with the employer,
and they divide the fee, and t he employer regu-
larly sacks the new client at the end of a fort-
night, so that he and the registry-office ruffian'
can have another fee to divide. What the case
calls for is a rigid licensing system —any
unlicensed person in the trade to be fined
a large amount; moderate fees to be fixed by
law ; the registry-man to be bound to find his
client a billet inside a month or return the
money ; and any registry-man found trading in
bogus billets, or in billets that haven’t been
specifically put in his hands, to be gaoled or
fined copiously and lose his license. The Bul-
letin implores the members of N.S.W. Par-
liament not to all bring in the required Bill
at once, lest it lead to confusion. One Bill
is all that is wanted.
;
Though the N.S.W. Council professed to be so
much exercised about the smallness of the vote
at last Federal referendum, not one M.L.C.
proposed to remedy the evil by making voting
compulsory at next referendum. Seeing that
the great alleged grievance was that 138,000
votes were not a proper test of the popular
feeling, compulsory voting was the best way
to get a proper test —if the Council really
wanted it. And, as theHEYDON crowd declares
that the 160,000 who didn’t vote were against
the Bill, the shortest way to prove the push’s
sincerity—if it has any—is to make these
160,000 roll up next time, and thus squash the
Bill for good and all. Pity the N.S. W. Assem-
bly didn’t oiler to accept the Council’s 80,000
minimum with the added proviso that every
elector who was in the country, and at liberty,
and in good enough health to reach the poll,
must vote, or be fined 40s.
Carruthers (he’s cocky !) went one better than
the unrisen Lazaruses of N.S. W. Leg. Council
in the Federal conference. “ Put-off the poll
for three months,” said the men in the shrouds,
“ so that everybody may have a chance to con-
sider and vote.” “By all means,” said Cocky,
“ and we’ll let the poll extend over two or three
days, so that everybody will have a chance to
vote—and there’ll be no distance-disfranchise-
ment of the out-back selector with 40 miles to
ride, while the Sydney-anti rolls up to record
his protest next-floor to home.” And, as that
wasn’t in the least what Lazarus wanted—
Lazarus wept.
Melbourne is a beautifully civilised city in
some respects. For instance, there is no hos-
pital set apart for specially-infectious diseases,
such as diphtheria. The ordinary hospitals
object to take them, as it would spread infec-
tion among the other patients. Private hospi-
tals are not allowed to take them, and hotels
and boarding-houses cast them out lest the
fear of infection should drive away other
boarders and destroy the business. A lady
residing with her child in a Fitzroy board-
ing-house was nearly being ejected into the
street recently because the child was taken
ill with what appeared to be diphtheria,
and for a while it looked as if they would have
to get themselves run in for vagrancy, for there
was no demand for the small patient anywhere.
At last the proprietress of a private hospital
admitted them in a perfectly illegal fashion,
and as the ease proved not to be diphtheria,
after all, the Board of Health has decided not
to prosecute the hospital woman and have her
severely punished. Which is really very kind
of the Board.
Despite all dicta to the contrary, Queensland,
on the whole, seems to be decidedly in favor of
Federation. Brisbane, the octopus, is certainly
not enthusiastic. Jealousy concerning her com-
mercial interests is at the bottom of her apathy.
Neither is Toowoomba ardently federal in her
views—out of fear of the competition of N.S.W.
cabbages and potatoes. Rockhampton objects
strongly to the 123rd clause of the Constitution
—but the rest of Centralia doesn’t care a hang
about that. The North (Charters Towers in par-
ticular) is decidedly in favor of saying good-bye
to provincialism, while the sugar-districts (de-
spite the black-labor question) want to unite
with the provinces that will supply them with
a market. Southern Queensland, with the ex-
ception of two cities above-mentioned, is feder-
ally inclined, but lazy in the matter. When
the question is seriously taken up by Banana-
land Parliament there’ll be such a confounding
of the old party lines and platforms as Queens-
land has not experienced since the coalition.
Dear Bulletin. —In the matter of the Asiatic
alien, the white working-man is the true delin-
quent. He abuses Asiatics with his tongue,
but at same time upholds them with both hands.
Arrived at this conclusion while swagging it
on W.A. goldfields. At Peak Hill, found all
the carrying done by white men’s teams, but
heard it rumored that the Afghans had threat-
ened the storekeepers that if they did not give
them (the Afghans) six months’ loading, they
would open a store and sell at cost price ; and
I found the generality of the miners highly
pleased at the prospect of thus saving a shilling
or tw r o per week and ruining the white store-
keeper, teamsters, and all dependent trades.
At Lake Way it sticks out again. One store-
keeper employed camel teams ; another did not
believe in giving the Afghan a footing, and
employed the white’s teams. Storekeeper No.
1 was able to sell a penny or two cheaper than
No. 2. So the miners bought from former,
forcing No. 2 to employ the camel trains or
“go broke.” Yet these very miners will skite
about the great wrong Govt, is doing in allow-
ing the Asiatic into the country ; and are ready
to call every sort of dog to the man who put a
starving Chow on for a week’s work.— Swaggie.
