Sydney Lyceum was re-opened by Charles
Holloway last Saturday—at least, it was presum-
ably the same old Lyceum, though, barring the
name and address, everything was different. The
house has been boiled and starched, and ironed
and filtered, also painted, papered, varnished, re-
carpeted and disinfected, and the germs have been
picked up separately, and placed on a flat board,
and then smashed with a club in the presence of
reliable witnesses. The accumulated grease-mark
of ages has gone from the gallery-wall, and the
accursed pea-nut man has been driven from the
front door. Also, the curtain with the picture of
the thin groveller urging the girl in the short
frock to buy somebody’s bile boots has vanished,
and in general the Lyceum is now the newest and
freshest theatre in Sydney, and all clean people are
cordially invited to attend.
* * • •
As for the new drama, the Lord only
knows what it is about. It happened in the year
6s. Bd., and the scene is laid in the country where
long beers roll uphill. The “ Guardian Angel ”
herself is a ridiculous brown woman who says, at
least 40 times, that the English are the guardians
of freedom, and that before the noble English flag all
wrong and injustice back down in shame and hide
their diminished head in the ash-hole. She is her-
self the slave of an Englishman, and her boy has
been stolen by an Englishman, and the same in-
dividual, aided by another Englishman, com-
mitted a horrid murder before the story opened,
and got her beloved brown husband gaoled for
life by swearing that he did it. And this astound-
ing brown lady shakes a carving-knife in the air
and says that the English are just and noble, &c.,
and does other ridiculous elocution in the year 6s.
8d: and the land of long beers rolling uphill.
Later on, she is kidnapped on an English ship,
where the English captain and his British accom-
plice flog her brown son to death, and the English
crew looks on and refuses to interfere. And
when the brown son is quite dead, the distracted
mother covers him with the English flag, and says
that the English have noble hearts and will see all
her wrongs righted ; only just then they find that
the English captain and his English accomplice
have scuttled the ship and left them to drown.
There are more shouts about the justice and
humanity and glory of England in the “ Guardian
Angel ” than in any other six dramas of recent
date—mostly on the part of the demented brown
woman. An Englishman attempts to steal the pro-
perty later on, and tries to burn out the hero’s
eyes, and kidnaps the heroine, and stuns a small girl
in a white frock, and proceeds to drive a reaping-
machine over her with a view to cutting off her
feet, or her head, or something. And the awful
brown character lets her hair down to the bitter
end, and goes on something dreadful about how
the noble English heart can always be relied upon.
* * * *
The reaping machine seems to fix the
date of the drama in quite recent times, and the
coffee-colored slave appears to throw it back into
last century. Also, an allusion to Surinam sug-
gests that the scene starts in the Dutch West
Indies, only the English magistrate and the vast
number of British sailors, and the constant cheer-
ing for the Hinglish Flag upset this idea again.
Anyhow it is a very gorgeous and picturesque drama
with leagues of red sunset and blue sea in it, and a
most realistic steamer which picks up the principal
demented characters after the English captain has
murdered the small boy and scuttled the ship.
The brown woman is nearly lost, but she comes up
with her mouth full of sea-weed and tries to say
something about the humanity of the English
soul. William Gourlay—as a boneless China-
man of remarkable dancing capacities—is the
most unusual person in the co. ; and when
this Mongol dances he deserves to rank
alongside Lauri’s Chinaman Wun, of the
“ Geisha.” The brown lady of Miss Ida Gresham
is excellent most of the time; Amy Gourlay’s
g’rl Sarah is good all the time; and Ada Guil-
rd is the nice, ordinary heroine who always
means nice, clean clothes and looks as if her head
was washed regularly. Inman’s hero is exactly
like all Inman’s heroes. As for Stanford, he is
caked so thickly with villainy that the outer crust
would have to be scraped off with a knife to find
out what sort of person is underneath.
#* * *
A large crowd gathered at Sydney Royal
on Monday night to see “Madame Sans-Gene”
and the new Brough comedy co. It was a rather
silent crowd for its size, and applauded little, and
laughed seldom—in fact, it seemed to take the
tale of the violent waslier-duchess in a decidedly
serious spirit. Its principal smile occurred four
times. Two of the occasions were when the wash-
duchess made emphatic allusions to ladies’ under-
clothing—more particularly on the occasion when
she declared that Napoleon’s sisters hadn’t any.
Presumably, therefore, they went about attired
only in a frock and a pair of boots. Also, it looked
really interested when the duchess was pulled
up short just as she was about to tell a story about
how a sweep had stolen her diamonds, and she
had the wretched man of soot undressed till he was
a sweep in the altogether with a view to shaking
the gems out of his clothes —only this tale was
sawn off short, and the interest was lost. And
when the Emperor smacked the duchess two
severe, friendly smacks on a great area of fashion-
able bare back, and made her wriggle with the
suddenness of it, the crowd showed decided traces
of animation—only it got a view of the lady’s
shoulders a moment later, and was disappointed to
find no red marks thereon. It was evidently looking
for a plot of some kind, and there was none—or very
little. “ Madame Sans-G6ne ” is mainly a series
of scattered incidents in the life of a wash-lady
who attains to high rank, and wears her pink
petticoat in the drawing-roam, and gets into and
out of some expensive clothes in public, and
stands on the tail of her stately robe, and wears
her hat over her ear, and will persist in making
loud and furious allusions to the scarcity of linen
from which the Emperor’s sisters suffered in their
younger days.
m m m m
Brough, as the Emperor, can hardly be
taken seriously. He was so distinctly himself that
this paper kept thinking of him as Napoleon
Broughaparte. His face doesn’t make up for the
countenance of the pallid Conqueror with the
Forelock, and his voice wants to be painted and
decorated to make it sound like that of a man
accustomed to command. He seemed peevish and
sour rather than strong —a hot, cross Emperor who
knocked small articles about in his anger, where a
more imposing man would have attacked the big
furniture. The company consists mostly of new
people, and some of these new people were pain-
fully inaudible in the big theatre. One of them
might almost as well have left his remarks behind
him in writing and gone fishing. Judging by
a first inspection amid their new surroundings,
W. H. Rotherham, who played Fouehe, made the
best impression—a long, lank retiring impression,
but a good one all the same ; and Joseph Carne’s
Lefebvre had also sundry good points. Mrs.
Brough was a tempestuous washer - duchess,
and her stormy allusions to underclothing were
relieved a good deal by the manner of their
delivery yet even she fitted her part none
too well. At time of writing The Bulletin
hadn’t made up its alleged mind as to the prospects
of “Madame Sans-Gene,” but it had severe
doubts concealed under its shirt-front. Perhaps
if the miscellaneous ladies of Broughaparte’s
court were a little more dazzling in their beauty
the play would have a better show. And there
was still a lingering fragrance of bright new paint
about the Royal on Monday night. Bright new
paint gets on this paper’s digestion.
*# * *
G. Rignold’s “ Othello ” has taken a strong
hold upon Sydney. It is, without question, the
greatest Shakespearian production for many years
past, and it has raised Rignoid many miles in the
estimation of a community which knew him
chiefly as a hero of much “ melodrammer ” and as
the monarch who clattered in copious hardware
through the rather meaningless pageant of
“Henry Y.” That monarch is mostly a series
of processions and detached episodes, and a great
enterprise in ironmongery ; and he never amounted
to very much save in a spectacular sense. After
seeing what splendid work G.R. can do in perhaps
the greatest and most tragic of Shakespeare’s
characters, to go back to Henry V. would be
bathos. Apropos of which, one Shakespearian
character that Sydney would like to see its Rig-
noid do over again is Nicholas Bottom. A few
years ago he staged the “ Midsummer Night’s
Dream ” in a casual sort of fashion to fill a gap in
his arrangements ; and in a perfectly casual way
he gave a rendering of Nicholas, the weaver,
which any intelligent community would be glad to
see over again. This isn’t a specially intelligent
community, but at least it tries to look like one.
** * *
Fitzgerald's valued circus is doing great
things at Redfern, adjacent to the Benevolent
Asylum and the old cemetery. The horse-riding
tiger and the biking elephant with the lion on top
of him are probably responsible for one-half of the
boom, and the general excellence of the show
which is engineered by brother Thomas and
brother Dan’l will account for the rest. Rickards
is running a good programme at the Tivoli, but
it isn’t so strikingly unlike some previous pro-
grammes that the difference calls for diffuse com-
ment, and any variation which exists will hold
over comfortably till next week.

Miss Pattie Browne, who is going to pack
her numerous properties in her bag, 'and shut it
forcibly, and hie away once more to London, is to
have a benefit at Her Majesty’s (Sydney) on
Friday (7th April), when as many people as can
get into the programme will lend their assistance.
As for the people who wanted to get into the pro-
gramme but were crowded out, they will gnash
their teeth outside in the cold. Other news from
H.M.’s is that the “Geisha,” now near the end of
its eighth week, hangs out there as usual. Long
may it hang.
** * *
“Round the World in Eighty Days ” has
lived into its second week at Sydney Opera House,
but when the holiday season is over the old-estab-
lished curse of the premises will probably get
into working order again, and pull down fat
Charles Maurice’s venture. At present the curse
is laid up for alterations and repairs, or is away
having a rest. Relative to the show and the com-
pany, there is no call to add anything to the gloom
which was published last week. As regards the
most-advertised lady of the cast, she is—well, she
is, and the matter is charitably dropped there.
The rest of the company are also, most of them at
least; they are the same thing that the lady is.
Dante will reach his 101st and final performance
at the Palace on Saturday, and Sydney parts from
the cherubic young woman with regret.
** * *
Truly, nothing ever seems to become quite
dead in this world. If any ordinary man were
asked to name, off-hand, the deadest piece of
literature on earth, he would probably suggest
that ancient snake and beer-pot horror, “Ten
Nights in a Bar-Room.” And yet, just by way of
something light and reviving for the festival of St.
Bun, the old tale of tanglefoot was staged at
Sydney Royal Standard, and no one fired a well-
earned pewter at it and laid it out.
## * *
“ Woman and Wine,” the new melodrama
at Melb. Royal, seems to be the most distempered
box of tricks that Bland Holt has ever put upon
the market. It starts in England without a
visible motive, and deviates into a misunder-
standing between Walter Baker and Miss Ross,
after which it rushes away to Paris and revels in
spectacular lunacy for two hours and a quarter.
Miss Watson, as the oft-told female with a
“marble heart,” stands in the centre of the pro-
ceedings and invites reprisals. She completes the
ruin of her infatuated Baker by persuading a de-
spondent jockey to slip from the saddle of Baker’s
gee-gee after he has cleared the last jump, and when
the jockey flies to her for protection from the mob,
she snorts defiance at him and he is seen no more.
Also, the red-haired siren flaunts her callousness
in the chalked face of Baker, and expresses cold
contempt for a stout johnnie (who is either a
French duke or a British attorney, The Bulletin
can’t say which), and trifles with the sinful affec-
tions of Albert Norman in the presence of his
other girl. A hunchbacked Caliban hovers around
these various indignant dupes, advising them
not to put up with any more of Miss Watson’s
scorn, and the result is an all-round effort
to get her murdered by Baker. Somebody
pours 15 drops of essence of homicide into
Baker’s liquor, but the dose is too strong,
and just when he ought to be choking the en-
chantress Baker falls asleep across a basket at the
flower market. So Miss Fitzmaurice Gill, as the
other girl, comes along in the early morning and
Miss Watson comes after her, and the two ladies
jab at one another with knives which good old
Caliban has carefully sharpened on a grindstone.
It is a long, monotonous duel, and Miss Giil wins.
Then Miss Watson’s body is buried in a box of
roses, where it is duly discovered by Miss Ross,
who is now a Parisian flower-girl and the sole
support of Baker’s blind father. Therefore, Baker
is tried for murder in the last act, but the moment
he is found guilty Miss Gill arrives in court,
brandishing the knife that did the deed, where-
upon the convicted prisoner hops out of dock, and
the curtain falls hastily.
* * * •
The one pleasing character in this comedy
of horrors is Caliban (Arthur Styan), who puts a
keen edge upon the knives, and shoves the corpse
into the box, and chuckles joyfully over the hope
that the wrong person will be guillotined for the
crime. None of the others are at all interesting,
or humorous. The “ comic relief,” by Bland Holt
in an Eton jacket, Charles Brown, and Mrs. Holt,
is very thin and utterly irrelevant to the plot.
Also the plot, which is mostly a collection of
worn-out situations from other melodramas, has
the peculiar demerit of leaving the British hero
stone-broke in a foreign country, with a blind
father and a lackadaisical flower girl on his
hands. Miss Watson is dead, Miss Gill expects
to be found guilty and recommended to mercy,
Styan and Albert Norman are in custody as
accessories before the fact, the jockey has disap-
peared for good, and nobody troubles to wonder
what became of the stout johnnie who was either
a duke or a solicitor. As for the staging of
“Woman and Wine,” it is exceptionally good.
Some of Brunton’s backgrounds are delightful;
the steeplechase is worthy of Blandolt traditions ;
the bustling cafe scene and the ballet in the third
act would make the fortune of a better drama.
But they are not likely to make this drama’s
fortune.
** # *
The Alexandra, which opened with mild
enthusiasm on Saturday, and drew a substantial
audience, at prices ranging from a tanner to two
bob, is likely to smell less mildewed next Satur-
day, when the odors of bygone desolation will have
had a chance to get wanned off the premises.
“The Life and Death of Jack Sheppard ” is the
Alexandra programme for next Saturday, and re-
cognised members of the burglary profession will
be admitted free on production of their cards.
Meanwhile the co., headed by ’Gustus Glover, is
producing “Lost in London” on a scale of tre-
mendous pathos. Miss Elsie Lander is the lost
one. A wealthy mine-owner carries her off to
London along with her guilty conscience what
time Glover, her husband, is digging coal from a
bluestone quarry somewhere in the bowels of
Lancashire. A large neighbor, by the name of
Tiddy, comes down in a basket to break the news,
and Glover registers a solemn oath not to dig any
more coal for the bloated capitalist who has pur-
loined his wife, whereat the gallery rises like one
boy and cheers vociferously. The story of how
Glover shakes the coal-dust from his boots and gets
into the basket and finds his wife is not as
lively as one could wish, but it will serve to pave
the way for “ Jack Sheppard.”
** * *
The “ Belle of New York,” which brought
a reputation to Melbourne, and disappointed the
public badly on Saturday night, seems to offer
scope for brighter treatment than it gets from the
new company at the Princess’s. True, the scheme
of the entertainment is exceptionally puerile, but
the same objection in the case of Gaiety burlesque
was overcome by the personal influence of Fred
Leslie and his smart colleagues. Their success
depended entirely on themselves. The “ Belle of
New York” is a tuneful little farce, with an
avowed lunatic roaming at large amidst the
extravagance of it all. The lunatic is a casual, un-
necessary intruder, but seeing that he has plenty
of space to move about in, he might be a
warm favorite if he could sing and dance
like a Tom Wootwell. A versatile comedian
would earn popularity rather than ridicule
for the lunatic’s frequent intrusion. Oscar
Girard, the company’s leading funny man, is a
humorous actor of the dry, pessimistic type, who
sings and shakes his legs a little ; but several other
male items offer no particular excuse for having
arrived here on their present business. The young
hero is especially lacking in suitable stage accom-
plishments. Among the ladies, Miss Louise
Hepner plays the name-part sweetly, and is the
one artist in the crowd whose personality grips the
audience and gives life to the situation. She im-
parts a suspicion of sentimental interest to her
young man, notwithstanding his uncertain age.
Miss Belle Bucklin, as an orthodox Gay
Parisienne, is fairly satisfactory, and the various
human extras and spectacular effects are rather
less brilliant than was expected.

The police and warders of Melbourne, who
are running a stall at St. Vincent’s Hospital Fair,
gauge the popular taste with much accuracy by
having as a side-show a working model of the gai
lows at Melb. Gaol. The erection of the blithe-
some timbers was carried out personally by the
Official Scragger, and every detail, not forgetting
the dull sickening thud, is faithfully reproduced
Large crowds paid their thruppenies on the °P.® n '
ing night to see the “ turning-off ” of the white-
masked doll which wriggles on the end of the rope,
and as Melb. produced 8000 who visited the
morgue to see “ the head ” of Mabel Ambrose, and
picnicked on the lawn there, it is expected that
several bushels of “tray-bits ” will be the resultant
of the present cheerful show.
** * *
The Age and Argus critics of the Firm’s
Melbourne productions seem to base their opinions
on the ‘ ‘ enthusiasm ” or otherwise of the first-night
audience. “The Geisha ” was ridiculously over-
praised in the press because it met with a “ great
demonstration of approval from a delighted
house,” or words to that effect. That is to say,
the loudly-applauding minority had things all
their own way on “ The Geisha’s ” first night, and
the newspapers published notices to match the
opera’s reception. “ The Belle of New York ” was
somewhat “guyed” last Saturday, therefore the
Age and Argus found no transcendant merit in
the show. The one said: “With whimsicalities
of this flavor a hit is scored now and again, but,
frankly, we think it problematical how far they
can be relied on to divert Australian audiences.”
And the other said, “The Belle of New York,
though in no sense a ‘frost,’ has at best scored
only an equivocal success, and it now remains to
be seen whether judicious alterations will serve,”
&c., &c., &c. This is not criticism, but mere
apology for a non-“triumph.” Intelligent people
comparing these notices with the nauseating
praise given to “ The Geisha,” will be disposed to
ask whether any part of the new show can be more
peurile than the tail end of the Jappy-Jap-Jappy
opera where Miss Perry comes along in the thin
disguise of a fortune-teller.

Miss Amy Castles, the very young lady
who lately hurled her big soprano voice at the
Austral Salonites with encouraging results, is to
make a first public appearance in Melb. Town
Hall next Saturday night. Wallace Brownlow
and W. G. Barker will hkewise contribute warbles
to the programme, but the main question for con-
sideration will be the effect of Miss Amy’s top-
note upon an audience that has been told to pre-
pare itself for paralysis.
Colonel R. D’Orsay Ogden, an American ex-military
actor of 22 years’ standing in Australia, is to have a
matinee benefit on influential principles at Sydney
Criterion next Thursday (April 13). The Bulletin’s cor-
dial invitation to the public to roll up and give the fine
old gentleman a good send-off on his retirement from a
thankless profession, will be expressed at greater length
next week.
M ary Weir, the danseuse, about to star in the law-
court, first appeared, at. 6 years old, as central figure of a
hornpipe introduced into G. B. W. Lewis’s juvenile
“Pinafore,” at old Melb. Academy of Music. Flora
Graupner, Violet Varley and May Pollard all debutted in
the same performance.
Herr Friedenthal, a well-known German pianist, now at
Perth, W.A., fs the latest important musician to visit
Australia. Messrs. Nicholson and Co., his agents,
announce his appearance in Melbourne in the middle of
April, and in Sydney some time in May. Chopin is the
new pianist’s specialty. He is a man nearing 40, so that
the maturity we seldom find in our visiting stars—who
are always overblown or underdone—should be a valuable
quality in his interpretations. He plays a Bechstein piano,
and, according to a memoir by one Carl Yhering, is a
scientific man and a great traveller, with a solid Con-
tinental reputation.
Harry Rickards has bought his gold-mine, the Sydney
Tivoli theatre and hotel.
Mr. Crowley, proprietor of Bendigo’s only theatre, died
the other day. He was father of Dr. Crowley, the medico
around whom the Melb. Women’s Hospital squabble
raged, and was a keen business man who always favored
five-act plays on account of the bars of the adjoining pub.
—also his. * Two of his sons are medicos, Neil, the subject
of the row, being one of the few men posessed of a B.A.
degree in addition to his medical one. He is a clean-
shaven, smiling-visaged, powerfully-built young-fellow,
and makes athletic enthusiasts weep tears of blood at a
great pro. lost to the world.
Archbishop Murphy (R.C.), of Hobart, who never goes
out after dark, and hadn’t been at a public entertainment
for years, went to see Fitzgeralds’ Circus.
At Broken Hill, the other night, during the opening
performance of a “Jack and the Beanstalk” pantomine
(Broken Hill’s first panto., by the way) a stalls patron was
accompanied by a bull terrier. The appearance of Jack’s
“cow” was received by the dog with growls of disap-
proval, and when the animal came forward and friskily
began to “bunt,” the little animal could stand no more.
Bounding on to the stage it attacked the legs of the
“cow”; and the spectacle which followed of the fore-
quarters streaking for the prompt side and of the hind-
quarters and ribs struggling to a place of safety on the
O.P. side brought down the house.
“ Haresfoot” : Fewer actors have excelled as Othello,
than in any other great Shakespearian part, except, per-
haps, King Lear. Garrick, Kemble, Macready, Phelp-
Irving, the Booths, and manjr other good men, comparas
tively failed in it. The first outstanding Othello of the
English stage is Edmund Kean’s. Then there is a gap
till Brooke startled London. Another gap to Salvini’s
terrific performance, ending by a kick right over Shake-
speare's straps, when he cut his throat with his scimitar.
Edwin Forrest is reported to have almost equalled Brooke
in Othello. They were wonderfully alike.
G. Republican Dibbs says that over the
Want cable affair Premier Reid is “ telling half-a-
lie.” Well, the Tennysonian adage notwithstand-
ing, N.S.W. politics are improving. Once it was
“ You’re a liar 1 ” Now, “ You’re half a liar ! ”
A large proportion of the second edition
(2000 copies) of Will Ogilvie’s poems, published by
The Bulletin, were ordered in advance. Single
copies will be sent post free, from Bulletin office,
to any address, for 4s. 6d. in stamps. “The most
charming book of Australian verse that has yet
appeared,” writes Victor Daley.

COLUMN
“R B.”: There is not the least prospect of any
shareholder'getting a penny out of either co. Neither of
them issues balance-sheets now, but the liquidator now
and then publishes a statement showing receipts and
expenditure for the year. The statements are mostly of
a kind calculated to drive an ordinary man to drink.
Better keep on owing your calls and lie low.
* * » *
One of the most senseless things in Victoria is
the system of granting endowments to municipalities.
Up to a certain point, the thing is admirably worked out.
Cities and towns get 10s. on the £ of rates collected;
boroughs, 155.; first-class shires, 12s. 6d. ; second-class
ditto 245. ; third-class, 28s. ; fourth-class, 40s. ; fifth-
class’47s. 6d. ; sixth-class, 555. on the £. There are only
eight sixth-class shires—districts like Orbost, where there
are 2700 people to make and keep in order roads and
bridges over 4000 square miles of precipitous country—
and they certainly deserve their 555. on the £ endowment.
But—and this is where the humor of the situation comes
in—the endowment is only paid on the rates up to
Is. in the £. On any rates over that there is no endow-
ment. If a 6th class shire levies a rate of 2s. in the £ on
£20,000 worth of annual value, the first shilling produces
£3750 ; the second shilling only £lOOO. Therefore there
is very little inducement to make the rates more than Is.
in the £, whether the roads go to wreck or not—the
second shilling produces so little by comparison that no
one takes much interest in it. The obvious way to make
Vic. local government brace up and raise enough revenue
to meet its liabilities is to pay no endowment whatever on
the first Is. in the £ of rates, but to pay it on everything
over Is. The system wants turning upside-down.

The Mutual Life Association issues a report for
1898 which compares with that of some previous years as
follows
Total Added
Premiums. Interest. Income. to Funds.
1891 ..£154,198 ..£45,257 ..£199,455 ..£76,578
1892 .. 151,066 .. 50,194 .. 201,260 .. 73,366
1893 .. 146,762 .. 52,525 .. 199,287 .. 54,748
1894 ..141,994 .. 53,235 .. 195,229 .. 46,515
1895 .. 141,822 .. 53,543 .. 195,365 .. 38,782
1896 .. 143,138 .. 54,584 .. 197,722 .. 47,993
1897 .. 151,203 .. 57,373 .. 208,576 .. 54,433
1898 .. 158,118 .. 58,079 .. 216,197 .. 67,411
Taking everything into account 1898 has been a good
year with the Mutual Life Association. The premium
income has more than recovered from the drop it suffered
in the worst period of the depression ; the interest receipts,
despite the fall in the rate of interest, and the total in-
come are the largest in the Association’s record, and the
addition made to the funds is the largest since 1892.
The total funds are now £1,257,604, so the Mutual Life
Association ranks sixth among Australasian life offices,
the five which are ahead of it being the A.M.P., Maoriland
State Life Department, National Mutual, Colonial Mutual,
and Widows’ Fund, in that order. The assets look very
sound, the only objectionable feature being that “ pro-
perties acquired by foreclosure, £71,700,” is rather a large
item as against only £646,000 of mortgages. Apparently
one-tenth of the whole mortgages have had to be fore-
closed, which is above the average. Against this the
Mutual Life Association has one good feature which is
peculiarly its own. It writes off losses and depreciation
every year, and never lets bogus assets accumulate in its
balance-sheet. This year £6196 is written off, and in the
last six years the amount written off has been £57,402.
This policy of providing for losses as they arise is one that
other institutions would do well to follow.
The Mutual Life Association is making reserves
to enable it to reduce its estimates of future interest to
3J per cent.—another precaution which can’t be too much
commended. The present interest earnings are £4 14s.
lid. per cent per annum, so the reduction of the esti-
mite to £3 10s. will leave a good margin of security. The
one great trouble about the Mutual Life Association is its
heavy expense rate. This year it spent in expenses £l9
13s. per cent, of the total receipts, or £26 17s. per cent, of
premiums—a heavier ratio than that shown by the
A.M.P., Maoriland State Life Department, National
Mutual, Colonial Mutual, or the Citizens’ (ordinary
department), though it is distinctly better than
the very bad record just put up by the Widows’
Fund, and far ahead of such offices as the City
Mutual and Temperance and General. Still there
has been a great reduction since 1890, when the ex.’s
absorbed £33165. Bd. out of every £lOO of premiums, and,
as the improvement seems to keep going on, the Associa-
tion will probably become a reasonably cheap institution
some day.

N.S.W. Civil Service Co-operative Society re-
ports a profit of £4968 for the half-year to 31st Jan., 1899.
Some recent profits compare as follows :
January, 1896 .. £3273 January, 1898 .. £4250
July, 1896 .. 3275 July, 1898 .. 4456
January, 1897 .. 3785 January, 1899 .. 4968
July, 1897 .. 3962
A dividend of 10 per cent, per annum to shareholders
(£350) and a bonus of 10 per cent, on shareholders’ pur-
chases (£4253) leave a rather narrow margin of £365,
making the profit balance £394. The paid-up capital is
£6995, and the reserve-fund £11,500. Considering that
the Society, at date of balance-sheet, had only £365 cash
on hand, and the bank overdraft was £3325, it isn’t any
too clear where the div. and bonus are to come from, and
it looks as if the business was growing too large for the
capital.
• * * •
The Co-operative Wool and Produce Co.
(Sydney) closed its first year on 28th Feb., 1893, with a
profit balance of £1077. Out of this it decided to pay a 6
per cent, dividend on its then paid-up capital of £843,
and to distribute as much of the balance as the directors
thought fit in bonuses to constituents. Second year
ended on 28th Feb., 1899, with a profit balance of £5394,
but, as no profit-and-loss account is included in the balance-
sheet, it is impossible to say exactly how much of this is
the year’s profit and how much is the balance brought
forward from previous year, after paying the 6 per
cent. div. and the bonus that the board thought
fit. Probably it is quite safe to assume that £5OOO
is the current year’s earnings. At all events,
the second year’s profit is a vast increase on the
first one, and the business is extending greatly. Share-
holders get a 6 per cent, dividend and 15s. per cent,
bonus on the value of their produce sold ; and these,
according to the balance-sheet, amount to something un-
specified, which, when deducted from the £5394 available
profit, leaves a balance of something or other, or there-
abouts. From a daily paper report of the chairman’s
speech, however, it would seem that the co. distributes
£2198; puts £l5OO to a reserve-fund and carries forward
£1696.

The hopeless-looking N.S.W. Shale and Oil Co.
made a profit of £691 for the half-year to 31st Dec., 1898
(equal to about 1J per cent, per annum on capital), as
compared with £284 previous.half-year. Being in a reck-
less mood, it paid a div. at the rate of £24s. Sid. percent.
per annum (£833). The Shale and Oil 00., like the faded
lady who runs a hash-house, has seen better days. Six
years ago its profits averaged £20,000 a year. It feu badly
in ’94 (profits, £1142) ; flickered up again in 95, when
the profits reached over £ll,OOO, and finally fell into re-
jooced circumstances ” in ’96. Now it looks as if the final
closure was not far off.

During this period, the robust Barrier railway monopoly
has capitalised £32,968 of its profits ; has paid off, also out

bought new rolling-stock (out of profits), and otherwise

* * * *
Hobart Railway League, hitherto a pliant
friend to the Great-Western Gridiron Railway Grab, has
at last snapped at the hand of Chaffey Baker, and that
worthy syndicator naturally complains gently of the
league’s disloyalty. At last meeting of the league, chair-
man G. P. Fitzgerald forced a motion that enquiries
should be made, as to whether the syndicate, as reported,
was really asking Bull a million-and-a-quarter for its
Gridiron, this exorbitant reach-out being the cause of the
delay. This decision stirred things up, for, within a
few days, Agent-General Fysh wired from London :
“Great Western underwritten to £1,100,000 ; flotation
certain.”

B.H. Proprietary announces shilling divs. per
quarter instead of eightenpennies—a change which,
though not officially whispered, was pretty easily foretold
from the last accounts. The change is “ temporary,” of
course, like all mining div.-reductions. That is the ad-
vantage of having such delightfully vague words as
“ temporary ” in the language. If, however, the word
means just a half-year or two, the shareholder has by
this time probably begun to wonder why the reserve-fund
which was created to equalise divs. is not touched. It
stands at £125,000 now—and has stood there for two or
three years past, earning about £4OOO a year interest.
Obviously, however, it wasn’t built up simply to give 3£
per cent, to shareholders. The best way to account for
the fact that this div.-equalising reserve hasn’t been
broached is that, for all practical purposes, this isn’t likely
to be a temporary reduction at all; and the fact that the
reserve dates from about the strike-time suggests that
this wasn’t the sort of div.-equalising that was intended,
anyway.
B.H. Proprietary is still not wholly a sulphide
mine. Last half-year 203,000 tons of ore was handled ; of
this, 78,000 tons was sulphide and 125,000 tons oxidised
ores. The smelting plant treated 164,000 tons or its
equivalent; the chloridising and leaching plants 39,000
tons. By the concentration of the sulphides the volume
of the just-mentioned 164,000 tons was reduced to 101,000
tons prior to smelting. During this six months the co.
had a larger smelting capacity than ever it had before.
Yet as just said, it only smelted 101,000 tons. In the
corresponding half of 1894, when oxidised ores were
exclusively handled, and on a smaller furnace capacity,
287,000 tons were smelted. But this reduction in the
quantity of ore which it has been possible to smelt, not-
withstanding the gradual increase of furnace capacity,
has been so marked since they began to treat sulphides
that the figures are given here for each half-year
Half-year to N0v.,’94 .. 287,310 tons
„ ~ May,’9s .. 236,972 „
„ N0v.,’95 .. 171,742 „
” ” Mav,’96 .. 167,352 „
, „ N0v.,’96 .. 162,577 „
! ! May,’97 .. 145,496 „
! „ N0v.,’97 .. 116,235 „
„ ~ May,’9B .. 104,393 „
” ” N0v.,’98 . 100,836 „
Clearly, then, the difficulties in the way of sulphide treat-
ment are not overcome by concentration, as too many
have supposed. The B.H. Proprietary is credited with
doing the best concentration on the Barrier ; yet the in-
troduction of only this comparatively small quantity of sul-
phide concentrates (less than 25,000 tons of the total 101,000
tons) has, with a somewhat less favorable mixture of
other ores, been sufficient to reduce the smelting capacity
per furnace so enormously. What would happen if,
through the too rapid extraction of the oxidised ores (of
which there are probably a million tons, or a little over,
exposed) a considerably larger proportion of the sulphide
material had to be introduced to the furnaces ? Even six-
penny divs. would have to be struggled for then. Yet it
is from the oxidised ores alone that large profits can be
got promptly. Shareholders will, in the circumstances,
probably accept the less of two evils, putting up with
the reduced dividends rather than using up merely as
ores that oxidised material which is becoming invaluable
for fluxing purposes. t *
Substance of a letter from Maoriland to Sydney
D.T. “Sir,—l have just seen a prospectus of a gold-
dredging co. on Turon River, N.S.W. I will give you an
idea how dredging co.s are floated here. An area of from
80 to 100 acres"is taken up along the banks of the river,
and when it has been prospected a prospectus is issued
with a capital of £5OOO or £6OOO in £1 shares. Vendors
take 1000 paid-up shares, and also 1500 contributing ; cost
of dredge, about £3500. Average returns from dredgers,
about 20oz. per week (with the exception of two or three
which got on some rich patches about Cromwell). This
leaves a profit of about £2O per week. The Turon co. has
a capital of £48,000, in £1 shares. Out of this ven-
dors take fully 24,000 fully-paid-up (no contributing),
and sell 6000 to public. Now, if vendors wish,
they can send up the price of these 6000 shares
to any value they like, and get out of their
own, making a fortune. Or, supposing the venture to
turn out a success, the vendors can sit down and smoke
their cigars, taking 16s. out of every £1 earned by the
money belonging to the public, and the few hundred
shareholders have the four shillings to divide among
them. What dq these vendors give for all this ?
Absolutely nothing but the ground they pegged out,
which may be worthless, for if there is any clay through
it it will be so. I think it would only be fair to the
public that these vendors’ shares should be held in trust
for six months after the dredge has been at work, and
there should be less of them. I have written this for the
benefit of those who may feel inclined to go into these
things without due consideration. —Puo Bono Pub-
lico." Which is a nice piece of oold iron for dredging-
promoters to chew.
The lucky Cobar tributors, who have now (say)
£200,000 a year to play with, are understood to be buying
right and left into all promising Oobar shows. Presently
they will practically own the whole field, and then (this is
an off-hand prophecy) hey for the (say) Great Cobar Con-
solidated Mining Corporation ; capital (say), three
millions.
* * * *
In consequence of the apparent initial success
of the Campbell Creek operations, there promises to be
a regular boom in Vic. dredging companies (and things
threaten to be ditto in N.S. W.). Everyone you meet has
a prospectus concealed about him, and the only defence
is to have a little “venture” of your own. As most of
these creeks have been turned over dozens of times by
fossickers and Chinamen, the public should be careful
before investing. The man who can find gold in a Chow’s
tailings deserves to.

“ Working Miner ”: Re par. of l'lth Feb., you
are in error in supposing that the 200 a. at Mt. Morgan,
acquired by Chief-Sec. Peacock, was not touched by
“ wild catters.” Fourteen years ago it was more-or-less
a “cat,” and comprised a portion of ground held by the
celebrated “ jumpers,” who were blocked by the exten-
sion of the M.M. northern boundary. Whilst prosecuting
their claim in the Privy Council, and prospecting for the
kaolin and ferruginous deposits peculiar in those days to
the mountain-top, they left the cap of a big mundic lode
in the bottom of a 100 ft. shaft, practically untouched. At
that time Mt. Morgan mundic ore was unknown. Any-
one doubting this statement can see for himself. The
thermal spring theory is “bust,” so far as Mt. Morgan is
concerned. The Linda lode is the main prop of the big
mine. It runs north and south. It now remains for Pea-
cock and co. to demonstrate whether the mundic lode
stops short (on the northern side) within the big co.’s
fence. It does not look as if it did. This is the true
history.
* • * •
Beautiful item from Melbourne Age showing
how Vic. Mining Department strains the law in favor of
the big foreign speculator “ In the Maryborough news
published yesterday in the Age it was reported that the
Mines Department’had refused two applications of Mr. L.
F. Sachs for leases of 400 and 1500 acres in Dunolly dis-
trict. It was stated that on a number of leases already
held by Mr. Sachs a very small amount of work has been
done, pending the introduction of English capital, for
which negotiations are in progress. The Mines Depart-
ment explains that the two applications were refused be-
cause it is not consistent with the policy of the depart-
ment to allow large areas to be held by one company or
syndicate. In relation to the leases already held by Mr.
Sachs, the department has been satisfied that the enter-
prise is bond-fide, and that as a large amount of outside
capital will be brought in, the suspension of the labor
covenants was reasonable. At the same time, there has
not been much work done, and the Department desires to
see some material progress made on the leases already ac-
quired. The absence of such work on those leases was
another reason for declining to issue any new leases, for
the present at any rate, to the same proprietary.”
Which seems to mean just this: That Mr. L. F.
Sachs holds a large amount of gold-bearing land, and doesn’t
work it as provided by law, and prevents other people
working it, and thereby hinders a few dozens or hundreds
of Victorians finding employment. That the Vic. Mining
Department winks at this state of things. And that its
alleged reason for winking is that Mr. Sachs promises to
discourage Victorian enterprise by handing over these leases
to John Bull. Why can’t the miserable Vic. Mining
Department be honest; or if it must stretch the law in
somebody’s favor why not stretch it in favor of the local
man who will employ local capital and labor, and keep
the dividends in the country ? At present it seems to
spend most of its time locking up the auriferous land
against its own people for the benefit of the top-hatted
speculator who wants to float a shijidykit in England.
“Cuprite,” who possibly has his own little
Chillagoe axe to grind, protests against a statement that
the Q. Mining and Prospecting Co. is the second best spec,
on the Chillagoe field. Also, he states that the Johnston
who goes *Ome to boom the co. is not “ Revel” Johnston,
but plain Johnston, a colleague of Revel. Anyway, he
will revel if he gets British boodle into the spec.

Croydon items: Waratah Co., Bull-owned,
cleaned up, after six weeks experimental crushing,
2330 z. gold (£667) from 1670 tons treated. Ore
taken from quarries and not weighed : various
estimates as to quantity crushed, some authori-
ties making it 1800 tons. Manager estimates £4OO worth
of amalgam left on plates, but, as this is guess-work, it
may be passed over. Experiment a failure ; and people are
now wondering where Warden Towner got his figures
when he estimated this ore to be worth £2 9s. per ton,
and gladdened the heart of Bull. The Warden, on the
actual result from the stamps, was only a trifle over £2
per ton out in his estimate —which isn’t much for some

* * * *
The Austral Otis Engineering Co. Ltd., South
Melb., has completed and delivered a very fine set of
winding engines for the Cobar Gold Mines, N.S.W., capable
of hauling a load of two tons from 1500 ft. at a speed of
1000 ft. per minute. The engines are a pair of 14” cylin-
ders, geared to 7ft. loose drum, and are fitted with link
motion reversing gear, vertical indicators, and powerful
brake gear. The pair of engines are set to one side of the
drums, and all the driving levers are brought behind the
drums. The co. has also just delivered a fine set of
pumping plant for the Paddy Lackey mine for taking the
shaft down to 1000 ft.

Strenuous efforts are being made in Westralia
to supplant Newcastle with Collie coal. Govt., having
leased its coal-mines to H. M. Dpakin, erstwhile of Sydney,
is now giving him special concessions to enable him to cut
out the imported article. The Singapore steamer Sultan
is using Collie coal on her present trip. It is highly
spoken of for steaming purposes, but is apparently useless
for gas-making.
* • * *
Collie, by the way, has had its first labor-
trouble—a “ difficulty ” with the wheelers. This after
less than two months’ working. Also the leading Vic.
coal-mines have had similar troubles; and there is'a
standing one at Newcastle. The coal-trade seems to be
never free from them.

• * * »
“R.”: Mount Bischoff (Tas.) tin-mine is over
the highest eminence in its career, and will now begin the
inevitable descent. No mine on earth has been luckier—
in location, climate, everything—nor easier to work. The
quality was there; what took looking after was the
quantity, and any man with a brain as big as a walnut
could get that out and through the mills without much
cogitation. Old Bischoff miners smiled when Ferd Kayser
is referred to as a “good manag'er.” Any good tin-
sluicer could have managed the Bischoff, with probably
less “ show ” than Kayser. He was for ever experiment-
ing ; every available space was crammed with trick-boxes;
every pint of water was saved to turn his little baby-
jiggers, and rock his little cradles. The more it rained,
the more he smiled, and the more his fakements roared
and rattled. But, lucky for him—lucky Bischoff again—
the tin rained, too. And the more it peppered through
the sheds, the more the shareholders clapped, and cried
“Oh ! good old Bischoff !—good old Kayser ! ” He could
afford to play gee-gee with such a mine as it was ; but
now—well, he has practically resigned.

“Hammer” to The Bulletin “Re Queen
Margaret mine, W.A., and the directors’ remarks as to
alleged gold-stealing. About two years ago a miner em-
ployed by the Q.M. Co. took lOOoz. odd to a smelter in
Kalgoorlie and casually remarked that the metal was
‘alluvial.’ Smelter at once saw it was ‘reef’ gold and
told the digger to take it elsewhere. Later on the co.
provided a changing house, other co.’s following suit.
Though stealing is still prevalent, the evil is somewhat
exaggerated, but, taking the fields as a whole, the aggre-
gate must be enormous. One of the worst features of
the well-nigh impossible task of catching the thief or
thieves red-handed is that sometimes right-down honest
fellows are suspected and lose their billets.”
And “Kink” sends this: —“A face-hand gob
his ankle hurt in one of Kalgoorlie’s non-dividend-paying
mines. Had been laid up a fortnight when I saw him ana
sympathised over loss of wages. ‘ Wages be hanged,’ he
shouted, ‘ what do I care about wages 1 I’ve lost 300
blanky quid.’ * • •
Mephan Ferguson, engineer, etc., has just com-
pleted at his engineering works, Footscray, Melb'., one of
the largest planing machines every made in any part of
the world. It weighs about 100 tons, and is the machine
to be used in planing and thickening up the edges of
28ft. x jin. plates required in manufacturing the patent
Lockingbar pipes for Coolgardie water supply contract. All
the steel bars for this contract will be supplied from the
Earl of Dudley works, Eng., but in order to get prompt
delivery the order for the steel plates has been divided
amongst four German firms. The first shipment of 500
tons will be dispatched by the Maori King, now loading.*
• * * *
Will someone start preaching about the folly of
clearing the timber off mining-fields without any attempt
at replanting ? There is hardly a metal-mining district
in the Australias that hasn’t its fuel-difficulty. The
Murchison (W.A.) was going to be an exception ; the
timber there was “ inexhaustible.” And yet here is
another, the Murchison King, added to the long list of
hung-up batteries—this one strictly on account of soarcity
of firewood. __
James Kell, of Parramatta, has started on
a hopeful trip to Glasgow. Kell understands that
his late father’s property, amounting to some
.£30,000, lies in the hands of trustees there, await-
ing a legal claimant.
Burgess, the man whom the Queensland
police tried so hard to hang over the matter of
the three dead Murphys at Gatton, has been fired
out of gaol in disgust, his sentence having expired.
He got two months on a bogus charge of vagrancy
to give the police time to grease the rope, and the
only compensation he receives for their mis-
take —so far as the public know —is the
curse which Perleeceman O’X threw at him
when he left the gaol. And yet any merely
human bobby has a natural grievance against
Burgess. If he had been the right man, then the
exhausted cop could have returned to the flesh-
pots and beer-pots of Brisbane with a light hearb
and a vindicated character. And by not being
the right man Burgess compels these cops to do
miles and miles of hard walking and riding, to
grovel with their heads in the dust looking for
“tracks,” and to seek for information among
resentful potato-growers who wouldn’t ask a
bobby if he had a mouth on him though his tongue
was hanging down to his lower waistcoat-button.

There was one very pathetic feature about
the track-side tragedy in the Wentworth district
lately—the tragedy of an old man and his wife
tramping for work and perishing together from
thirst. The old people had apparently all their
possessions with them, and it wasn’t a very big
sv ?®g then ; but it included the Family Bible
with the usual entries of births and deaths—all, so
tar as can be ascertained, that they had to show
for nearly 25 years of married life in N.S.W. If
here isn’t a Heaven there ought to be.

And in telling the jury, as I did, to disregard the New

Up with Hodges ; down with Moses ! Mind

* * * *
When one strikes the Victorian law-courts
for the first time (writes an experienced newspaper
reporter to The Bulletin) and sees the Crown-
Irosecutors at work, a sense of vast wonderment
overtakes him that such men—one of them only is
excepted from this remark-should earn as much
as a municipal mud-shoveller. After a very short
experience of the courts, however, the feeling of
wonderment fades away, and underlying the ap-
pointment of such men to the position one sees
good sound reason. In no country should a
brovernment pay twice over for anything. It
is unnecessary to pay keen Crown-Prosecutors on
the floor of the court when there are such excel-
lent men doing 1 the work on the bench.
* * * *
Gaol administration likes all criminals to
die admitting the justice of their doom, and so
on ; and if they won’t admit it words are dropped
which serve the public as a base for a consolatory
report. Blue Mountain Butler, according to gaol
report, fathered his crimes on drink. There is
plenty of reason for rejecting this as a pious tee-
total invention—Butler’s crimes were sober and of
infinite cold-blood. A high authority dropped
hints to the effect that Butler confessed to him
the perpetration of four murders. The number,
notvyithstanding, may have been less or more.
Louisa Collins was rumored in the same way to
have acknowledged herself an absolute Borgia,
but the public are now satisfied that her lips
stayed sealed as the Sphinx’s. Johns, executed
for attempted murder, was represented as peni-
tent, but the true record of his last utterances to
beseeching parsons was, “ You think you can save
my soul; why don’t some of you try to save my
body ? Deeming, another reputed contrite, died
a mixture of funk and bravado. The dying state
of Archer and others was similarly misrepre-
sented. Gaol warders and chaplains look on a
penitent death as the fitting result of their treat-
ment of a criminal, and self-interest and self-
conceit torture trifling words of the condemned
into repentance and confession. So Sunday-
school stories are built up. Few tales are more
unreliable than those which, on or about the day
of an execution, gaol chaplains and warders
whisper respecting the frame of mind in which a
condemned dies.

Queer fact that the recent sensational
Melbourne inquest should have brought Dr. Gaze
into trouble. Gaze practises at the house where
Coroner Youl lived and joked for 40 years, the
house that was in the first place a snug little cot-
tage confronting a paddock where cows foraged
f or .. tmficer, and is now a solemn three-storey
building at the toney end of Collins-street. One
doesn t need a very strong imagination to believe
that the spirit of jolly old Youl hovered around
the trial of Dr. Gaze, and urged the jury to give
his new medical friend the benefit of every doubt.
* * * *
Lately a 17-months-old child died at Mt.
Gambier in great agony from tuberculosis.
Another child of the same family was then found
to have the disease. No hereditary taint being
discoverable, the family cow fell under suspicion ;
but her fine condition and healthy appearance were
standing evidence that she had been libelled. Yet,
examination, tuberculosis was found in
the udder ; and the microscope disclosed bacilli
111 milk. South Australia puts every tree
under an inspector’s eye in dread of some grub or
other ; but a diseased poley gets a chance to kill
a lot of people before she is turned into hide and
tallow.
• * * •
For one thing Westralia may be praised.
It has an Early Closing Act that is being en-
forced. Shops in Perth, Fremantle, &e., may not
open before 8 a.m. or after 6 p.m., and on Wednes-
days there is a half-holiday. On Saturdays they
open till 10 p.m. From this chemists, news-
agents, fruiterers, hair-dressers and such are ex-
empt—but the Act applies strictly to the bulk of
retail shops. There has been a little trouble on
the margin of the Act, defining the operation of
exemption clauses. Otherwise the law works
smoothly, and is a benefit.

A young Melb. civil servant applied to
Vic. Insolvency Court last week for the annul-
ment of an order directing him to pay .£3 per
month to his creditors. When he filed his
schedule (in ’96), he was “doing the heavy,” it
appeared, on £9 per month, and such incidental
items as 16 guineas for a dress-suit and 30s. for
lure of a carriage to take himself and lady to a
ball (probably Guv ment House ball) were charged
against him. At this distance of time, the insol-
vent deriding can’t remember whether the lady
aforesaid was young or old. He has been to so
many different balls that his recollections are
mixed, fourteen months elapsed between the
filing of his schedule and the issue of the order—
beastly, cwuel awwangement, doncherknow—to
pay £3 a month ; but seeing that he had already
paid £63 on the beastly ordah, the young man
thought it was time the Court gave him an uncon-
ditional certificate. Judge Molesworth took a
measure of compassion on the crippled swell.
The order was reduced to £2 a month, and insol-
vent made his own terms as to payment of five
guineas costs incurred by his pathetic application,
the terms being cash within the next three years.
Meantime he may be expected to achieve a
wealthy marriage as the reward for his social
enterprise and 16-guinea dress-suit.

Westralia is glad nowadays to get almost
anything in the shape of population, so General
oalvation Booth—he of the pawnbroker’s red nose
and the sunset jersey—has acquired 21,000 acres of
good coal, agriculture and timber land, with 17
miles frontage to Collie River, on which to plant
a colony of neglected youths and soiled doves and
other derelicts. If Booth lives it will probably be
the richest spot in Westralia by-and-bye. When
that eager, hungry-looking patriarch, with his
HALF-BRED.
D ICK: “What breed is your dog , Tom? ”
T OM: “ Well, he’s one-half terrier and one-half plain dog."
claw-like hands and his three-ball eye, pits his keen
business methods against the listless Sandgroper
he is bound to come out on top. That tired drag
in the feet which characterises the agricultural
’groper doesn’t run in the Booth family. Even in
the General’s old age the fleetest sixpence in the
land with 100yds. start wouldn’t keep ahead of
him in a mile race.

Religion has had a lot to answer for lately.
The woman who drowned her handsome little son
at Port Melbourne by pressing him face down-
wards into a few inches of water is now getting
moments of crazy satisfaction from an idea that
the child “was to be exalted.” The mad baker
who slaughtered himself and family at York
(S.A.)_ was an ardent member of the York
Christian Church. At Easter-time a true Believer
chews his Lenten dogma cud : A t Easter-time his
morbid fancy darkly turns to thoughts of blud.
During recent examination-in-bankruptcy
of a Sydney doctor it appeared that he had sent
to London for sale a valuable collection of stamps,
representing the labor of eight or ten years,”
but had never received a penny of the proceeds,
which should have been about ,£BOO. The con-
signee, “a well-known stamp-collector,” has “dis-
appeared.” The Bulletin is prepared, any day,
to hear of one philatelist murdering another for
the sake of his collection.

. S' r > If any person that is ill of any incurable disease
likes to go a few days without all kinds of liquids and live
on dry bread and cabin biscuits, they can cure themselves
any disease the human system is liable to. The process
of this cure is the navel and kidney. It is a faithful cure.
Habitual inflammation and suppuration takes plane in
two or three days. The very worst kind of sore eyes are
cured in three days. Take care of broken bones during
this cure, if any, and keep warm. Warm weather is
favorable to the cure. Any weak or delicate person
should have half a pint of water every third day. I can
speak of this cure from personal experience. Luxuries
•V o*’ 0 *’ l a k en with this cure. I had rupture,
hydatids, stoppage of the bile, paralysis and tuberculous
consumption all cured with this cure. I went nine days
without water, not even washing myself, while under-
going this cure. At the end of that time I was cured.

BARRIS.
THE DERELICT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Lord, if it be Thy will that I should float
A loveless stranger on the ocean’s breast,
Without a convoy hanging in my wake,
Without a friendly hail from passing boat;
Wrapped into loneliness, yet ne’er at rest
And nought at stake,
Then, God of Thunders, give me stormy days !
I cannot bear this empty summer sea,
This driftless gliding on an even keel :
Stretch forth Thy hand and touch the waterways,
And let the waves and rollers hammer me
My heart to steel.
VON KOTZE.

More linotype. In one Southern daily’s
“funerals” recently occurred, “Will leave the
deceased’s late residence for the place of amuse-
ment,” also, “ Will be interred in the Melbourne
General Assembly.” As said Gen. Ass. was sit-
ting at the time, this was classed as too personal,
and got the operator the “ shoot.”
• * m - •
Since the announcement that Sydney
steam trams would be gradually superseded by the
electric system, numbers of scared drivers and
assistants have taken time by its solitary white
hair and applied for billets on the railway, many
jumping down from 12s. or 13s. a day to as low as
355. a week. Seeing how the Commissioners’
scheme now hangs fire, most of those cautious
people are already lamenting their haste.
* * * *
Disgraceful scenes are reported to have
taken place at the Victoria Barracks, Sydney, last
Thursday. Most of the permanent men having
gone into encampment, the Barracks was practic-
ally handed over to the volunteers. On Thursday
night there was a scene of riot lasting from about
10 p.m. till 3 a.m. The men began to drink, and
went on to smashing windows and throwing things
into disorder ; as a result of the mix-up some had
to be taken off to hospital. There was talk of
turning the whole lot out afterwards, but things
were .smoothed over. Now the gates are closed at
10 p.m. both for ingress and egress. Great dis-
satisfaction is the outcome of the encampment of
the Australian Horse upon “ Constitution Hill,”
this Easter. “ Constitution Hill ” turns out to be
merely another name for Mount Rennie, the
locale of the main city dustheap.

The first leader in Melb. Age of March 30
began: “The circumstance that the Age will not
be published to-morrow brings to mind the
approach of Easter holidays.” The politician who
dated the new era of progress from the day he was
elected to Parliament was of the Age order. Why
not re-ehristen Good Friday “ Nonage Day ”?

Religious crankiness strikes a silly height
at Bathurst. A local parson holds “white” ser-
vices on Sunday. The worshippers’ dresses are
white, the emblems are white, hymns with the
word' “ white ’’ predominating are selected, and
the sermon displays as much “ white ” as possible.
There is probably also a lot of white of eyes.

Police-magistrate King, who lately left
Australia for Norfolk Island will have some pecu-
liar laws to dispense among that mentally-feeble
community. A dog that chases goats is visited
with the death-penalty. If he is found breaking
an egg, he is summarily executed. If any person
kill a cat, that person’s dog is seized by law and
killed in return. Should the cat-killing person
have no dog, the magistrate must seek an
equivalent in some other direction. If a pig
be found running loose, the owner must
satisfy the court that the animal was sick at
the time, and was out for medical exercise,
or he forfeits the porker. And so on.
Magistrate King will feel himself re-
urning to the Jatck and the Beanstalk
state of mind.
Martha Adams, who lately
suicided by hanging in Norfolk Is-
land, is given rank as the first
suicide in Norfolk Island annals.
Jf the annals of the descendants
of the Bounty mutineers be meant,
the rating may pass, but Marti 1a
is not by hundreds the first suicide
of Norfolk Island. That hell of the
Pacific had its suicides by the score
on every judicial visitation from
Sydney. Scores without physical cou-
rage to kill themselyes vised ito kneel to
companions apd beg to have the knife
driven into them. Scarcely a cell of
the old prison but was credited with the
spectre of a self-murderer. _ Since the
penal establishment was abolished there,
the -island has probably been lying
asleep, resting from its prg.y ,of malig-
nant cruelty and despair, up til Martha
prods ft now with her toes in the air.
And it is worth noting that, as the first
suicide in Botany Bay was effected
by hanging, so, as soon as N.S. W. re-obtains
control of Norfolk Island, the first suicide is
again by hanging.
* * * .*
Time a royal commission, or something,
was appointed to inquire into the demoralising in-
fluence of a Sydney northeaster. During this
wind’s prevalence, the average citizen goes out iji
■the morning dressed in a clean shirt and a rigid
•morality. By lunch-time his collar is half-way
down his back and his integrity is out of plumb }
ere night he is a physical wreck, and utterly des-
titute of moral principle ; also, he is generally
‘half-drunk. Sydney can never be a moral city
until the northeaster is abolished, and unless the
N.S. W. provincialists will guarantee to do this,
The Bulletin will vote the establishment of the
Federal city elsewhere.
* * * *
There has been much talk about black-
mailing in Melbourne, Sydney is not clear either.
The Domain, of course, is a favorite hunting-
ground, but Mosman is nearly as bad. One recent
Saturday afternoon writer observed an organised
gang of six men working the Cremorne side of
the bay. Coming along shore, he saw two of the
sneaks flat on a rock just above the heads of a
couple beneath. A shouted warning brought a
volley of stones in his direction—and this in
broad daylight.
Every woman has her price ; some of the
best go for a ten-shilling wedding-ring.

nas nearly faded from people’s minds. In method

ease the man took the initiative. In each
case perverted passion was at the base of
the crime. In each case the husband
was the slayer of his wife. To find a
parallel with the Miller-Allardice

Seen by a Bulletin man, on the “ seven -
teent of Ould Ireland,” in a Melb. street: Two
little girls walking placidly to school, neat and
tidy, and at peace with the world ; one carrying a
large bunch of yellow flowers. They pass a third
girl, of about eight, bathing her bare feet in the
gutter. No. 3 espies the flowers, and her face
lights up with sudden venom. She dashes at the
bearer, snatches the bouquet from her hand,
batters her about the face with it, scratches and
claws hair with animal ferocity, and then throws
the flowers into the gutter and jumps them into
the mud. “There’s fer yer dirty yaller !” she
screams after the weeping victim.

In many Sta,te-schools children
are taught hpw to act in case of snake-bite.
The directions officially supplied are, however,
ludicrously insufficient. The other day, a case
pf snake-bite at Roma was treated by depart-
mental methods. Ligatures were placed on the
bitten limb, left there for eight hours, until circula-
tion was completely arrested, and then the arm
had to be amputated at theshoulder !
* * • •
A queer snake-story copies from Foster,
Gippsland. A six-year-old youngster, having been
bitten on the finger, seized the snake near the
head and ran with it to his mother, who killed
the snake and chopped off the youngster’s finger.
Doctor expected to pull the boy through at time
of writing.

The other day a woman went to Lavender
Bay (Sydney) baths, disrobed, and plumped into
deep water, though she could not swim. The proprie-
tor’s sister jumped in,.clothes on, and rescued her.
Woman then dressed, and, on going out, asked
for her 3d. back as she had not had her swim. ,
From “The Banjo
Dear Bulletin. —l see that my recent article on the
Northern Territory has evoked a pathetic protest from a
correspondent, who says that I “ never met Paddy Cahill
and have wronged the N.T.”
This is a terrible state of things. It was bad enough
for me to wrong the N.T., but not to meet Cahill was
simply unpardonable. I can only plead that Cahill, on
the occasion of my visit, was away murdering buffaloes,
and I unfortunately missed him. It was my loss. I hear
by all accounts that, he is riot, only a crack buffalo-shooter,
but a very white man, and when I said that he hunted
buffaloes dressed in a towel and a diamond ring I did not
think he would take it much to heart. To save trouble I
withdraw the statement, and now assert most emphati-
cally that he always hunts buffaloes while dressed in a
frock coat, a pot hat, and patent leather boots, like ap
English Book goipg to shoot dipjtybirds. While ( arp
about it I might as vyell withdraw spine other piis-state-
ments ip my article, ps I would do apything rather than
hurt the feelings of the N.T. folk. To hegip with, I
accused them of Booze, Blasphemy ami Blow, and I wifi
show’ you how I came to make the mistakes.
As to the booze, I was misled by seeing all those heaps,
piles, mountains of bottles all along the D.T.—I mean
N.T. —railway line. Of course, I see now that they never
could have contained booze, but only soft-tack—sarsa-
parilla and hop-beer, with perhaps a little limejuice-
cordial here and there. My mistake, gentlemen, my
mistake ! You see, the bottles were rum, brandy, and
square-face bottles, which only shows how easily a man
can be misled, and how careful one should be before ac-
cusing sensible people of Booze.
As for blasphemousness, Coleridge himself speaks of
“ The bloody sun at noon,’' and who is to complain if they
speak of the sun that way all day long at Palmerston !
They are only following a great English classic. Besides,
what’s the use of language if you don’t make it a bit
picturesque ? The correct N.T. method of using language
is to start with a long-drawn howl like the hoot of a loco-
motive on a steep grade, thus—“ Hooooo !” This attracts
attention, and ensures a respectful silence and a fair
hearing for what you have to say.
As for blow’—well, De Rougemont was a Northern Ter-
ritory product, and I don’t think that he would have
been top-weight in any liars’ handicap there either. But
perhaps it is hardly fair to reproach them with De Rouge-
mont, as they all said up there that he was a liar.
As for the other matters in my article—well, no doubt
the mines will begin to pay, and the Northern Territory
rubies will turn out to be genuine, and the Dog Act will
be entirely suspended, and there will be a great tea-
meeting and mission to the blacks, with the G.R. in the
chair. All these things will happen after the next wet
season, or the next wet season but one.
As for the huge crane that fell into the water, I wish to
STRANGE TO SAY, HE HURRIED.
“My dear Pa. You needn’t hurry home ’cause
you think ma and me are lonely, ’cause Captain
Willis comes round every evening to keep ma com-
pany, and stays awfully late. They’re always
laughing about how small the gas-bills are since you
left, but won’t tell me the joke.—Your affectionate
daughter.”
amend that statement also. It didn’t fall at all, some-
body must have pushed it.
I think this handsome withdrawal of mine should re-
move any idea that I wished to wrong the N.T. They
are very hospitable people up there, especially the
buffalo-shooters Rees and Martin, and Lawrie, of Glencoe.
In the town, too, I could have swum in beer and square-
face if I had wanted to. But I don’t think that any
amount of beer and squareface should prevent a writer
from telling the truth in his articles, and that has made
me all the more anxious to correct the mistakes in my
first yarn. 1 trust that this will put matters on a satis-
factory footing.

Another comment on the Dungey-Bromley
case: W. T. Stead, in the “Maiden Tribute”
days, did wrong to prevent wrong—just as the
Melbourne ’tec. and the woman did —but Stead
was found guilty and sent to gaol, while ’tec. and
woman escape.

respondents with reference to the “ Albatross Wick.”
All agree that the comfort and cleanliness are well worth
the extra cost, which is more than saved by the lessened
quantity of oil consumed and in the labor saved in that
they “ require no trimming’.”*
