A WOMAN’S LETTER.
„ Sydney, April 4, 1899.
My Dear Myse—
Mr. Justice Stephen finds his health sufficiently
recovered to permit of his taking a return passage
to Sydney this month by R.M.S. Himalaya. This,
however, is not surprising, for Lord Beauchamp
has also booked per Himalaya, and the Stephens
nourish in vice-regal society better than in any
other. One of them once travelled to Sydney
accompanied by his son, in the same boat as Lord
Jersey. “Now I know three generations of
Stephen, quoth the (then) new Governor. Mr.
Jack Want will probably come in the same boat’
unless he desires to miss a good chance of putting
the Federal situation before the new Governor.
At a recent ‘At Home, ” given by one of the
emebnties of a certain Australian capital, the
officers of a visiting warship were present. Of
course a big crowd turned up to see the blue-
jackets, and the officers, desirous of assisting,
found themselves well employed in helping the
only maid the establishment boasted of to wash
up the two dozen cups and saucers with which she
was vainly endeavoring to supply 300 guests with
afternoon tea. But “all’s well that ends well,”
and it is doubtful if ever those officers have been
present at such a novel and original “At Home”
as the first they attended on Australian shores.
All Sydney’s wild anxiety to have its winter
frocks ready for the Easter A. J.C. meeting ended
m clouds of dust, and midsummer muslins on
Saturday. The flutter to get new garbing for the
Show, the Races, and the four Easter balls,
agitated the whole city last week. One large
drapery firm sold something like twenty imported
day-dresses in half-an-hour. But most of these
superior gowns stayed at home from the first day
of the meet. Heat and dust lent an unreasonable,
isolated look to the few that ventured out. You
pitied the wearers of the frocks, and, sartorially,
pity isn’t akin to love.
Strange how few women acknowledge Autumn,
and dress for it. Heavy cloths, reps, and cash-
meres, follow right on the heels of airy summer
fabrics. The one time of the ye:.r when the soft,
dull aesthetic dresses of the well-dressed woman in
novels, may be indulged in happily is insulted
with thick, cold weather garments, or tired
summer frocks.
In Saturday’s filmy dresses, the latest Madden
bride, Mrs. H. Osborne, snared the closest
attention. Her plump little person was gowned
in a mauve-colored flowery-silk muslin, made with
flowing panniers, and her grey felt hat was turned
up in front in the characteristic manner of her
sisters, and was trimmed with purple velvet, and a
sweeping feather or two. Another keenly-in-
spected bride was Mrs. Herbert Marks, in a pink
flowered muslin and a mauve hat decked with
bunches of lilac. This extremely pretty bride was
accompanied by her mother, Mrs. Pilcher, in a
brown foulard sprinkled with red flowers, and by
Dr. Herbert Marks, wearing quite an English
complexion. A graceful dress, that would send
many people into ecstasies, was Mrs. O ’Gorman’s
long-tailed black silk, appliqued with jewelled
cream lace, and lightened with a pink waistband
and smart pink medici collar. Mrs. Hugh Dixson,
junr., wore a cloth frock and velvet toque in the
new shade of Cuban red. This color is a hesitation
between purple and the good old magenta, and is
going to make itself felt during the coming season.
Mrs. Lovely crowned a grey frock, braided in
mauve, with a purple hat. A large fair-haired
living picture, now domesticated in Melbourne,
was another who defied temperature. Her bright
“gendarme-blue” cloth was about the most con-
spicuous frock on the lawn. An extensive
polonaise, covered with thick guipure, was finished
off with blue velvet sleeves and underskirt, and
topped with a wide black feather hat.
After all the talk about the new tea-room, it
was disappointing to come on a common asphalt
floor, hard white paint, and a record contrivance
for letting in draughts. This sublimated barn
still clings to the racecourse traditions about
catering. The rough-and-ready style of waiting,
the “social ” brand of the provisions, the durable
structure of the antique sandwiches—these have
all turned up again, in spite of the much-vaunted
‘ improvements.”
In the evening, the races went bodily to welcome
back the Broughs at the Theatre Royal. You saw
there all the same faces that had passed you on
the lawns in the afternoon, looking—well, night
works miracles. A great, gay, vivacious, much-
dressed circle overflowed into all the boxes and
the front stalls. Mrs. Stanley Mylius, in a black
sleeveless frock with diamonds and no gloves,
made a lovely centre for a big party in a lower
box. In the box above, Mrs. Bernard Wise’s girl-
ish profile peeped round the curtains. As for the
circle itself, they were all there —all the names
you know so well. Nobody with rings or mani-
cured fingers wore gloves. Spangled fans were
everywhere, and so were the long velvet cloaks,
made with loose sleeves and a satin lining, that
will sweep over well-bred evening gowns this
season. Mrs. Rennie, in the front row, wore
white satin with pink roses on the corsage. Also
m the front was Mrs. Harry Levy in blue.
By the way, the front row isn’t sought after so
frantically by men as by women. Looking round
at the Broughs’ first night I noticed that most of
the masculine faces at the inner edge of the circle
looked melancholy. The ordeal of sitting out a
play in the full sight of hundreds of restless eyes,
and, worse still, of having to gaze at hundreds of
human faces when the gas goes up, is hard on
those who don’t run a perpetual show. And, worse
still—but that is another question which you, dear
Myee, are not supposed to understand. Still, if
its a dry, hot night, you may be allowed to sym-
pathise, blindly, overhead in the dress-circle dur-
ing the prologue of “Sans-Gene.” “If Mrs.
Broutrh irons a shirt, I’ll say she can act.” This
was said by a Man. Strangely enough, a god was
smitten with the same idea. “If she’s a laun-
dress, why don’t she iron one of the boss’s shirts.”
To which the other god: “You don’t s’pose
Brough’s goin’ to have a shirt spoiled every night,
do you ? ”
To you, Myee, I own I would like to have
seen it done. The piquancy of watching a'shirt
ironed by Mrs. Brough would have added an in-
describable novelty to that rather tame bit of
handkerchief flattening. Besides, who knows ! !
It might have made shirt-ironing fashionable, and
think how that would have helped out the weary
existence of the bachelors who are always wailing
in a certain paper that they can’t marry because
the girl of the day can’t put a gloss on a shirt-
front.
Three beautiful cloaks in “Sans Gene” strike
very close to the dominant fashion-note of our
own day. They are long, ample, and fashioned in
velvet. The first is of cerise velvet bordered with
black fur that falls over to reveal a dull green
lining. Over the cerise a bold, square sort of
pattern is gorgeously worked in black and gold
embroidery. The second is a fresh, vivid apple-
green edged with ermine and lined with white
satin. Miss Temple covers Napoleon’s sister with
yet a, third striking cloak. An apricot plush em-
broidered with steel and gold in a pattern that
looked like bunches of grapes from the d.c. This
was lined with a lemon satin and bordered all
round with grey fur.
Few plays lean as heavily as “Sans-Gene” on
the acting of one woman. The piece is really
nothing else but Sans-Gene herself. On Saturday,
Mrs. Brough was in such a highly-strung condition
that it wouldn’t, be fair to take that night as every
night; but the impression she left on me was that
her method was just the good old novelist’s plan of
telling you all about a character on the first page.
You know the style. He was a very ambitious
man. He had a noble disposition. His nature was
generous to an extreme. And then, as everything
is set down on the outside, the inner man has no
necessity to show himself. Under all the vivacity
and violence, the gestures of the laundress, her way
of saying this and that, and moving, and laughing,
and slanging, and blazing out heroically, one
failed to see the definite woman who would give
blood and reality to the shadow.
Another impression from this play is the harsh-
ness of the English language when brought into a
French atmosphere. To hear the laundry-girls
address Sans GSne as “ Miss,” “Yes, Miss,” “No,
Miss,” to hear the inimitable Frenchwoman her-
self talk about her boots “ busting ,” and “lydies”
in up-to-date Cockney English, destroyed all illu-
sions about Sans Gene being a Frenchwoman of
Napoleon’s day.
Monday, the second day of the races was wet.
Hpwever, as Sydney Cup is always dressed for
beyond any other day of the Easter meeting,
women who had been keeping back their smartest
fripperies persisted in sporting them despite the
ruinous weather. The bright colors that used to
flash about the vice-regal box when the Hampdens
and their friends were there gave place to sombre
effects under the Darley regime. Lady Darley
was wearing black silk, like one in authority, with
a black velvet cape and a little black bonnet. Miss
Sylvia Darley made her reappearance after her long
illness in mauve—yes, the veto of many years has
been taken off the word, everyone uses it; and
a scheme is even on foot to set free another old
servant-galism, that got into disgrace through
being seen everywhere, the artless puce— and a
feather boa. Miss Kitty Darley and the Misses
Pearson, who were also in the V.R. box, left an
impression of dull fawns and greys that was not
peculiarly striking.
Many of the dresses in the members’ stand were
lovely schemes that never got a chance to unfold.
There was no lawn-walking; consequently the
long, eel skirts, that follow the figure without one
solitary gather or pleat at the back of the
waist-belt, and are so clammy and clinging on
a wet day that to walk in them is an
art—or an error—were not seen at their
worst. Mrs. W. B. Mitchell was less conspicuous
than usual in a grey green “ tailor made, ” and a
purple toque with a long shaded feather running
backwards. The two little Geisha sisters, Miss
Florence and Miss Beatrice Perry, suffered from the
unwholesome colors of their brick-red coats and
small white sailor hats with green straw linings
and green bands. Miss Wade-Brown looked
skilfully seasonable in a red toque, and smart little
red jacket over a plaid skirt. Miss Gillin’s
pale grey coat and skirt was a thing of charm,
with a turquoise hat, and an exceptional cut.
Miss Fosbery sported a piped purple cloth, with a
blue vest—a combination that many people
affected. Mrs. Burdekin in cigar-brown cloth,
and green velvet cape, joined in the plot to abolish
veils, and wore her brown fur and feather toque
without one.
They say this year’s N.S.W. Agricultural
Society’s show is the biggest for years. The open-
ing came off on Thursday, and was graced by the
acting-vice-regal party. Very few extra frills
were put on for this occasion. Mrs. John See,
wife of the society’s president, had a tea-party in
one of the refreshment-rooms, and there one saw
that the show was not extensively patronised by
snobility proper. In short, people don’t go to a
show without a motive,_ unless they come from the
country, where a cow is a motive for putting on
one’s best dress, and a ploughing-machine is a
motive, and a ride in a Sydney tram is quite a big,
enticing motive. Still, despite Nice people’s
reluctance to dress for anythin" so distinctly rural,
there were many prominent folk present. Mrs.
W. R. Wilson, from Melbourne, in a dull green
muslin and a big black picture hat; the pretty,
fresh-faced bevy of “Sam Hordern” girls, in
simple little summer frocks; Mrs. Harry Levy
in a French-grey tailor suit.
Madame Emily Sol dene is leaving Sydney next
month for London, and a big circle of friends are
at work on a matinee by way of a farewell benefit.
I hear that Mme. Soldene is to continue her racy
Evening News gossip in the shape of a “ London
Week-by-Week ” letter.
.The benefit of this week will be Pattie Browne’s
matinee at Her Majesty’s on Friday, April 7.
An alluring programme includes Pattie in the
letter-scene from “ The Country Girl,” the Geisha
cp. in the second act of “The Geisha,” Lottie
Moore, Dante, The Holloway Co., Chas. Kenning-
ham, and other attractions.
. A pretty young soprano, Miss Daisy Davies,
gives a concert, under Ministerial patronage, at
a 6 .X-M.C.A., on the evening of Thursday,
April 6.
-Paring Holy Week the impressive services at
ot. Mary’s R.C. Cathedral were attended by thou-
sands of worshippers, and the regular members of
congregation declared that whenever they went to
the big church, no matter how early, the seats
were packed with Protestants—the' pew-holders
and supporters of the church having to stand or sit
wherever there was room for them. This is indeed
a strange cause of complaint from the R.C’s., ad-
mittedly the world’s strongest proselytising body ;
but they probably guess that it is the sensuous,
and not merely the devotional, side of the heretics’
natures that drives them to the one true fold
during Lent and Easter.
Yet another (very youthful) pair have fallen
victims to the romantic atmosphere of the Uni-
versity. The engagement is announced of Miss
-Banny Marr and Mr. Jack (son of the Q. Govt
geologist). Miss Marr will take the B.A. and Mr.
Jack the B.E. degree on the 22nd inst.
The wedding of Miss Dolly Wilson and Mr.
rank Russell, barrister-at-law, is to take place
about 20th inst. Miss Wilson is the eldest
daughter of Mrs. Bowie Wilson who died last
year and is a very clever girl. Mr. Russell is one
ot the few University students who attended
evening lectures and gained a B.A. degree in three
years -the average length of time for evening
students being five years.
All the Sydney Lancers are not made of very
stern stuff, judging by the heartrending accounts
that have already reached their friends and rela-
tives of the miseries of the voyage. One young
hero, who protested that he was feeling more
homesick than seasick, begged to be put off the
boat at Albany so that he could get back as soon
as possible to his own dear home, and not go fur-
ther on the way to that nasty Old England. To
his infinite distress he was told that he was under
a three-years’ engagement, and that he was bound
to see the thing through once he had started.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ ‘Belle of New York’ drew such an Easter
Saturday crush at Melb. Princess’s that there
wasn’t even leaning-room left back of the d.c., and
late-comers thanked their stars if the person
standing in front was short enough to be seen over.
Indeed, small men were sought after more than
they liked in such sweltering weather. A little ’un
could be easily located in front row of standers by
his rear-guard of belated women, peering over
each shoulder and dodging his head.
“ Local youth, ’tis said, especially those too
young to have been warned by last Yankee in-
vasion, have been quite unnerved all week by
anticipations of the ‘ Belle, ’ and accompanying
galaxy of American beauty. When big, fair,
buxom Louise Royce, first sample shown, frisked
on, dressed for her 9th wedding in white satin
princess-robe, she tripped and fell heavily, poor
soul! on her front corsage. 11l omen. And bad
luck sat tight, for the programme dragged, and, as
Girard refrains in one of his songs, ‘A cloud came
o’er ’ things in general.
“The fallen heroine aforesaid, like her col-
leagues, changed her frock every two minutes or
s .°\ But, as a rule, her things are no more de-
liciously chic than anyone else’s, though her black
gauze over green silk, worn with terra-cotta hat,
has no cause to feel down-tailed. Belle Bucklin,
as petite Parisienne ‘Fifi,’ is like a leedle Deutch
gals rather than a Frenchwoman, with her flaxen
thatch, wide mouth, and well-nourished, inexpres-
sive arms. Mostly, she favors short skirts, begin-
ning with white muslin as a candy-charmer off a
wedding cake, and passing on to something peachy,
flounced, and fleecy, in which she trills a dance
rather prettily re ‘La Gay Parisienne,’ in com-
pany with other pinky-lilac poseuses.
“Judging by ‘ Belle ’ toilettes—lilac, lavender,
petunia and purple are rampant in New York,
with yellow thrown in to break the monotony.
‘Over thar,’skirts flounced to the hip join on to
fussy belt-bodices with transparent sleeves and
chemisette. A wearer, whose figure-curves merit
publicity, affects princess robe, or polonaise of
princess-cut, never forgetting lashings and lavings
of frills and flounces. On the Atlantic slope, lacy
lovers’ knots, muslin ‘ bugs, ’ and similar applique
fal-lals have sunk, seemingly, to the level of
Bowery she-toughs.
“Local Salvation Sarahs should take a costume
wrinkle or two from the ‘Belle ’ in her Hallelujah
days. Red satin bonnet-lining, glimpse of scarlet
underskirt, auburn plait a-hanging down her
back. As Pa Bronson’s heiress, the ex-devil
thumper (Louise Hepner) starts an upper-crust
rescue league, uniformed quite deliciously. Said
regimentals, in scribe’s opinion, are worth all the
rest of the wardrobe put together. The Belle
herself sports vivandiere coat, and knee-high skirt
of the richest, sheeniest, ivory satin extant. And
such a tip-top pair of glove-fit, w'hite kid gaiters,
with cunning loops and buttons on the off-side.
Long white gloves, mass of sulphur roses under
bonnet brim, white and gold military staff.
“All the same, judging by data supplied, it’s
easier to be Belle of New York than of Melbourne.
To capture the latter position, a candidate who
can only describe herself truthfully as a fine figure
of a girl with a handsome head of hair, must
indeed get up early—indeed, she needs to get up
the week before.
“Asthmatic and hay-feverish subjects in the
d.c. on the ‘ Belle’s ’ first night were to be pitied.
One sniffed in violet powder and Fuller’s earth
with every breath. It must have been shovelled
on at the theatre, because home-layers would have
brushed off en route. Some women are whales for
powder. Wild horses couldn’t drag them past a
gratuitous supply. You’ll see them contorting
themselves to reach down their spines with some
bald-headed, repulsive, all-things-to-all-men puff.
And it isn’t as if critical male brutes would get a
chance of spying out the swarthiness of their back-
bones, ’cause floury females always wear their
things so tight that when they faint, as they’re
fond of doing, you couldn’t wedge so much as a
remedial watch-key down their backs.
“A local Sawbones tells me that he once wrote
with his finger in the starch lying thick on the
nut-brown shoulders of an unconscious grass-
widow, ‘ Whited sepulchre! ’ I hae ma doots, for
she was his best patient, so he says.
“ Gallery inmates were a shade skittish that
first night. They tired of Baseballer Don in his
rdle of a crazy German, a daft stranger in a
strange land. Mummer’s dialect and quaint
Teutonic simplicity were realistic, but there was
an overdose of the ‘polite lunatic,’ as of every-
thing else.
“Oscar Girard, as Pa Bronson, is like a dried-
up Yank edition of Gov. Hampden. No novel
dress wrinkles to be picked up from the plumage
of these stage ’Merican johnnies. Millionaire 8.,
I noticed, moistened his fingers to work on new
gloves. But better not adopt this as ‘ real N’York
style ’ till it transpires whether the old man
merely acted so as a reminder that he began life
with half a dime.
“The irreproachable dames who have hitherto
managed Melbourne Women’s Hospital in the
interests of male doctors, and who took an annual
opportunity to despise the applications of femald
doctors, are fairly beaten at last. Two or three
of their trousered idols cleared out of the show in
a hurry, and when they advertised for a resident
medical officer for the infirmary department, the
only candidate was Dr. Violet May Plummer,
resident surgeon at Melb. Hospital. So the com-
mittee pulled a long, sour face and told Dr. Violet
that she would do. They might have added that
they couldn’t help themselves.
“That well-known journalist, Mr. Bob Burt,
erstwhile of the Agk, lately returned to Mel-
bourne from Queensland, so weak from phthisis
that he could do no work. Mr. David Syme has
sent him up to his fine station near Lilydale, in
clear mountain air, and bidden him stay there and
get well if he can.
“ The illness of Melbourne’s popular Mayoress,
the too energetic Mrs. M‘Eacharn, which began
with a cinder in the eye, is proving much more
serious than was at first anticipated. Partial
paralysis of the face set in, followed by erysipelas,
and the three doctors at present in attendance
take a grave view of the case.
“ The news that a highly-connected N.S.W.
lady had become suddenly insane was received by
all shades of Melbourne society with very real ex-
pressions of regret.
ours affectionately,

awarded the first prize and gold medal for the

PERSONAL ITEMS.
The Bulletin wants to see Premier Reid
appoint John Norton to N.S.W. Marine Board.
There are seven editors elected to the new
Q. Parliament. At least, seven of them say they
are editors.
Mr. Haddon has slipped back into the
Argus editorial chair at the first chance (Mr.
Willoughby’s holiday).
Frank Farnell expects to be swamper
when the new appointments to N.S.W. Leg.
Council are announced.
Among the judges of the pigs at N.S.W.
Agricultural Show was more than one Hebrew.
Chorus of pork : “ Fancy meeting you t ”
Queenslanders, blow hot and cold. The
scheme so much talked about to give the late T.
J. Byrnes a monument is as dead as Caesar.
Henry Austin, wool-broker, brother of
England’s poet-laureate, is, according to Minis-
terial whisper, to be appointed to N.S.W. Leg.
Council.
Q. Premier Dickson’s great ambition is to
be knighted. He is now a C.M.G., and when he
gets the coveted bauble he may give politics best
—if politics don’t drop him before then.
Essence of Q. Worker cartoon on Premier
J. R. Dickson, the ■ politician who’s dead —
Strange that “J.R.D.” should represent both
such a poor statesman and such good whisky !
Little Cowley, speaker of the last two Q.
Parliaments, may be looking for a job shortly.
Cowley is fretful and peevish, and, moreover, he
fell foul of Phil]) last session, so Joey Bell,
son of Joshua, is a likely candidate.
The relationship to Lord Beauchamp of
the Dowager Lady Raglan, bound for Sydney
with the new Governor, is the peculiar one of
having been the second wife of Lord Raglan,
whose first wife was Beauchamp’s aunt.
The Bulletin was glad to receive by the
English mail a letter from B. E. Minns, the well-
known Australian artist, announcing his progress
towards convalescence. He was still in a private
ward at Guy’s Hospital, slowly recovering from
the effects of an operation.
Vic. Commercial Travellers’ Association
has decided not to offer another prize for the Most
Beautiful Bagman at the annual picnic. The
lovely creature who won it last year has endured
much subsequent unhappiness through the taunts
of his plain, ordinary contemporaries, and the
association feels itself responsible for the sad de-
terioration of his witching smile.
The grandfather of S.A.’s new Governor
had a queer ad venture once in Russia. Grandpa
Tennyson was dining with Lord St. Heliers,
Bull’s Minister at St. Petersburg, when the topic
of the strangling of the inad Czar Paul came
up. It should be remembered that Bona-
parte and Paul had arranged simultaneously
to invade India, but when Paul’s mania
became too pronounced and he was
strangled, Bonaparte declared in Paris
Moniteur that Britain had engineered
the assassination With reference to
this statement, the Tennyson in ques-
tion thoughtlessly remarked “ that it
was perfectly well-known that Count
So-and-So .murdered the Emperor! ”
A 400-ton silence fell on the company,
for amongst the guests was the man
referred to. After dinner Lord St.
Heliers gave Tennyson the “office ” to
clear out for his life, which he did—
travelling in obscure ways for weeks
until he reached a Crimean village. Once
in three months an English Embassy
courier used to pass that way, and, with
his aid, Tennyson hoped to escape. The
courier, a drunkard, dropped his des-
patches, which Tennyson picked up, and
the courier in gratitude smuggled him
through a frontier town, and eventually Ten-
nyson made his way to England.
One of the unpublished yams of Vic.
Black Wednesday relates to the reinstate-
ment of Capt. Standish as Chief Commis-
jioner of Police. Standish was included in the
Civil Service sweep-out, but he merely smiled
and told the late George Bowen, the Gov. of the
day, that he expected to be reinstated at once.
Fond of “ bachelor life ” as Bowen was he did
not hesitate, and Standish’s reappointment was
gazetted. The keen-eyed captain was, no doubt,
choke-full of valuable in formation about Bowen.
Lord Beauchamp’s family motto—“ Strong
through Faith”—is suitable for any person who
complacently assumes the position of Himperial
figurehead. Strong through Faith—in himself.
“ Born to Self-Assurance,” or “ Believing in Beau-
champ,” would be equally impressive.
N.S. W. Chief-Justice Darley lias held his
present law office 13 years. His predecessors :
C.-J. Forbes sat from 1824 (the date of his arrival
in Sydney—he was appointed the year before) till
1837 —13 years; C.-J. Dowling, 8 years; C.-J.
Stephen, 29 years, and C.-J. Martin i 3 years.
One other black mark against the coming
Gov. Tennyson. He wound up a letter acknow-
ledging a public meeting’s congratulations on his
appointment with the words “Fare you well.” A
man who writes like that nearly always goes on
stilts ; and his seem to be particularly high ones.
The name of Burgess is unlucky in Aus-
tralasia. Butler passed for some years under it,
another Burgess was unjustly arrested for the
Gatton murders, a Burgess was the boss Maunga-
tapu murderer ; and last week a Burgess in a fit
of insanity murdered his wife and two children
in S. A.
Broken Hillionaire David James, who has
had almost enough of the turf, now developes an
unexpected desire to fly his colors in Parliament.
He has his eye on Light (Kapunda), where he is
principal in a big foundry. James owes his
“ rise ” to the fact that he happened to be in the
district with his team when the Hill was pegered.
Dick Ireland, Q.C., in Melb.,
had a knack of winking at the jury
while his antagonist orated. One morn-
ing Dick turned up depressed in court,
and said, “My jury-eye is out of
order.”
Of course such things are never
done nowadays, but in the period of
Vogel’s scramble for office politicians
grabbed things as they came and asked
few questions. Once when Julius was
holding on by a hair, one of liis faithful
supporters in M.L. House of Represen-
tatives was a Canterbury member with
a drouth on him. His drouth generally
came on him worst at times of political
excitement, and the result was that just
when his vote was most wanted he
would be most unanimously absent.
On one critical occasion Vogel locked
his supporter, with the latter’s consent,
in a top-floor room to keep him dry till
the division. But Oppositionists who
heard of it got at a skylight and silently
lowered into the room at the end of a
string one bottle of whisky, one of
brandy and one of beer. The Canter-
bury man took these blessings as they
canie. Half-an-hour later some more
Oppositionists heard of how Vogel was
saving up the beer stricken member,
and they at once got the same inspira-
tion. So they also risked their necks
on the roof, and having mistakenly
broken open the wrong sky-light they
lowered a vast quantity of miscellan-
eous refreshments into a room where
some clerks were working. The latter
gave 16 cheers as they saw the liquor
descending, and proceeded to drink it
there and then. Vogel found his mem-
ber incapable, and in his wrath tried to
carry him under his arm and have his
vote counted, but the Canterbury man
was too far gone, and Julius dropped
him under the table in disgust.
Inspector Urquhart, who has had charge
of the Gatton murder enquiry, has been head of
the Criminal Investigation Department in the
Northern capital for about two years past. His
qualification for the position is somewhat unusual.
He began life at sea, in the mercantile marine ;
was afterwards engaged in telegraph-line con-
struction in N.Q. ; was then in the native police,
and had charge of the police cutter in Torres
Straits. After a short connection with the metro-
politan police as sub-inspector he was promoted
to the charge of the detective department.
MORE PIN-PRICKS.
USHMAN: “Bey pardon, young lady, but could
you kindly assist a poor fellow ?”
SASSIETY LADY : “ No, my good man : I never
give to beggars in the street.”
BUSHMAN: “ I don't mean that, miss; I only want
the loan of a hairpin to put down my pipe.”

(.£2000), with little to do, and J. H. Want, Q.C.,
Attorney-General (,£1820), with nothing to do,
are in the big smoke.

“ Maorilander ” : Re that yarn (Bulletin
25/3/’99) about Sullivan, the Maungatapu (pron.
“ Mokotap ”) murderer, seeking gaol-protection in
Victoria, and afterwards dying in a N.S.W. hos-
pital. Have always understood, and never heard
any other story in M.L., that Sullivan died of
cancer over ’Frisco way. As to his seeking pro-
tection, writer has frequently heard from his own
people, who were among the searchers for the
bodies of the victims, and living in Nelson at the
time, that Mr. Kempthorne (Kempthorne, Prosser
and Co.), whose brother was among the murdered,
had repeatedly said he would shoot Sullivan
on sight, if opportunity offered. Kempthorne was
notoriously a man of his word, and it is known
that, when the miscreant was pardoned (Queen’s
evidence), Govt, had to keep him in gaol till he
could be smuggled off in an Amci ica bound vessel.
A present Australian gaol-
photographer once applied for
the triple offices of photo-
grapher, flagellator, and hang-
man. A pity his application
was refused. Granting it would
have saved the brutalising of at
least two alleged human beings.
Vogel, like all leading
Maoriland politicians, died poor.
He was so “stony” when he
came to M.L. to re-enter poli-
tics in ’B4 that his enemies
alleged he borrowed .£lOOO from
Stout to pay his expenses. Stout
was a generous man, and, so far,
the story looked plausible, but
its improbability lay in the fact
that Robert of Shetland was
none too rich himself in those
days. Vogel wasn’t poor, like
so many other M.L- politicians,
through any neglect of his own
interests. He made money, as
Colonial Treasurer and Agent-
General, out of the handling of
the huge loans which the pro-
vince put through in his time
(some of Vogel’s demands for
commission were a public scan-
dal), but he spent freely on
lavish entertainments, such as
no Australasian Premier nowa-
days would dream of, and his
card-parties were at one time
notorious in Wellington. He
was a good loser and prompt
payer—and that is why Julius
Vogel was thankful in his old
age for a paltry .£3OO a-year as
Maoriland Financial Adviser in London.
The Mr. Wood who recently introduced a
deputation to N.S.W. Mines Minister Cook is
no less a person than the able Harrie Wood,
N.S.W. Under-Secretary for Mines for nearly a
quarter of a century. Mr. Wood is now mining
“on his own.” Forty-five years ago he worked
over the better part of Victoria. He originated
Ballarat School of Mines, and held several official
positions under the Vic. Govt.
Some of the Vic. Labor-members are noted
for the fact that they never, by any chance, miss
a free feed, a Parliamentary excursion, a Govt.
House shivoo, a Mayoral invite, anything, in fact,
where the drink and vittles are at all passable. It
is somewhat to Trenwith’s credit that he seldom
appears at such affairs, and, when he does, he often
departs as empty and as dry as he came. Tren-
with may be nobbled some day through his ambi-
tion, but never through his stomach.
Once again a N.S.W. Ministerial crisis
occurs when Attorney-General Want is holiday-
ing. Every Ministry witli which Mr. Want has
worked experienced a crisis, some of them more
than once, while he was out of the provinoe in
pursuit of health and happiness.
The trial of Michael Kelly, charged with
murdering his inamorata, Maria Flynn, was set
down for sth April, the day fixed for the execu-
tion of Briggs, who slew his sweetheart, Maggie
Dutt. Kelly and Briggs were confined in the
same gaol (Darlinghurst), and at 10 o’clock (the
hour appointed for the choking of Briggs) Kelly
was to step out into the court next door, to stand in
the dock where Briggs was sentenced to death, to
be tried before the same Judge. Some men need
all their nerve.
W. H. Groom has again been returned for
Toowqomba, which, despite all opposition, he has
uninterruptedly represented for 40 years —ever
since Separation, in fact. .Various suggestions
have been made as to the secret of his success,
but it is undoubtedly the individual favors and
assistance which his experience enables him to
confer among his constituents. His political
record is indeed remarkable, seeing that since he
first entered politics a generation has passed in
Toowoomba, and another has taken its place.
Claude Vautin, well - known consulting
metallurgist and director of a number of Westra-
lian and other co’s,' has been committed for trial
at the Old Bailey on a charge of having exten-
sively defrauded London stockbrokers. It is
alleged that he ran off to America with about
£20,000. When in Far N.Q., years ago, Vautin,
then a handsome young fellow, the idol of the
women and the envy of the men, was mining-
partner of “ Jim ” Parker, who disappeared
mysteriously from Sydney not long ago, and is
believed to be dead. Vautin was a very expert
metallurgist, and was well-known in Australia in
connection with the Newbery-Vautin process.
When in Opposition, Julius Vogel was his
own whip, and a'good one, too, though rather
reckless. Stafford’s Ministry was in power at the
end of ’72 and the beginning of ’73, but Vogel was
only one or two votes short of being strong enough
to regain office. He proposed his no-confidence
motion, and “The Smiler,” a dreary, giggling
nonentity from down South, became the centre of
attraction, he being the only man who had not de-
clared himself. The division was reached after
fierce debate ; “ the Smiler ” was the very last
member to leave the chamber for the telling
lobbies, and, after much consideration, he walked
into the Opposition camp. Vogel won by two
votes. Seen shortly afterwards, an angry Govt,
man said to him: “ Look here, Vogel, is it true that
you offered that beast ‘ the Smiler ’ a portfolio for
his vote?” “Perfectly,” replied the Hebrew.
“ And do you mean to give it to him? ” “H —m,
that’s another question,” said VogeL “ The
Smiler’s ” portfolio never came.

“ It's so sad to be a young widow.”
“ And it’s so common to be a grass one.”
N.S. W. Lieut.-Governor and Chief Justice
Darley, at St. Patrick’s Day banquet, boasted of
his Irishism. He comes from one of the Toriest
families in Dublin. His father was an attorney
and a clerk in the Court of Chancery; his grand-
father, High Sheriff of Dublin in the time of the
1798 insurrection, and, in later life, chief P.M.
and alderman of the city, of which he was Lord
Mayor in 1808-9. The alderman's father was a
merchant of. the.city. Judge Darley’s uncle, still
alive, was called to the Irish Bar in 1830, the year
in which the N.S, W. Chief Justice was born, and
has not long retired from a County Court Judge-
ship.

Aboriginalities.
“ Oamiro ” : In the “ good times ” about
SO shearers, fresh from a “ cut-out,” struck
a lone shanty out-back, and were soon seated
round the long table in the eating-room. The
shouting started, and went round once. Mid-way
round the second time Brown didn’t respond, and
it was his “shout.” His neighbors shook him—
he was lying back on his chair, head on chest.
They remarked that Brown had got drunk “ very
sudden.” Then an observant shearer found that
Brown wasn’t drunk—only dead. It was resolved
that they couldn’t spoil the fun. So they took
the dead man’s cheque out, the corpse shouted
and they drank his health, and the ball rolled on
till Brown was stiff as well as defunct. His
cheque was for 30 odd sovs. !

At a country race-meeting a horse fell, and
his jockey was severely hurt. A subscription was
hawked round for his benefit, and the canvassers
called upon a local publican, by name Melchise
dech Bumblestein. “ Vhat ! ” he exclaimed,
wrathfully. “Subscribtion! Eferv day is a
subscribtion ; I vas tired too much of it. Vhy !
I haf a poy in Sydney with some skin knocked off
his nose—in a fight! I vill gif you his initials—
Moses Bumblestein. There !go and get him a
subscribtion. Vhat! ’

“T.D. ” : Was standing at door of a Dubbo
pub., t’other day, when a well-known local abori-
ginal was waiting about for the chance of a drink.
A small boy, running by, suddenly stooped and
picked up something from the asphalt, whereupon
Jacky: “ Wot dat you pick ’em up, eh?” “A
shillun’,” replied the youngster. “Well, you
juth pik ’em down agen, I puth ’em dere jus’ to
try you honest. ” The boy rendered-up, and Jacky
went jubilantly to the bar.

Dear Bulletin. —Re “Two Westralians
have a wager as to whether or not poisonous
snakes bite their prey with poison-fangs before
swallowing.” No Australian snake kills its prey
with its poison-fangs. The fangs are always used
by the snake to hold its prey, and when in that
service are not unfrequentlv broken ; but in no
case is poison injected into the wounds the fangs
may inflict. In some six or seven different ways I
have proved this. Nature never blunders. An
Australian snake can only bite with deadly effect
four times; frequently not more than twice. Now
a hungry snake will swallow at a feed from eight
to fourteen mice ; and if it had to bite and poison
each of these its poison would be exhausted and
itself rendered harmless. But will anyone ven-
ture to try a bite from an Aust. venomous snake
after it has dined off even 14 mice ? I for one
shall not. —H. J. M'Cooey.

“ Cec. P.” : The irony of the drought,
Recently stopped at squatter’s place near Nyngan.
Remarked at dinner on good quality of the mutton.
“That sheep,” said my host, “came from Home-
bush ! ” The man, with scores of thousands of
sheep, sending 300 miles for his meat! Chaff-
feeding of stock is common. Enormous numbers
so fed on Cannonbar and by Meat Preserving Co.
at Nyngan. Average cost (hearsay) 3d. per head
a week. Some talk of using wheat.
• * * •
“K.K.”: Re “ bushies’ ” bedroom ar-
rangements. In the old country, where family
has only one room for children to sleep in, a
double-decked bed is rigged up. Lower deck is
boarded, up one side, and upper deck closed on
opposite side, so that each storey is entered from
different side and occupied by different sex.
Decency, however, is, after all, only a luxury.

“S. Snell”: Entirely disagree with the
<<IVT „ , . . J , -jvyr i
“ Mac. who in a recent par. declares the Mongol
of real life to be an earnest purveyor of vege-
tables who never cracks a joke m the whole course
of Ins existence. This is the mistake of the per-
son who knows Ah Cat only when on business
bent. The common Chow is often a gay and fes-
tive cuss given to frantic horseplay and immoder-
ate laughter. As a youngster I saw much of
Chinamen in their camps at Ballarat, Bendigo,
Clunes, Ores wick, Dunolly, St. Arnaud, and other
big Mongolian centres, and the sight of a score or
so of adult Oonfuciana performing maniacal
monkey tricks in the vicinity of their gardens and
shrieking like schoolboys was not infrequent. The
Chinese joke is usually practical, and often turns
on the torture of animals, but nobody who has
observed the Chow in the lump will deny its
existence. Used to see much of the Mongols at a
camp near the old Bonshaw, Ballarat, and there
the favorite Sunday morning entertainment con-
sisted in baiting a fowl with crackers. The
wretched hen was secured by one leg, and crackers
were thrown at her until she gave up the ghost.
It takes a long time to kill a robust pullet in this
way, but throughout the proceedings the heathen
raged with exceeding glee. The impression that
the Chinaman is kind to animals is due to his
tender devotion to his horse, or his pig—animals
which represent hard cash, and the value of which
is deteriorated by ill-usage. The baited hen was
always eaten.
* * * *
It was in the sixties, when squatters “ of
the old sort ” still existed. It had been a grand
season, and the wool-yield was phenomenal, so
from his far-away Queensland station Duncan
Chisholm started for Sydney on “a bender.” On
the second or third day out he got to a quiet little
place—a pub., a store, and a blacksmith’s. That
was the lot. Duncan had his tea, and then a
whisky or two—and the fun began. He smashed
everything in the bar, and all the crockery he
could see in the kitchen, and then quietly sat
down on the verandah and demanded “ the bill.”
It was presented—.£l2—and he gave a cheque for
it with the pleased smile of a man who was having
a good time. Two days later he arrived in Too-
woomba, and broke the Sabbath evening calm by
a demand for dinner and a dozen boiled eggs.
When they arrived he asked the waiter and
generally-useless: “James, were you ever a
cricketer?” To which the unfortunate James
innocently replied, “Oh, dear, yes, Mr. Chisholm:
I was longstop to our first eleven for three years.’’
“ Then stop that! ” shouted Chisholm, seizing an
egg, and shying it at James, whom it hit
right on the cheek; “ and that, and that, and
that, and that!” as egg after egg spattered itself
over James. And after the eggs—forks, spoons,
cups, saucers, cruets, one after the other went
hurtling through the atmosphere, some sticking in
the panelling, and some wrecking the windows
and the pictures. James fled, covered with
egg, and left the storm to rage as it might. Dun-
can demolished the chairs, mirrors, chandeliers,
tore down the mantelpiece by main force, and
even threw most of the carpet out of the window,
and, when it was all over, sat down again, quiet
as a lamb, and called for “the bill.” .£4l was its
amount, but he drew a cheque for £45, the £4 to
be divided amongst the servants. He seems to
have got safely through Brisbane, and the end was
in Sydney. He was known there, as also were his
habits. At the Royal, at which he stayed, a big
public dinner was to take place. It was an invi-
tation affair. Chisholm wished to go, and asked
the head-waiter to procure him the needful. The
waiter did his best, but Chisholm was met with a
refusal. He did not say much, but he thought.
All was ready for the dinner—three long tables
were set, with choice flowers and fine cut-glass—
and the guests were assembling, when suddenly
an almighty smash was heard in the dining-room,
Chisholm had got (unknown to the waiters) a stout
needle and thread and sewn all the tablecloths
together, and, when everything was ready and
the waiters had gone to fetch the soup, he burst
in, got hold of the end of the cloth, and drew
everything clashing to the floor. That cost him
between "£lOO and -£2OO, which he at once paid
and then he swung back again to the old home-
stead, quite happy. Chisholm was a squatter of
the good old school. He is dead now, and joy-
“illy smashing things m a brighter sphere,

“ V. 8.” on a droving subject:—
In camp at night one restless bullock can cause im-
mense trouble. Curious that if any beast in the mob
considers it his bounden duty to be restless he always lies
on the outside. He gets up, generally at dead of night,
stretches himself, twists his tail, and if you don’t go up
and keep speaking to him (in order to take his attention)
he will walk up to his nearest neighbor and give him a
friendly prod, either in the ribs or the small of the back ;
this always brings the prodded beast to his feet. He in
turn goes through the stretching performance, and gives
his neighbor a prod. In this wav, with each fresh riser
putting in a friendly prod, the whole mob
is soon brought to its feet. If a bullock
seems restless, keep your eye on him.
As soon as he gets up, go and talk to
him ; if this is done he soon lies down
again, leaving the rest of the mob
A GOOD PERFORMER.
MR. CITYBRED : “ You say your horse is fast. Has he any performances?"
MR. BACKBLOCKS : “ Waal, yes. He killed a man up North, and throw'd Yalter Jim at the

undisturbed. Apropos, at night you can always ted
when a bullock lies down. As soon as he settles he draws
a long breath, and gives forth a sort of human sigh on
a large scale, audible in the stillness for a couple of hun-
dred yards.

“Scot'y” : Even in the bush exists the
desire for titles. A jackeroo, left temporarily in
charge of a station, alters the signature of trespass
notice to “Nincom Poope, sub-manager.” The
manager is now “ superintendent,” on the stations
of one large grindicate. The storeman (,£52 per
an.) is “book-keeper.” There are also “in
charge ” and “ second in charge ” of sheep (17/6 a
week). Slushy is “cook’s assistant”—“wood
and water joey ” is “ superintendent’s yard-man.”
The milk and cowboy is “ dairyman ” (10/- a
week). Same in the towns. The bank-clerks are
“bankers,” the herdsman is “ common ranger,”
the bum is “sheriff.”
» * * *
“Kink”: The W. A. goldfields’ black-
fellow buries his dead after a fashion of his own.
Defunct is placed in a shallow hole
and loosely covered with dirt; two
horseshoe-shaped mounds about a
foot high are scraped up around
him, open ends of horseshoes point-
ing towards sunrise. Tops of mounds
are thickly planted with small
sticks, giving a potato-patch ap-
pearance.

“ Aleck ” : Talking about the
appetite of emus. I had a friend
who was farming furthest out in
Western N.S.W. He had
bought the newest thing in
harvesters, and, having
carted it out there, had
to leave it out unguard-
ed. The thing was pulled
to pieces, and nearly
every scrap of ironwork
removed. Well, it was
an amazing mystery to
us all, till we heard that
a mob of emus had passed
in the night. Ten of us
saddled up, and started out
after the flock. We hunted
them for three days, and shot
every head. Then we opened them
up, collected all the missing iron-mongery, and,
in a week, we had that harvester together again,
and working at her best gait. When the emus
up there couldn’t get iron any other way, they
would steal the shoes off the horses.

“T.G.”: Near Dubbo, lately came on
Chinaman and horse on river-bank. Horse stand-
ing on three legs, while Chow walked round him
making a row like to a cockatoo cracking corn.
Said John to me—“ You please tellee me what
matta my holsee?” “Why, John, his leg is
broken.” John looked thunderstruck. “By cli,”
he said, “bloken! Just fancee ! I ownem that
holse fi’ year an’ he nevva do that befoh ? ”

“ A Working Miner ” writes from Marble
Bar, Westralia:—
I have much plasere in riting to you the way things is
carried on up here. If a native will not sign on again
after serveing all there life time with there masters on
Bread and water and beaten about all roads like a dog
the squarter will wait untell his Brother squarter is on
the Bench and have them trid before him and they get 6
months to consider weather they will go to work for a
White Boss well it is this way it is realy to Bad for any
niger to work in under some of these White masters up
here it is one of the Best countrys inunder the sun they
can allways find food all the year around and they
are of no expence to the Govenerment unless the
police have there hand gresed to go and run some
poor unfortunet nigger down that is been going
with his Boss’s gin
and he will get
charged with sheep
spearing and the squarters have all the station
.to back him up in what he says. He is brought
into town and put into the cell . . . they are put
in the wittness or prisoners Box and when they get
in that they do not no what to do when they get there
they walk around in the Box like a dog they want to set
down on the floor in the Box and they are made to under
stand not to set down they want to set on the rails and
there that frightened he dose not no what is going to
happen he thinks he will be hung there and then all they
ask him do you want to ask your master anything, the
native cannot understand one word of white lingo and he
is packed off 2 years hard labour. I have put it on paper
the way in which 3 natives were tried at our last court
Day. I can tell you this much that there is many a man
would loose the number of his mess only the natives find
them and report. Dear Editor try and do some thing for
the poor nigs.

It doesn’t take long to fix up a township
nowadays when a nugget is stumbled over in the
West. Lately, someone found a 30oz. piece at
Wollubar, 27 miles from Kalgoorlie. There was
a rush ; and a man who had some sheep out that
way joined in with the animals, an axe, a knife,
DROUGHT TIME.—ON A DRY TRACK.
" Don't stir it up, Scotty !”
and a water-bag. Same evening there was a
butcher’s shop built of boughs, and a blood-stained
monopolist offering chops at Is. a lb. —the begin-
ning, probably, of the Wollubar Chilled Meat and
Fresh Food Supply Co. And (who knows?) per-
haps a knighthood in a coming boom-time.

fully away and went to consult the leading
local lawyer. “ No, I won’t touch any case with
Daly in it,’’ said the man of law, who was in the
swim. “ He’s got a murderous push behind him,
and I’d be in fear of my life ever after.” With a
look of sickly terror the agent returnod to his
hotel, out of which he firmly declined to stir for
five days. Then one of the conspirators offered
to seek out Daly, and try and square the
thing if the agent was willing to part. lhe
latter agreed, and a meeting of the pair was
arranged to take place outside the Stock Ex-
change that night, the agent to be strongly
escorted in case of trouble. A hurried search was
made around the town to find a man to represent
Daly, and a burly miner was at length secured for
half-a-sov.—his part being duly rehearsed. Busi-
ness was suspended on the Fjxchange that night,
and half the town rolled up to see the fun. Daly,
followed by the finest selection of toughs in the
district, met the agent at the appointed time and
demanded a “tenner” to say no more about it.
The agent’s supporters agreed that this was a fair
thing, and he had no alternative but to “fork
out.” Then they all adjourned to the nearest
hotel and “did the tenner in,” and after the
fortieth beer the matter somehow leaked out. By
the early train next morning there was a passenger
who never wants to see anybody named Daly
again.
* * * *
Recent instance of black-tracking. Beside
the water (a sea-inlet) stood a mortgaged house.
Mortgagee had threatened foreclosure on a certain
date, a day or two prior to which the house,
which was empty but insured, was totally burnt.
Next day owner directed police attention to the
indent of a boat’s bow on beach, and footmarks
apparently leading up to site of house. A black
tracker was put on, and he traced the footprints
to the ruins and back to the beach. Then he re-
turned to station and said to sergeant, “ Him
catehum quick! That him tracks,” pointing to
some footprints in the sand, where the owner of
the burnt house had walked across the yard about
an hour before. Result—owner awaiting trial.

They still debate
how Bendigo got its
name ; and, as time
passes, the difficulty of
deciding will no doubt
increase. The late
Richard Grice (Grice,
Sumner) wrote over 20
years back that “ it was
named by Tom Myers
(Heape and Grice’s
overseer) about 1841.
Tom was a bit of a dab
with his fists, and a
great admirer of the
boxer Bendigo; hence
the name.” And that
is about the best bit of
evidence available to
date.

Dear Bulletin. —Recently saw a swarm of
Ibis fly in a formation making a distinct letter
“ F.” The ancient Romans considered the Ibis a
bird of ill-omen, but “ F ” is the initial of Federa-
tion. Are even the birds of the air crying out
for it ?—R.H.

The young aboriginal girl whose photo,
appears on this page is now in charge of Lady
Lamington, who intends taking her to the old
country. The myall maiden who has thus fallen
into a soft place is named Alreena, a native of
“ Binambi,” near Durundur, Queensland. Her
mother’s name was “Ualmi,” and she spoke a
language called “ Gooray.” Both father and
mother are dead. Alreena will be accompanied
to Great Britain by an aboriginal boy of about 12
years of age as a suitable partner when both are of
ALREENA, daughter of Ualmie, native of
Binambi, Durundar, Queensland, aged 7 years.
To be taken to Scotland by Lady Lamington.
Photo. Mathewson & Co., Brisbane.
marriageable age. These will be the first abori-
ginals ever taken to the old country by any Aus-
tralian Governor. It appears that Lord and Lady
Lamington have taken a very earnest interest in
the welfare of the aboriginals ever since their
arrival, and they are taking these two young
people in order to dispel some of the dense ignor-
ance of the old-eountry with regard to the intelli-
gence and physique of the Australian aboriginal.
This boy and girl were selected by Mr. Meston,
who doubtless took care that they came from pure
and healthy myall stock.

Premier Seddon has already saved £36 a
year off his anticipated old-age-pension expendi-
ture. Two old men (80 and 75) were awarded the
full allowance of =£lB a year each, but as one of
them promptly killed the other with an axe, both
pensions stop short after the first instalment.
This is the first death among Digger Dick’s pen-
sioner and his opponents will doubtless set it
down to the evil results of “ experimental legis-
lation. ”
Adelaide’s tram service is a curiously anti-
quated affair in one respect. There is no central
control; the horse-cars of about ten private com-
panies radiate out of the Holy City, but there has
never been any serious attempt at State or muni-
cipal ownership, or at combining the whole con-
cern under one private management, and cable or
electric traction has never been seriously advo-
cated. The old live-meat force has always been
good enough for Adelaide —some wild humorist
has suggested going back to ox-power. And, on
the other hand, S.A. has, in its Betaloo reservoir,
the biggest, most successful, and most up-to-date
water-service south of the line.

The English Institute of Journalists has
at last agreed to affiliate with the Maoriland
Institute. The original idea of M.L. pressmen
was to get a local charter which would have made
journalism as close a profession as medicine or
law, and allowed the University to sit heavily
upon its chest 1 regardless of the fact that the
cleverest men in newspaperdom are those—like
Kipling -who have gone about with their eyes
open and made the world their school. As things
are already, few M.L. press billets are independent
of the Institute, and the privileges conferred by a
Parliamentary charter would have made it too
lordly a body altogether.
Forest Creek Hydraulic Dredging Co. is
being vigorously pushed up from behind by an
enterprising Castlemanian, G. H. Clark, whose
level-headedness is evidenced by the fact that he
started life a few years ago, more or less, with
about £2 17s. 9d. more than the proverbial shilling,
and now shares with his dad a business worth
about £.‘50,000 or thereabouts. Also he feels all
right about adding considerably to his accumula-
tions by his Forest Creek investments. Pros-
pectus of venture invites attention in another col.
N.B.—Dad aforesaid has no connection with Dave
or Joe. *
A Bulletin correspondent :
A yet unpublished fact in connection with the Darra-
hill murder has been known to many residents of adjoin-
ing districts for weeks past. Shortly after the boy Hill
was lost a local constable sent to the Commissioner of
Police a report, in which he stated that, as Wilson and
his cripple son had passed from Goodna to Oxley just
at the time the boy was coming from Oxley to Red-
bank Plains, he considered it probable that Wilson had
met the boy and decoyed him off the road into the bush,
and that, having effected his purpose, Wilson, to prevent
discovery, had shot the boy. The result was that the
constable, who has been long in the service with a clean
record, was ridiculed for his over-zealousness, and his
report, which exactly fitted the since-discovered facts of
the case, was contemptuously thrown aside. Had this
report been acted upon, Wilson would have been arrested
right away.

No mistake about Braddon being Boss of
Raspberry Land. The other day extensive
arrangements were made to officially open a new
drive up to the Springs on Mt. Wellington with a
glittering society function headed by Lady Gor-
manston. The Lands Minister, after whom the
new road is to be named, enthusiastically fell in
with the project; then he “ fell in ”in another
way. Braddon suddenly discovered that the ffite
was to be used by a well-known rival politician as
a means of airing his eloquence and his patriotic
authoritativeness on Beauty Spots at Govt, ex-
jjense. After that, there wasn’t any function
whatever. The weary Premier politely intimated
through the press that “inasmuch as he con-
sidered the mountain drive somewhat dangerous
to congested vehicular traffic “ (which is quite
true), “the Govt, had reluctantly decided not to
incur the responsibility of an official opening.”
Of course, there was much heartburning, and the
kindly old Minister for Lands looked foolish, but
the Boss had spoken.

“The lock-out of the lumpers was not a
move of the united shipowners, but of that section
representing Australian co.’s in league with the
railway department,” writes a Bulletin man
from Perth. “Paul Strelitz, representing the Ger-
man-Australian line, declared that his co. did not
want a reduction of wages, that he got no invita-
tion to the conference, and that he would not have
had any delay with his trade only for the com-
plicity of the railway department. The depart-
ment had blackleg-labor on the trucks. Strelitz
offered to put his own (union) men into the trucks
and pay for whole time, but the railway dept,
refused to put the trucks alongside. Piermaster
and district superintendent were applied to but
refused assistance. ‘We have been drawn into
the dispute practically by the Govt, who had no
right to interfere in the disturbance.’ ”

How the at-least-theoretical Radical Stout
and the Tory Vogel came to coalesce. Maoriland,
in ’B4, was in a bad way. The “ Continuous
Ministry ” had held office (save three years of the
Grey Govt.) so long that the country was weary.
The Liberals had not recovered from the muddle
into which Grey had landed the finances. There
was confusion and shuffling and re-shuffling of
Ministries. Meanwhile Vogel lay low and said
little. But one Sunday afternoon he invited a re-
porter to have a glass of wine in his room, and there
in quite a casual, innocent manner he began to talk.
He professed not to see that the pressman was
surreptitiously making notes. Lolling hack with
his eyes half closed, Julius poured his glittering
day-dreams into his captive’s ear. He had schemes
for raising money out of nothing, for making rail-
ways everywhere, for paving Maoriland with gold,
and for generally making the two islands a fairy-
land. The glittering, plausible Julius was talking
for his life—office meant life to him just then
and no one could talk better. The pressman, who
thought that he was really pumping reluctant
knowledge out of the great man, put columns of
Vogel’s golden programme in Viis paper next day ;
the country wanted anyone with a programme
just then, and the. glamor of the big forcible Jew
fell on Maoriland once more. A week before,
Stout would hardly have taken Vogel into his
prospective Ministry. Not long after, Vogel took
him in. He had either to join Vogel or stand out.

The Rakaia Butchery.
A man connected for many years with Maori-
land Railway Department writes :
The inquest on the Rakaia railway smash has resulted
in a practical verdict of manslaughter against driver
Carter of the train that did the damage. Yet the fact
that the intelligent jury also pointed out that there was a
lack of signalling appliances at Rakaia, that the brake-
power was insufficient, that there were not enough
capable guards (meaning guards of proved ability), and
that the stationmaster at Ashburton committed an error
of judgment in not warning the driver that his trained
guard Was left behind; indicated that that body had
a vague feeling that the driver was more victim than
transgressor.
The last person who should go to gaol for the Rakaia
smash-up is driver Carter, and if a jury sends him to gaol
for manslaughter it should see to it that certain other
people are tried for murder. In Christchurch traffic
office is an officer who draws a fat salary to point out to
another highly-paid gentleman in the head office at
Wellington what is needed for the safe working of the
section. And if that officer lias never pointed out that no
single-line station where as many as three trains some-
times cross can be safely worked without “home” and
“ distant ” semaphores, then M. L. should deposit that long
row of corpses at his door and ask him to account for
them. If he has pointed it out, and the great man in
Wellington has ignored the report, then he ought to stand
his trial. Also, whoever is responsible for the absence of
the YVest'nghouse, or some other system of continuous
brake, from M.L. railway system, ought to do a sudden
drop at the end of a long, greased rope.
If driver Carter was reckless, which does not seem at
all clear, h's negligence is as nothing compared to the
utter in lilference of the alleged management. Rakaia
station, approached from the south, lies at the foot of a
small hill or ridge, about 800yds. from the summit. This
driver ha 1 a tra : n of 15 carriages ; there was a high wind ;
it was raining and the rails were slippery, which
neutralised the action of the brakes; the man in the
van was only able to control the brakes on his own
vehicle, and the driver only able to control those on the
engine iiself. The brakes on the carriages could not be
applied ; so, out of 17 vehicles, only two had brakes that
could be used to prevent accident. The driver could not
see the station till he climbed over a ridge and looked
down on it in a hollow only 800yds. away, but the night
was bad, and the foggy, drifting rain obscured the view,
and there was nothing to tell him that the line was not
clear. Presently he could see the tail-lights of another
train through the drifting rain and thought they were on
the other side of the station. Anyhow, he was justified
in coming on because, if the line was not clear, there
ought to have been a man with a lamp to stop him ; and
he did what any other driver would have done—came on
and whistled for a signal. Just here his alleged error of
judgment came in : he should have had his train in hand,
and he had not. But perhaps it was impossible for him
to have it in hand owing to want of brake-power and
the high wind behind, and the slippery rails and high
gradient. Anyhow, a red light was flashed in his eyes
and, right in front—soyds. away only—loomed up the
other train on the same line. On went the steam brake,
the whistles screamed for van brakes, but the howling
sou’-wester drove the heavy train along the slippery rails,
and then —crash !
Had there been a semaphore signal at the top of that
hill 800yds. away, driver Carter would have stopped before
he cleared the crest of the ridge, and there would have
been no accident and no “ Rakaia victims.” It was the
absence of a semaphore that made the accident possible
and probable. Had the train been fitted with continuous
brake, in all human probability it could have been
stopped in time to prevent the telescoping. And the re-
sponsibility for the lives that were lost and the injuries
sustained in the Rakaia collision lies on the soul of the
criminally careless official who failed to provide Rakaia
station with distant and home signals, and the author of
the cheese-paring policy that has so long shirked the
continuous-brake question. And it rests with a jury to
see that these criminally careless officials do not make the
grimy driver Carter the scape-goat of their sins.
Maoriland railways are full of death-traps like Rakaia.
The most hideous smash of all will be the one that will
take place between Oaversham and Kensington stations,
just outside Dunedin This scribe has turned sick with
dread on wet, windy nights in Oaversham station when,
r giving “line clear” to a heavy train at Burnside
(the station on the other side of the hill), Kensington
(further down the hill on the s ame side as Oaversham) has
suddenly signalled “Obstruction” while a shunting train
ran out to Hillside (half-way between Oaversham and
Kensington) for empty trucks. And one night of hideous
SSKSTfe
nailed to stop the goods, as they had got a truck across
the line. I stood awe-struck for a moment; from the
other side of the hill came the gasping of the engine mak-
ing her last desperate tug to haul the train into the tun-
neh I rushed madly up the hill to where a red light
could be seen at the earliest possible moment. But
before I got clear of the platform she,came roaring out of
th e tunnel. I waved the red light wildly, the signals
shone red, but nothing but omnipotence could stop a
heavy train on that gradient with the sort of brake-power
they had. As she roared past all the danger-signals
I saw the white face of the driver hoi,ling down the steam-
brake, the frenzied stoker struggling with the lever-brake,
an< 4 the guard with set teeth gripping his hand-brake in
an a "°n.V’ °f desperation ; and I threw myself down (I’m
not ashamed to say it), and screamed a prayer to God for
a miracle to save the lives of those men. Down at Hillside
they heard the thunder of the coming train, and, with
one desperate effort, hauled the truck clear. As it top-
pled over 011 its side the goods crashed past them and
pulled up half a mile beyond.
That was in the days when M'Kerrow, Maxwell, and
Hannay were Commissioners. M'Kerrow knew as much
about railway management as Tub Bollktin does of Theo-
sophy. He is a surveyor by training, and was appointed
Chief-Commissioner because he had a most pleasant way
of saying “No” to people who wanted special district
rates, and unionists who wanted shorter hours and better
pay. Maxwell and Hannay had a brutal way of
saying “No ” that used to arouse political hatred. And
M‘Kerrow, as an ex-Chief Commissioner, is one of the
triumvirate who, for years, neglected to supply Rakaia
with semaphores and introduce the Westinghouse brake,
and is therefore one of the men more-or-less responsible
for the Rakaia accident. And this is one of the men who,
with Judge Ward, is to make what the Minister for Rail-
ways is pleased to term an “independent investigation.”
To ask him to declare that the absence of semaphores and
continuous brakes was what made the accident possible,
is to ask him to condemn his own management, and take
a share of the blame on his own shoulders ; and this
writer asks any intelligent Maorilander if any man whose
name commences with Mac is likely to do that ?

BILE BEANS AGAIN TRIUMPHANT.
[Fully Investigated by the Adelaide Advertiser.)
Diabetes, Rheumatism, or Bright’s Disease are
severally among the most distressing and danger-
ous complaints with which man or woman can be
afflicted. But when those complaints are found
to collectively attack a patient, then the patient’s
condition is a truly deplorable one, and one where-
in hope might be abandoned with much justifica-
tion. Mrs. M. E. Austin, of Exeter Curve,
Semaphore-road, Australia, is a lady upon which
the frown of fortune had brought such misery as
to afflict her in the manner indicated. It needs
no stretch of the imagination to paint a pitiful
picture of the sufferings of the unfortunate lady
during the two years over which her affliction ex-
tended. Added to her physical pains was the men-
tal torture which she was obliged to endure when
the doctors, by whom she was attended, pro-
nouuced her case hopeless. It is a cheerless
knowledge to have that your life is fast ebbing
away, and that your few remaining days are to
be spent in suffering and in misery. Yet this
was the knowledge possessed by this unfortunate
lady. But the most remarkable portion of her
story has yet to be related, and it is her thorough
and permanent cure by the use of Bile Beans.
Mrs. Austin’s story is in her own words, as fol-
lows:—“I contrac-
ted rheumatic
fever about 11
years ago. At that
time I was laid up
foreighteen weeks,
and the illness has
recurred to me
every year regu -
larly since that
time until two
years ago, when I
had a very bad at-
tack. At the same
time I had dia-
betes (a form of Bright’s disease) and hemorrhage
of the lungs. I went into the Adelaide Hospital
to be treated for the diabetes, and whilst in the
institution the hemorrhage of the lungs and the
rheumatism came on. I remained in the hospital
from the 29th August until about a fortnight be-
fore Christmas, when I left—uncured, and, in fact,
not a bit better than when I went in. I could not
have been worse and be alive. I was so weak that
I had to be lifted out of the cab which brought
me from the hospital. Two or three days after
leaving the hospital I had a very severe attack of
rheumatism. I was attended by two doctors, and
they informed me that I had Bright’s Disease,
which, they said, had been brought on by the
attacks of rheumatism. All the doctors who at-
tended me—including one at the Adelaide Hospi-
tal-informed me that my case was hopeless.
Owing to my slender means, I then gave up
consulting the doctors, as, in the face of
what they told me, I thought it would
have been useless to continue spending money in
obtaining medical assistance. My symptoms
were: Pains in tbe small of tbe back, a dry,
parched tongue, I did not know what it was after
August, 1897, to have a good night’s sleep, and a
weak and languid feeling. I continued in this
condition until the first week in September, 1898,
when I was advised by many friends to try Bile
Beans. I decided to do so, and accordingly pur-
chased a box at Mr. Rowe’s, the chemist, at Port
Adelaide. After I had taken three or four doses
(I took two pills at night and one in the morning)
I felt an improvement in my health. My appetite
became more natural, and I could sleep better,
and the pains in the small of the back began to
disappear. After I had taken the contents of
half-a-dozen boxes, the pains disappeared entirely,
and I felt an improvement in every respect. I
now continue to take the Beans in small quanti-
ties to preserve my health. I may state that I
now do all my housework and washing without
any trouble, and life is worth living. I attribute
the whole of my cure to Bile Beans, and they
deserve every credit. The neighbors can bear out
the truth of my statements. lam doing all I can
to induce others to take the Beans.”
Summer-End Fatigue.
Fatigue is one of the most common, and, in
fact, nowadays one of the almost natural effects
of summer. Owing to the heat, our blood has
become thinned and our systems debilitated, in
which condition the body is very susceptible to
disease. By reason of this state of exhaustion,
the body requires something to brace it up and en-
able it to repel the dangers which threaten it, and
to sustain the extra demands which will be made
upon it by altered climatic Conditions. In order
to do this the blood must be in a healthy state and
free of impurities. But before we can have
healthy blood we must have healthy blood-pro-
ducing organs. The stomach and the liver are the
two great blood-producing organs of the human
body. If aiiy impairment of these organs exists,
it must follow that the supply of blood cannot in
its purity or abundance be sufficient, and, as a
consequence, the body is made ripe to receive
any disease. This condition of the body is
indicated by a general debility, which is the
direct outcome of a combination of nervous ex-
haustion, indigestion, deranged liver and consti-
pation. The waste matter is not properly carried
out of the system, the blood becomes poor and
the complexion sallow. To remedy this serious
state of affairs it is necessary that the root of the
evil should be sought out and a remedy applied
to it with vigor. Bile Beans have been proved in
thousands of cases to be the required remedy, and
to be the most efficacious known medicine in deal-
ing with the fatigues and illness which generally
result from a long period of hot weather. They
act upon the liver and stomach in such a manner
as to assist these organs to satisfactorily perform
their natural functions, and when this state is
reached a supply of pure rich blood, and conse-
quent good health is ensured. Bile Beans are so
compounded that they act in a gentle manner,
and repair the waste to the nervous system which
results from summer fatigues, loss of tone, lack of
physical force, etc.

Constipation.
Everyone suffers at some time or other from this
most distressing and rather dangerous ailment. It
is an irregular and weak action of the bowels, and
in extreme cases the bowels may not act for weeks.
In a case of this sort the complaint is very
dangerous, and a remedy should be applied im-
mediately. Women often suffer more from it than
men, and the frequent headaches suffered by this
sex can nearly always trace their origin to consti-
pation. It is caused in most cases by carelessness
and neglect, but in some cases it is brought about
by a disordered condition of the liver. It is,
nevertheless, a fact that when regularity of habit
is practised constipation is seldom found. The
effects of constipation on the system are many and
varied, among the most common of which may be
mentioned piles, sick headaches, debility, sour
stomach, humors, and intermittent fevers. Regu-
larity is the main thing to be observed in cases of
constipation, and, in conjunction with a short
course of Bile Beans, will prove very effective.
The early morning, immediately after breakfast,
is the best time to make a trial, which, if unsuc-
cessful, should nevertheless be persevered in the
next day, and so on until the case is cured.
Indigestion.
Indigestion is an ailment which may develop
into a most severe disease if not properly attended
to. Acute chronic indigestion is by no means a
slight affair, as witness the case of Mr. Samuel
Fisher. Attacks of indigestion in a more or less
mild form are too common to dwell upon for any
length of time. As its name implies, this disorder
is an insufficient action of the various digestive
organs, which prevents the food being properly
dissolved. In cases of this kind the effect is two-
fold : First, the waste part of the food is kept in
the stomach and becomes sour instead of being
carried off ; and, secondly, the blood-making or
useful part of the food is kept from performing its
offices. The causesmf indigestion are many : In-
sufficient liver action, over-eating, eating too fast,
and partaking of rich and indigestible foods, etc.,
etc. Constant worry or mental strain, or lack of
regularity, may also cause an attack. The first
thing necessary to cure an indisposition of this
sort is a thorough opening of the bowels, and a
complete clearing out of the whole digestive or-
ganism ; then the liver should be stimulated to
action, and regularity observed in everything.
Bile Beans will do the first two of these, the
patient must attend to the third. Chronic indi-
gestion, of course, needs longer treatment than a
temporary attack. It is well to bear in mind that
headaches, face pimples, listlessness, and a host of
minor ailments arise from indigestion, or some
other form of digestive disorder.

THE MAYOR’S GUARANTEE.
“ Glen Innes, N.S.W., Oct. 11, ’9B.
“ H. H. Warnerand Co., Ltd., Melbourne.
“ Gentlemen, —I have often thought it my duty
for the sake of others to write you. It will be five
years next month since my wife was given up by
two doctors as a patient who could not live the
night through.
She had been treated for heart complaint; but
we afterwards found that it was kidney disease.
On Sunday when the doctors told me that she
would not live another day—the dropsical swelling
had then extended from her legs into the body—a
friend said to me : “ Try Warner’s Safe Cure.”
At half past ten that night I procured a bottle and
gave her one dose, then in about an hour another.
She said soon after, “Jim, I feel a little easier.”
I continued with Warner’s Sab- Cure, giving up
the doctor’s medicine, and to day she is a living
monument to its efficacy. The doctors and every-
one who knew her were astonished.
I have recommended this medicine scores of
times since, and we always keep it in the house.
If my liver is sluggish I take it as a tonic and
invigorator.
After five years I consider the trial a fair one,
and that it is consistent to call my wife’s cure per-
manent. lam well known in Glen Innes, having
lived here sixteen years, been fourteen years
Alderman, and twice elected Mayor of the muni-
cipality, and I am not afraid to sign my name as a
guarantee that Warner’s Safe Cure is what it is
represented to be.—Yours faithfully,
JAMES HEALEY. Mayor.”

THE MOKE'S MURMUR.
SOL: “ Well, this is a climate. I’m melting one
end and freezing to death the other! ”
There are big rocks ahead of the Q. Govt.
The Southern members, sick of Northern domin-
ance in the Cabinet, are reputed to be meditating
a rebellion with a view to a re-distribution of
portfolios. Mackay boasts two Ministers and
Brisbane none ! . .
Premier Dickson (Q.) wired to Premier
Reid last week, regretting the “ obstruction ” of
N.S.W. Leg. Council in re the Federal Enabling
Bill. That is the first time that a prominent
Minister of any Australian province has inter-
fered, by a message of encouragement or rebuke,
in the pending Parliamentary proceedings of an-
other province. Had Parkes received such a wire,
whether for or against his cause, he would have
suppressed it till the local Parliament had finished
its fight.
N.S.W. ex-Treas. M‘Millan boasts of
trusting to the “ Sovereign- People.” But ask
him what he means by the S.P. and he will
“ The people as represented in Parliament.”
Therefore lie professes trust in the people but dis-
trusts the Referendum.
“The Labor-party has treated me in a
chivalrous way,” said Premier Reid at Newcastle.
It out his tea duty from 3d. to Id.; wiped out
his coffee, chicory, and other taxes; and in the
course of every important debate manages to call
him a wriggler about a score of times.
The 19M’s.L.C. who carried the proposi-
tion that there should be no Federation in
N.S.W. unless the affirmative majority reached
80,000 votes, were an ancient lot and practically
all past their mental prime. They aggregated
1142 years, so their average age was a shade over
60. '
Obviously the Age is at heart no friend of
Federation. It has been forced to enlist in the
federal ranks by the action of its hated rival, the
Argus ; but it is protectionist-provincialist above
all things, and quite happy so long as "Victoria
can enjoy the N.S.W. market, while N.S.W. is
shut out of Victoria’s.
Arthur Rutledge, who again enters Q.
Parliament through the bungling of the local
Labor executive, has been appointed Byrnes’
successor as Attorney-General. Rutledge is an
easy-going, happy-go-lucky, well-fed ex-Wesleyan
parson, destined to caress a fair round stomach on
the Supreme Court bench.

all pay high tribute to the organisation and disci-
pline in the ranks of the Labor-party. Said a

Well, Lowles and Vine materially influenced

A few more such victories and the Boodle-
wraith will rot in its grave instead of putrefying

F. B. Suttor and Premier Reid concur, if
one may; judge from a question asked and answered
in Parliament, in the view that N.S.W. Agent-

company. Right to begin with. But why not
