The rag-tag-and-bobtail character of the
platform support accorded to G. Republican Dibbs
at his Sydney Town Hall anti-Federal meeting is
accounted for by the fact that John Norton had
been selected as principal speaker. When the
Commercial Bank and M‘Laurin crowd got to
know that John was to figure largely they declined
to “take any.”
Significant that the members of the
Kingston Govt, all, with a single exception, repre-
sent districts so Liberal that the other member
is a Laborist (there are two members for each
S.A. electorate), or is almost certain to be
one. after next election. The exception is
Yatala, represented by Education Butler; and
even that is thought to be not quite hopeless, in
view of what the State Loan Department and the
Export Dep6t have done.
Q. Parliamentary Labor-party has
lost the only real and original thinker it
ever possessed—Henry Turley, ex-member
for Brisbane South. To those who appre-
ciate only the racy in political discussion,
Turley was generally a bore ; but, never-
theless, he was the solid bulwark of Q. Par-
liamentary Opposition. Cleverer talkers,
like Dawson, dressed up the facts and con-
clusions of Turley and scored heaps of
flattering press-notices ; but the hard thinker
from South Brisbane was the man who was
doing the work, raking statistics, drawing
conclusions and comparisons, and acting
generally as draught-horse of his party.
The godly towns of Queensland,
Ipswich and Maryborough, return Govt,
supporters, as heretofore. Wherever priest
and parson are strong in the North the
Democrat goes down. Wherever the
churches are in abeyance, and the voice of
the gaunt Gospeller is almost a curiosity,
there the Labor-men flourish and the Democrats
are returned to Parliament. Apparently, the
true mission of the churches is to set the workers
at variance on non-essentials.
The list published of members of N.S.W.
Assembly who, it is alleged, will declare against
the Bill only numbers 43, despite the assertion
that a secret ballot would reveal a majority
against it. The 43 includes Dick (who has spoken
in favor of the Bill), Barnes (who is counted
twice), Stent (who is not in the House), and
Mayor Harris.
Said that Premier Reid counted up to 193
gentlemen with wide waistcoats and narrow views
willing, to swamp N.S.W. Leg. Council for the
“honor” of M.L.C. and the profit of a railway
pass ; and left off with the list unfinished. The
rush of Tories who have suddenly become ardent
Federationists puts the Barron falls in flood-time
right out of running.
The immediate future of Federation in
Westralia depends on the will of one man—J. W.
Hackett, editor of the West Australian, bell-
wether of the Leg. Council, and managing director
of the Forrest Govt. Forrest and Hackett are
for once on opposite sides, but whereas Forrest is
a passive Federalist —not as Premier, as a private
member—Hackett is a fighting anti-Federalist,
and will probably persuade the Leg. Council to
prevent the Bill going to the people. If the
people get a word on the question, Federation
would be carried—as it will be by the Assembly.
LESLIE MACDONALD,
“Bobadil’s" stage manager.
Attorney-Gen. Isaacs says that the Vic.
grand-jury system, which was responsible for the
placing of Detective Dungey and his female
“assistant” on their trial, is a matter to be
earnestly considered by the Crown Law dept.
The same Isaacs saw nothing but good in the
system when it clashed with Bryan O’Loghlen’s
nolle prosequi in the Mercantile Bank case.

According to Q. Worker, the Boodle-
wraith Govt, has at Q. general election really
gained a majority of seats with a minority of
votes. Total votes given for Labor and Opposi-
tion candidates, 45,837; for Govt, candidates,
39,753. But Govt, has in an Assembly of 72
members 43 seats ; Labor and Opposition only 29.
Isn’t this a tip for Labor to use the contingent
vote?
N.S.W. Leg. Councillors are an obliging
set of humorists. When the Assembly objected to
their proviso in the Enabling Bill that N.S.W.
shouldn’t federate unless Queensland came in, the
Council cordially offered to make things worse by
providing that N.S.W. shouldn’t federate unless
both Queensland and Victoria came in. The
accommodating nature of the old gentlemen is
something quite remarkable.
Eight lepers, according to Worker, were
unearthed in one recent week in Queensland—six
at Mackay, one at Maryborough, and one at Bun-
daberg. All in the sugar districts, where the
yellow and brown men are thickest. The Govt,
and capitalistic press did not think it worth while
to mention this, especially as the elections were
on and the colored labor and Asiatic restriction
question is one of the main planks in the Labor-
party’s platform.
Doubtful if Q. Premier Dickson will
manage to keep office in the just-elected Parlia-
ment. Many of the so-called “ Independent
Ministerialists ” are simply anti-Dicksonites in
disguise, and it is noteworthy that most of those
“ Independents ” are men who bid for more than
=6300 a year, and, being mostly bald and blear-
eyed, they can’t afford to wait too long for their
] *oi tfolids and the thousand. Then K. B. Forrest
lias the energy and ambition of MTlwraith, and is
a big cloud on the weather bow.
One of Premier Reid’s amendments pro-
vides that the boundaries of any original State
cannot be altered without the' consent of a
majority of the electors of that State. Some legal
minds argue that under this clause N.S.W. can
practically choose the site of the federal oapital
(always providing for the 100 miles from Sydney
limit), because, by handing over to the absolute
jurisdiction of the Commonwealth the area
required for the capital, the mother province must
of necessity alter her boundaries.
Two of the quaint laws at the pious island
of Pitcairn, where the women all lose their front
teeth :
No person or persons to bring coooanut orcocoanut*
from t’other side or any other part of the island without
their being accompanied by one of the authorities or
churchwardens.
No person or persons are to kill any cat unless doing
him damage. If any one be found so doing, shall be
punished by having his dog taken from him and be killed;
and should the person have no dog, he shall be punished
by the magistrate. Should a dog go out with his master
and fall in with a eat and chase him, and the owner of
the dog make all effort to save the cat, this will save his
dog, though the cat die afterwards ; but should no effort
he made to save the cat, the dog must he confined for the
first offence, and be killed for the second.
According to N.S.W. Labor-m&mber
Hughes : “ There can be no doubt whatever
that the proposed Federation will kill Demo-
cracy.” Why, cert’nly ! Of course the shortest
way to kill Democracy is to have both Houses
elected by the people, with one-man-one-vote for
both Houses, members of both Houses to be paid,
no property-qualification for members, a joint
sitting in case of deadlocks, the majority to decide
—as provided by the Draft Constitution ! ! That
Constitution is practically a copy of the Swiss one
which has made Switzerland the most democratic
country on earth, and which the Hughes and
Holman party is always holding up as a model to
the world. And now that it is offered to him
Hughes denounces the Swiss constitution as a
beastly engine of Fatman.
John Norton’s Truth calls on the people
to reject the Federation scheme because
it doesn’t provide for federating the
debts and the railway. So does
Sydney Telegraph. And yet the
maligned Bill, in clause 51, para-
graphs xxxm. and xxxiv. at
least allows the Federal Govt, to
take over all railways with the
consent of the States, which is
something ; and in clause 105 it
gives the Federal Govt, absolute
power to take over all the States
debts as they exist at the date
when the Commonwealth starts,
i which gives it every opportunity
l to take over =6200,000,000 of
| debt to begin with. That is
W to say the Bill provides that the
Commonwealth “may” take
1 over the debts and that it
“shall” fix a capital inN.S.W.,
And this section of the Anti-
Billious says that it should read
that the debts “shall” betaken
over to make the matter abso-
lutely certain; also that the
“ shall ” in the other case doesn’t
make anything certain because the
capital will be in Victoria all the
same ! !
Three years ago N.S.W. Labor-party
stumped the country in denunciation of the very
existence of the Leg. Council. Now, per Holman
and others, they stump the country maintaining
that the Leg. Council is the saviour of the people.
What a downfall had the Laborites been strong
enough three years ago to abolish that body 1

Biter Bit ” : Re Sydney Safe Deposit, a friend
of mine, going on holidaj's, got- even with an enterprising
burglar recently. He took his plate, etc., to the Deposit,
and posted this notice inside his safe: —“My friend, I
regret you should have all this trouble for nothing. I
anticipated your call, and have left my valuables at the
Sydney Safe Deposit, in Ash-street, back of Paling’s. Try
there. I have also arranged so that when you open the
safe-door it warns the police. Au revoir.” The latter
was bluff, but I guess the e.b. scooted some.*

PROSPECTUS OF
Forest Creek Hydraulic Dredging Co.
CASTLEMAmB.
(To be Registered as a No Liability Company under Part 2 of the Companies Act 1890.)
Capital - - £22,500, in 45,000 Shares of 10s. each.
♦-
15,000 Paid-up Shares and £5OO in Cash to be allotted to the Vendors in full payment for the property.
30,000 Shares are offered to the public at Is. 6d. per Share on application and is. 6d. per Share on allotment.
The whole of the proceeds, less above payment and brokerage and flotation expenses, not to exceed £750, to be
placed to the credit of the Company.
THIS company is being formed to acquire that
magnificent dredging area comprising the
whole of Forest Creek, extending for about two
miles from Patterson Bridge, Castlemaine, to
Manchester Bridge, Chewton. About the centre
of the lease Moonlight Creek, which drains a large
area of rich auriferous country, empties into Forest
Creek. There is no private property in this lease.
The three richest diggings in Castlemaine dis-
trict were Fryer’s Creek, Forest Creek and Camp-
bell’s Creek, and the question as to whether
hydraulic dredging will pay has been practically
settled by the splendid return obtained by the
Campbell’s Creek Dredging Company at their
first clean-up.
Thousands of people all over Victoria know
what Forest Creek was in the early days, which
makes it altogether unnecessary that a number of
expert reports should be submitted. The vendors
rely on the well-known character of the ground
ensuring its flotation.
The large extent of the lease will make it quite
practicable to put several dredging plants on the
property.
All those taking shares in this first company
will participate in any future flotations, which of
itself ought to be sufficient inducement to the
public to take up the shares.
A complete hydraulic plant of improved con-
struction can be procured on exceedingly liberal
terms.
Starting with this large amount of money in
hand, and being able to arrange extended terms
for payment of plant, two calls of Cd. each within
six months will be sufficient to see the plant at
work, when it is reasonable to expect that the
first clean-up will see the machinery paid for and
the company in a position to pay dividends. The
fullest inquiry regarding the property is solicited.
Shares will be allotted strictly according to
priority of application. It will be necessary to
enclose amount of application money with order
to secure shares.
Application for shares to be made to
Feed. Thonemann and Co., 58 Queens-street,
Melbourne.
G. H. Clark (G. Clark and Son), Castlemaine.
The Bank of Australasia, Castlemaine and
Melbourne.
Legal Manager (Pro. Tern.):
David Blair, 17 Queen-street, Melbourne.
Directors:
To be Appointed First Meeting of Company.
Bankers:
The Bank of Australasia.
Solicitors :
Messrs. M'Cay andThwaites, Castlemaine.
Consulting Engineers :
Messrs. Gilfillan and M‘Creery.
Plans of the lease may be seen and prospectuses obtained at the various offices where applications
are to be made, and at the office of Messrs. Gilfillan and M‘Creery, Consulting Engineers, 434 Collins-

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Old Carnage has indirectly given rise to,”
writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent, “ a ludi-
crous contention between the racing authorities and an
Irish owner. And thus it arose, and has arisen : The
Australian entire was introduced to a feminine equine
named Tenderfoot who has been a fair ‘plater’ and is
the propertv of Hon. J. B. Roche, M.P. for East Kerry.
To the foal that obtruded itself among the shamrocks
Jimmy Roche gave the most appropriate title of Bally-
bunion—after a seaside place in North Kerry. Straight-
way the ignorant turf autocrats, seeing naught but blood
and corns in the name, declined to register it, as Rule 15
bars curses, swearwords, or blasphemy. In much cool
and restrained—another straining wouldn’t have hurt it
■ language Roche commented with characteristic Celtic
calmness on the emaciated condition of the T.A.’s ‘jo-
graphee,’ hinted at a map of Ireland, and finalAd if he
wanted to call a horse of his ‘Gory Warts’ he would
call him G.W.—and not Bally Winkles. Up to now, the
odds are more than even on J. B,”
* • * *
The A.J.C. A-utumn meeting started on Satur-
day. with fine weather, and got grievously bogged on
Mo f'9 a y- Affairs began with a gleam of luck for the
public. Kelso’s Wongy, favorite in a small unobtrusive
way, won the First Hurdle Race, and there was a faint
moan in Israel. Then another favorite, Merloolas, car-
ried off the Autumn Stakes, and Israel moaned a trifle
louder. After that, the public’s luck went to pieces.
Dewey, a 7 to 1 outsider, came in first for the
two-year-old Maiden Stakes. The favorite, Sirdar, was
only beaten by a head. In the Doncaster Handicap the
public put most of its ill-gotten wealth on North British,
who turned up about seventh, while Vigorous, the winner,
was an 8 to 1 chance, and the second animal, Hopscotch,
Carried hardly any public money. Vigorous, ridden by
£. fielder, came with a brilliant rush in the straight after
being a very unobtrusive animal all through the early
part of the race, and snatched the victory from Hop-
scotch, whose effort came just a little too late. Time,
lmin. 42sec., the slowest since Donizetti’s win in ’94.
The St. Leger broke the hearts of the punters
who piled their money on Bobadil, and saw the prize
scooped by the more or less obscure Johansen, who
started at 10 to 1. Bobadil and Lee Metford ran a dead
heat for second place. It was a great win by two whole
lengths, in 3min. lljsec. (Newhaven, in ’97, took 3min.
22£sec. to cover the distance), and the hat of Moses went
up in the air. Then al2 to 1 outsider (Tanner) won the
High-weight Handicap, and the public walked home.
On the boggy Monday the public’s money dis-
solved like a snowball in perdition. Dan O’Connell,
hardly mentioned in the betting, won the Flying Handi-
cap with ridiculous ease. The favorite got left in the
Maiden Stakes, won by Nuka. Reviver (second favorite)
captured the Champagne Stakes, which was the best
thing that happened to the public on that sad wet day.
Bobadil had been scratched for Sydney Cup, and the public
piled what money and enthusiasm it had left on Merloolas ;
and H. Power’s Diffidence, a 20 to 1 chance that hung
fire in the ruck of the betting, made a holy show of him
and all the other fancied animals. Still, the public got a
good run for its lost boodle. The time (3min. 31sec.) has
never been beaten for Sydney Cup, and only equalled on
three occasions—by Carbine in ’B9, Wallace in ’96, and
Merloolas in ’9B. It was a day when the winner had
almost to swim for his prize, and Diffidence captured it
with a rush that smothered all opposition.
• » * »
The defeat of Bobadil in the St. Leger was en-
tirely owing to Melb. Paunch. Twice within five months
Paunch has published a double-page portrait of Bobadil,
and each time the 'orse was beaten, in a quite ordinary
sort of way, a day or two after (he event. Who pays for
all this advertisement ?
• * * *
Chieftain, the Moree horse which took first prize
in the trotting exhibition at Sydney Agricultural Show, is
the finest stamp of the trotting horse shown for many
years. He is a noted country prize-taker, but this is his
first assault upon metropolitan honors. He is by'Child'e
Harold from the imported mare Ella Chieftain. Mr.
Beckett’s horse. Bentwood, showed a phenomenal dash of
foot in parts. He is one of the best-bred trotting stallions in
Australia, and moves so much like Tuxedo that numbers
who saw Mr. Rivett’s little flyer last year mistook Bent-
wood for him when passing round the ring. Mr. Bryan’s
stallion. Souvenir, by Era, is a horse that improves with
age. He moved round the track like a piece of machinery,
and has improved out of all recognition upon last year’s
form.
• « * •
As might have been expected, Dubbo Trotting
Club made a mistake as regards attendance in fixing its
Sydney Kensington meeting for Thursday last when it
had the Agricultural Show to fight. The sport was fair,
but, as usual, the trouble lay in the failure of the handi-
cappers to bring the horses together at the finish.
One long-bottled horse was let loose, but the good thing
did not come off. The country people did not bring down
any of the fliers one hears so much about, and Sydney
took all the prizes. Mr. Beckett showed he has a good
one in Silver Boy—a gelding by Era—who won the 3min.
class-trot in The most consistent performer of
the day was Mr. M'Millan’s Silverhue, who ran second to
Silver Boy, second to Dr. Slate’s Huon Prince, who won
the Wonbobbie Handicap Trot, and won the Dubbo
Handicap Trot from 4sec. behind in smin. 13J4sec.
* * * •
Operatic Tom Pollard, who races ’osses in M.L.,
achieved a delirious success when Djin Djin (Apremont—
Dudu) walked off with Dunedin Cup, which has been won
by some notable horses, including Sir Modred. The
Pollard troupe were on to a man (and woman). It is the
old story over again of a dotty neddy winning a big race,
but the public tumbled when a stable-mate was scratched
to make the way clear. “ Tom ” won’t rest easy now until
he gets a Carbine for a shot at Melb. Cup.
• * • •
Broken Hill police, after several vain attempts
to overcome the local Ikey Mo and the tote-man, hit
lately on the grand expedient of running in all the tribe
to serve on a Saturday-afternoon coroner’s jury. And, as
the subject had shot himself several days before, betting
was “ off ” for that day. Now the local" paper is calling
for tenders for a regular supply of corpses demanding in-
quests.
• * • *
“ Quare ” : Attended a race-meeting at Menzies
(\V. A.), and, being aware that betting is illegal in that
province, was interested to hear a constable ask a bookie
“ What price Wild Wave for the Hurdles?” He was told
“ sixes,” and had a pound put on for him. Wild Wave
won, and “ Robert ” drew his div., but I kept my wonder-
ing eye on him, and found that when the man in blue lost
he didn’t pay. Of course, if Ikey had squealed he’d have
been threatened with expulsion from the course.

N.S.W. schools’ swimming championship carni-
val came off at Fitzroy dock (Cockatoo) last Saturday in
the presence of about 3000 people, hundreds of whom
were enthusiastic relatives of the competitors. The
anxiety of lady relatives to secure an unobstructed view
of their undressed pet-lambs’ performances was pathetic.
The boys, their skins brown as acorns, all
showed signs of heavy training ; the whitest among them
was a little Chinese, whose nationality was hard to dis-
tinguish when his clothes were off. Australian manly
beauty runs chiefly to legs. Several of the adult per-
formers wandered around in trunks, coats, vests and
hats, and their beautiful brown legs were a source of
delight to at least a section of the fair sex. But the ex-
perienced ones didn’t seem to thrill very much, and
remarked loudly to one another that although the shapes
were “pretty right” there was a most lamentable lack of
hair, and one jaded-looking matron of about 25 declared
that the noble supports were “shaven and polished.”
There were several noteworthy struggles between
the schools. In the 4th heat of the All-Schools cham-
pionship for 13 years and under, Walford, of Fort-street,
had a great battle with Sims of Crown-street. But Fort-
street. had the muscle and got home. The final 100yds.
Public Schools Championship was very exciting.
Anlezark (Bathurst), Martin (Fort-street), and Byers
(Darling-road) sailed along in front of a stiff breeze with
their noses in a line, and right up to the finish it was any-
body’s race. Then Anlezark’s red cap got ahead. Anle-
zark and Byers also had a big struggle for the All-Schools
220yds. Championship. Byers, turning quickly in the
last lap, passed him and won.
There’s the makings of a champion in a 10-year-
old mite named H. Roberts, of Smith-street school. He
was second in the second heat of the 50vds. for 12 years
and under, and won the first heat of the 40yds. for 10
years and under. In the final 60yds. for 12 years he got
home third, scrambled out, darted for the starting-point,
and got there just in time to plunge in and splash home
first in the final All-Schools Championship (40yds. for 10
years). A regular water-rat, with promise of easy grace.
Ex-champion Wylie gave an exhibition of long-
distance diving, and “Turns” Cavill gave a fancy swim-
ming display. But the crowd was hungry for the big
contest between Bishop and Lane. Sporting bucks were
shouting encouragement to their fancy and defiance to
his rival. One man kept droning “60 to lon Bishop,”
and another begged the crowd to take him up at 100
sovs. to a rotten potato on Lane. People climbed on to
the piles of rock to get an unobstructed view of the great
race. Bishop and Lane appeared on the pontoon in red-
white-and-blue and black-and-white respectively, and
there was a great cheering. The starter shouted
which was Mr. Lane and which was Mr. Bishop ;
everybody yelled, and both men jumped. They went
away down the dock neck and neck for 40 yards, when
Bishop got his head in front amid great excitement and
offers of 2tol on the leader. And Lane never got level
again although at 75 yards he made one |supreme effort.
The judge gave it as a win by four feet. The time was
lmin. 1 3-ssec., an Australian record for 100 yards over a
straight course, and only 1 2-ssec. slower than the world’s
best. But the Association won’t let it stand as such on
account of the strong wind that helped them along. The
two styles are utterly dissimilar. Bishop’s mighty arms
seem to tear great holes in the water into which his body
is shot by his piston-like legs. Lane shears through it
with a tireless sleuthlike motion that in a longer distance
would probably wear his bulky conqueror down.
* * * *
Swimmer Lane left Sydney for Melb. Sunday
night intent upon reaching London at the beginning of
the Engjish swimming season. Provided the climate
agrees with him, which is doubtful, as his constitution is
none too strong, Lane should leave his mark down-under,
for Australia has never produced a better all-round per-
former.
* * * *
Frank Laver, the lengthy 13th man in the
Australian team, is a solicitor by profession, but is
attached to Vic. Crown Law Dept, and was formerly
C.P.S. at Melb. City Court. He is a brother of the long-
haired Melb. musician of that clan; was one of the ill-
fated Australian baseball team that got stranded in the
States, and he it was who wrote the history of the tour
which appeared at the time in one of the Melb. dailies.
Laver’s old cricket style, and his most successful, con-
sists of pulling everything to leg, and is bound to get
“rats” from the superior English cricket scribes. But
all the same, he “ gets there.”
• « • *
All Darling’s friends support the statement
that at his father’s urgent request he intends to give up
his business and embark his profits and savings in farm-
ing. Darling left a farm to resume cricket about five
years ago, but his fond pa, who is wealthy and has re-
tired, only tolerated the cricket racket when the fact
that Joe was making money at it appealed to his “ verra
Scotch ” feelings. Only last January Darling seriously
contemplated standing out of the present team; illness
in his family was one of the reasons.
• • * *
The Australian Eleven made a good start to
their career by being mostly seasick. Rough weather for
the first few days would rid them of all traces of farewell
drinks, and put them on their sea-legs for the rest of the
voyage.
• • * *
The League of Vic. Wheelmen has begun to
cry stinking fish, one member complaining that business
has drifted to the bad at the rate of £4O a month for the
last year, whilst another member remarks that “ the
whole thing is a standing disgrace.” As already noted,
the lavishly furnished club, opened with a flourish in
'97, is to be sold to the highest bidder, and the secre-
tary’s salary is threatened with an awful slump. Mean-
time, the M. 8.0. continues to wax fat on the profits of
the annual Austral meeting, which owes all its success to
the sight-seeing facilities provided by the M.C.C. ground.
To a casual observer there is no apparent reason why the
bloated cricket-club shouldn’t go right into the bicycle-
race management—form a sort of partnership with the
hard-up L.V.A., institute new Austral fixtures on the
dates now enjoyed by the M. 8.0., and boss the show in
commanding manner.
* * • *
“Jack” had a night out at Alhambra A.C.
(Sydney) last Saturday, when Bob Wade, of H.M.S.
Wallaroo, and Arch. Hempstead, of the Royal Arthur,
banged away at each other, in good old British style, for
the title of champion boxer of the Australian fleet and—
what was probably worth a hundred times as much—a
£l5 purse. Tars, marines, and petty officers formed
three-fourths of the big crowd present, and threw them-
selves into the thing heart and soul. Both principals
were grit to the backbone. Wade was slightly the
cleverer, but Hempstead had the better physique, greater
strength and stamina. There was no let-up from begin-
ning to end of the 11 rounds fought. Long shots were
conspicuous by their absence, broadsiders being the
order of the day. The pair stood toe to toe, dealing out
gruel smilingly, and taking it unflinchingly round after
round, and not once did the referee have to caution
either—even when firing was hottest—for a breach of the
rules, while their mates in the auditorium applauded im-
partially. Ultimately, the Wallaroo champion had to cry
enough through severely maiming his wrist, and Hemp-
stead was declared winner.
» * * *
Alhambra A.C. (Sydney) opening night last
Wednesday introduced strong man Wm. Pagel, who
played with heavy weights that bothered Lucifer and
Hajek, and showed himself away ahead of any who have
preceded him at the brawn business in N.S.W. He is 22
years of age, and finely put together, especially about
shoulders and chest. Last week’s show was Pagel’s first
public display of his remarkable strength, cultivated and
developed in Sydney.
* • * •
Mick Roberts, a rod-in-pickle for some time,
went down badly before black Tom Mitchell at Alhambra
A.C. (Sydney) Easter Monday night, but Roberts got the
big end of the £4O purse, for while he was prostrate in
round 11—to all intents and purposes “ out’’—and just
as referee Sid. Broomfield had counted nine, the colored
bruiser broke away from his seconds and, bounding
across the ring, landed a vicious punch on Roberts
jaw as that individual was trying to rise. The blow
cost the darkey 10 sovs., for, instead of getting the
winner’s portion (£25), he got the loser’s (£l5). He won’t
be in such a hurry next time. The fight was a disappoint
ment. Neither boxed up to expectations. Roberts, a
clever sparrer, proved a rotter as a fighter, and shaped
like an old lady.
* * * *
Rennert, the German Graeco-Roman wrestler
who has apparently scared Harry Dunn out of the arena,,
is an unusually fine specimen of physical manhood with
enormous chest-development and arms as big as an aver-
age man’s thighs. He is a handsome fellow to look at,
too, with well-cut features, large blue eyps, and a face
every line of which betokens a happy mixture of good
nature and determination.
There was an amusing occurrence at Golden
Gate A.O. (Sydney) last Saturday night. Coal-huod Ike
Stewart and Charley Weaner were boxing “ the beat of
20 rounds,” and things were brisk, with science cast to
the winds. For 10 rounds neither had much to boast of
over the other on the score of having given or received
punishment. Both went to corners much the worse for
wear and tear. The black had just sent for the referee
to say he was not going further, but Weaner, whose legs
had given way, got in first, and Ike secured the honors
and the fat end of the purse.
• * * •
Sharkey and Fitzsimmons are not to meet just
yet, despite all the newspaper fighting. Sharkey wants
to wait till Nov. ; to fill in the time, Fitzsimmons has
taken on Jeffries, the date fixed upon being May 26. It is
said Sharkey wants a rest, and no wonder, as he goes into
the ring with the full expectation of taking a gruelling.
And he is seldom disappointed. Maher, Choi’nski, Jeff-
ries, Ruhlin, M'Coy, and Corbett bashed him about ter-
ribly, whilst Fitzsimmons alone dealt out enough punish-
ment to use up an ordinary man for life.
* * * •
Details of the fight at Cincinnati (U.S.A.) Feb.
4, between Australia’s clever middleweight, Jim Ryan,
and Joe Walcott, the “Barbadoes Terror,” show that
Ryan fought in just the way that most people of Wal-
cott’s color prefer. Ryan refused to carry war into the
black’s territory, preferring clinching and sprinting tac-
tics in order to escape the negro’s fierce rushes. The
average colored bruiser is much less dangerous when
attacked than when attacking. All who have gained
decisions over Walcott did so by forcing the fight
viciously at every stage. Walcott does not like punish-
ment, and loses heart when opposed to a boxer who re-
fuses to break ground before his bulldog onslaughts.
Ryan’s apparent timidity and unquestionable unwilling-
ness to “get to it” convinced the ebon-hued one that
this vis-a-vis was not only averse to being hurt, but badly
scared by the fiendish grinning of his ugly opponent.
♦ * * »
Jim Jeffries, matched to meet Australian Bob
Fitzsimmons in America next month for the world’s
championship, is the man who made such short work of
poor old Peter Jackson upon the latter’s return to the
arena. The “ Los Angeles giant,” as Jeffries is called,
follows, when not fighting, the avocation of a boiler-
maker. He can use both hands well, and his blows are
delivered straight out with tremendous force. Ori his
feet he is fast and can break ground quickly and neatly.
Jeffries is said to be one of the most symmetrically-built
of men, His measurements: Height, 6ft. tin.; neck,
18in. ; chest, normal, 43Jin.; inflated, 48in. ; biceps, 16in.;
forearm, 12£in. ; wrist, 7%in. ; waist, 35in. ; hips, 42in.;
thigh, 26in. ; calf, 17£in. ; shoulders, 15in. ; reach, 76Jin. ;
and weight, 16st. 31bs. He is a bigger man than sailor
Sharkey, who beat Corbett and McCoy recently—except-
ing in the chest and neck. Sharkey’s neck is almost the
same size as Jeffries’, but the sailor’s chest is nearly 3in.
larger. At lung-testing Jeffries registered 3271b5.—71b5.
more than Sharkey.
* * * •
One M.L. beak fines bookies convicted of laying
tote odds a tenner each and gaol. No option about him.
The latest pig-horror comes from Guild-
ford (W.A.) The local swine were smitten with a
curious mortality, and when 40 had given up the
ghost it occurred to someone to enquire into the
cause. It was found that they had been fed on
hospital refuse, including bandages, poultices, and
other abominations which, instead of “curing”
the pigs, or turning them into medicated pork,
laid them out with marvellous regularity. Causes
of death were carbolic acid and belladonna
poisoning.
The N.S.W. Royal Agricultural Show was a
brave show despite the season, and perhaps superior in
general merit to any yet held. The inaugural luncheon
was a fine spread somewhat handicapped hy the dry
speeches ; no orator of over 100 words, especially by those
who know nothing about their subjects, should be tole-
rated on such occasions. Good Friday was an off-day,
almost a Sunday (though there were 50,000 people pre-
sent), and only relieved by the excellent jumping of the
ponies, a display finer than that of the horses, always ex-
cepting the bright particular star, Fairfield, and his
runner-up, ’Frisco. Perhaps it would be an improvement
if, without reducing the solidity of the jumps, they were
moveable, so that when the take-off became too cut-up
to be safe they could be placed on fresh ground. A
feature of the horse division was the sister to Correy—
Novelette—and her baby son by imp. Haut Brion, a pair to
be looked at. A grand exhibit, also by Mr. S. Hordern,
was his pair of upstanding bay carriage horses, which you
might readily imagine were straight from Rotten Row.
Another sight worth seeing was the style in which that
good but excitable little fellow “ Pippo,” was mounted
and ridden. Yet another prominent feature, but this
time a very indifferent one, was the alleged-buck-jumper
riding. Probably the long-haired, long-limbed Texan is a
good horseman —he sits like one—but his chosen Rosinante
was all too tame to show him (or throw him) off. Fair-
field, the champion of champions, has once again been
successful over the big jumps (this time carrying 15st).
A practice that must be stopped, or it will yet lead to
terrible accident, is that of permitting racing
trotters (or any horses) to extend themselves, some
going left and some right-handed round the ring;
also, that equally-bad fashion of putting on spurts
whilst the parade ground is crowded with all manner of
vehicles. The judging in the horse section, taken all
round, was most satisfactory, though at times bordering
on the monotonous —it was so slow. Want of punctuality
in parades and jumping contests was only too noticeable.
Some reliable person should take this responsibility off
the over-worked secretary’s hands, The water-jump
should be a separate item and not, attached to a timber
contest. There should in this event be a first and second
prize for the horses that clear the water best, quite inde-
pendent of length of jump. As it stands at present, a
horse might jump almost into the middle of the water, but
having taken-off a long way back he might still have made
the longest jump and thus become the winner. The
“Champion Coacher ” Freedom is well named, freedom be-
ing noticeable in every movement of this fine horse. There
were some high-class Arabs, of a kind likely to improve
our horses in point of quality, soundness of feet and con-
stitution. Many veteran breeders are firm (and justly
so) in their belief that it was the admixture of Arabian
blood that made our old-time journey horses so famous.
Cattle and pigs were shown of very high merit, but sheep
are a back, almost a missing, number. A splendid gather-
ing of exhibits and sightseers—a goodly shilling’s-worth,
evidently highly appreciated.

A month’s turhdver sit the rabbit-canning
works at Mt. Gambier, in the fertile S.W. of
S. A. : From Jan. 19 to 16th Feb., 135,929 carcases
were “put up.” Greatest number on one day,
10,802. Employes work night and day, two
shifts ; the co. has an order for 360,000 tins in
hand, to be delivered by the end of May. But the
rabbits of those parts are not the emaciated
wrecks of droughty western N.S. W., where,
according to popular belief, they put in most of
their time climbing trees for a mouthful of mulga,
and then scale wire-netting in the hope that they
may lick enough dew off the top wire to wash
down the sapless twigs.

On the selection of firemen :
Dear Bulletin.—lie “Jack Shay’s” protest (8.,
14/l/’99) against the fire brigades’ notion that sailors are
the fittest men for scaffold and ladder work. It has been
so completely proved that sailors do make the best fire-
men that the London fire brigades are made up wholly of
seamen. Being used to standing on tall scaffolds proves
nothing. Why sailors make the best firemen is that they
are used to facing dangers of various kinds ; in fact, their
whole life at sea is such that no danger can take them
wholly by surprise ; consequently they are, as it were,
“ educated ”in risk. Your average painter will, perhaps,
think nothing of mounting a tall scaffold, but it is a very
different thing to mount the same height with hook and
ladder, with fire bursting through every window around
him. How many painters would venture up a scaffold
swinging and pitching in all directions in a black, howl-
ing gale V They are simply not used to it, whereas Jack
has to go aloft in all sorts of weather, night or day. Pic-
ture him reefing topsails off the Horn in winter 1 Ship
plunging, gale howling, nothing between him and death
but the foot-rope and beckets, with the chance of being
knocked overboard any moment by a blow from the sail.
Thus does Jack get used to facing, almost daily, dangers
which the average painter would not face once in a life-
time, and thus also do the fire brigades know particularly
well what they’re about in their selection of men.—
H.R.A.
THE! AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newmanj the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to

be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
The organ-grinder claimant to the almost
barren Poulett title is not going to have it all his
own way. The old legal maxim, Pater est quern,
nuptioe demonstrant —a child born in wedlock is
the husband’s offspring—which is sure to be
trotted out in the Poulett trial, has been set aside
four times already this century. First in the
Knollys’ claim to the earldom of Banbury. Next
in 1821 Lord Gardner succeeded in setting aside
the title’s descent to a reputed son of his father by
the first wife. The elder Gardner was born in
Dec., 1802, but as his supposed father had not
seen his wife from Jan. BO to July 10 of that year
it was decided that it was impossible for a birth
in Dec. to arise from cohabitation after July 10 or
before Jan. 30—be., in 151 or in 308 days. The
law’s extreme limit is 208 days. (The Poulett
claimant’s birth was 175 days after the marriage.)
No. 3 was the Lord Saye and Sele case, in which
the wife’s lover frankly admitted the paternity ;
and No. 4 was the Earldom of Aylesford claim in
’B5. On Nov. 4, ’Bl, the Countess of Aylesford
bore a son, but as the Earl happened to have been
in America for at least a year previous to the
birth, when he died, Parliament upset the succes-
sion and passed it on to the deceased Earl’s brother,
the present holder. Parliament also has gone
further; in the Earldom of Macclesfield and in
the Marquisate of Townsend cases it passed an
Act dissolving the marriages and delegitimatising
the children thereof. In France, a child is the
husband’s legitimate offspring if born after 180
days from the earliest possible date of cohabita-
tion by the husband. This is roughly six lunar
months.

Bayswater, Sydney, October, 1898.
W. H. Malyon, Esq., Man. Chemist, Ballarat.
Dear Sir,—For years past (about ten), I have been
troubled with “ Acne ” in its worst form, and have sought
advice from the best Australian and London doctors, with
only fair results. A Victorian friend of mine, some four
or five weeks ago, sent me a tin of your “Ligmolliene,”
and speaking very highly of its wonderful curative powers.
I naturally took same “ Cum Grano Salis,” and was in-
clined not to use it ; eventually I tried your ointment
with truly marvellous results, and am now entirely cured;
and think people who suffer from skin diseases are greatly
indebted to you for your wonderful remedy. I shall
advise all my friends with skin troubles to use same.
Wishing ‘ ‘ Ligomolliene ” all the success it richly deserves,
Yours faithfully, GEORGE J. O’REGAN.

THE ARALUEN VALLEY GOLD DREDGING COMPANY is formed for the purpose of acquiring and working
certain valuable Properties by ihe New Zealand method of Bucket-Dredging.
The group of properties, comprising about 190 acres, which has already been secured for this Company by the
Promoters, is in the Araluen Valley, and is KNOWN TO CONTAIN RICH DEPOSITS OF GOLD, IN MAKY
PLACES UNTOUCHED.
For want of proper appliances to cope with the influx of water THE BOTTOM, W’HERE ALL THE WASHING
WAS DONE AND THE RICHEST GOLD WAS FOUND, HAS BEEN VERY LITTLE WORKED. This can only
be reached by the Bucket Dredge, or some similar modern method.
It will be seen by the Reports that from portions of this ground, in the old days, when Araluen was a famous
Alluvial Field, and when the primitive methods then adopted" lost much of the gold, THE CLAIM HOLDERS,
WHOSE RECORDS ARE NOW AVAILABLE, AND WHO WERE THEN WORKING UPON THE COMPANY’S
AREA, TOOK OUT 73,781 OUNCES OF GOLD IN LESS THAN THREE YEARS !
EARLY APPLICATION IS ADVISED. Prospectuses, Reports, and Plans on application to the Brokers.

Weary to the
Tips of My Fingers
A DOUBLE PROOF.
THE CASE OF Mrs. J. BOYT.
(by our special reporter.)
Our reporter having safely made his way
past two ferocious-looking watchdogs to call
at the residence of Mr. John James Hoyt,.
Collins-strcet, Marrickville,Mr. Boyt opened
the door and our reporter explained his
business. “ Walk in, please, and I will
introduce you to my wife,” said Mr. Boyt,
“ and she will tell you all about her illness
herself.” In the diningroom our reporter
found Mrs. Boyt, who required no pressing
to unfold the history of her complaint and
its cure.
“ Indeed,” replied that lady, “ it always
gives me pleasure to tell people about the
remedy that pulled me through
A Very Serious Time.”
“What was the matter, Mrs. Boyt.”
“ I overworked myself. In addition to
my household duties I had undertaken the
care of a sick friend. I attended to her by
day and by night, till at last I got into that
state that I could hardly crawl over to her
house. At last I broke down. A continual
weary feeling was upon me, even to
The Tips of - my Fingers.
It required an effort even to raise my hand.
When in health I have always a good appe-
tite, but then I didn’t care for anything. I
dreaded the night coming. Lying in bed
was a torture to me, for 1 could get no rest,
but tossed about, wondering what ailed me,
and trying to force myself into slumber.
But the more I tried to go to sleep the more
wakeful I became. In the morning I felt
I couldn’t get up, and when I at length man-
aged to reach the sittingroom I felt too lan-
guid and tired to do more than sit back in my
chair. I became very despondent as time
went on, I got so ill. I can’t describe the ter-
rible feelings that used to afflict me. I felt I
would like to get away somewhere all by
myself, where I could see and hear no one,
and there be still.
I Was So Irritable
and nervous that if anyone spoke to me
suddenly I should tremble and shake all
over.”
“ I presume this illness must have pulled
you down a lot? ”
“Of course it did. I was not like I am
now, but was not nearly so ill looking as
might have been expected from one who
suffered as I did ; but then my complaint
was seated chiefly in my nerves, and I am
not one to show much change in my appear-
ance when lam sick. lam perfectly satis-
fied, however, that if my nerve affection
hadn’t been properly treated in time it
w. uld have ended very seriously for me.
As it was I got into an awfully low state.
My nervous
System Was All Unstrung.”
“ But you recovered from all this rather
quickly, I am told, queried the reporter ? ”
“ More than * rather ’ —extremely quickly,
replied Mrs. Boyt most emphatically. My
husband was very much concerned for me,
for nothing—and I tried many medicines —
seemed to have the least beneficial effect
In the end he got me some Clements Tonic.
I thought it would be like all the other stuff
I had tried—about as useful as a glass of
water ; but to humour him, or rather because
I wasn’t strong enough to resist his pressing,
I took a dose.”
“You hardly expected to receive any
benefit from one dose ? ”
“ Now that’s just where you make a mis
take, replied Mrs. Boyt. Strange as it may
seem to you (and I must admit that a good
many people hardly believe me when I tell
them of it) that the first dose had
A Most Marked Effect
upon my nervous system. I felt somehow
as though I had had something like a good
strong dose of brandy, and yet not the same
feeling as the stimulation caused by spirits,
for there was no reaction. I felt as though
my nerves had been stimulated and invigor-
ated. When I took the next dose I wa»
overjoyed to find that I was not mistaken.
Clements Tonic was at Work,
for I improved with every dose, and I
thanked my husband most fervently for
having at last found me a remedy that was
able to strengthen my
Weak and Debilitated System.
I never missed a dose afterwards, and im-
proved every day—was able to eat with a
relish once more, and could sleep as soundly
as a top Under the influence of Clements
Tonic I soon threw off the cloud of melan-
choly which had been afflicting me. I grew
lively, and was able once more to go about
my housework cheerfully and with a real
pleasure.” J
“ And you owe that pleasant change—?”
“To Clements Tonic alone. Without it
I don’t know where my complaint would
have ended, or what would have happened
to me. You ask my husband and he will
speak more strongly than I have done on
that subject.”
Our reporter then turned to Mr. Boyt,
who was present during the whole interview.
“ What is your opinion, Mr. Boyt, as to
your wife’s recovery ?”
“I give the whole credit of it to Clements
Tonic, he replied, and I can endorse every
word of what my wife has told you. You
could actually see that remedy doing her
food and bringing her along towards health,
know the
Virtues of Clements Tonic
myself, for many a time when I have felt a
a bit down or oixt of sorts, or when I couldn’t
sleep or eat well, I have taken two or three
doses of that remedy.”
“ With the same result as in Mrs. Boyt’s
case ?”
“ Yes, it always put me right quickly,
and I never knew Clements Tonic to fail.
Do you know that if I want a pick-me-up
or stimulant I would rather take a dose of
Clements Tonic than a Whisky.
It props me up better, and its effects are
more lasting.”

Queensland electoral laws were always, and
are now, among the most unsatisfactory in Aus-
tralia. And yet Q. was the first province for
which one-man-one-vote was really proposed.
The author of the phrase, “ one-man-one-vote,” in
Australian history was Gov. William Denison,
who ruled in Sydney from 1855 to ’6l. At the
severance of Morton Bay from N.S.W. Denison
spoke and wrote in favor of trying the experiment
of one-man-one-vote with the new territory. He
insisted that it should be one-man-one-vote and no
man with more than one vote, but astutely con-
ditioned that each electoral district should have
at least three members. The working classes, he
argued, would all vote for one candidate, leaving
the wealthy to elect the other two. These were
the days before labor organisation was under-
stood. But, anyway, Denison’s proposal was
rejected.

a delicate inquiry made, had better write to J. W.

Under the Rose.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Oh, all you happy lovers, who may acknowledge
one another before the world, have a pity for us
the outcasts, who must meet and vow eternal con
stancy in secret, whose love prudence or morals
forbids. Your path is so smooth, you sit in snug
drawing-rooms, and discreet coughs announce the
approach of intruders. Everyone smiles on you
and approves of you. We must descend to artifice,
strategem, and ruse. We advertise in the “ miss-
ing friends ” column, or hide letters in a log or a
tree. You run to meet the postman, and smile
openly and happily over love’s eloquence. You
are artless, frank, sincere. We —well, never mind
what we are ; the words sound harsh.
“ We loved, sir—used to meet:
How sad and bad and mad it was —
But, then, how it was sweet! ”
We meet by the grass-grown banks of a swift
little creek, and from the shelter of tall bracken
and rushes glance guiltily now and again over our
shoulders —at what ? A cow or a horse. And we
scarcely dare whisper as we presently approach
the homestead, and part under the shelter of that
kindly old white gum. Or away on some lonely
road, with not a habitation in sight, where the acres
of dim country seem to slip into the far horizon
under the moon ; the horses tied to the wire fence-
crop the grass, and the sheep, huddled together,
bleat now and again, and we start, fearing someone.
Or down by a cliff near the sea, the stars track-
ing a pathway across the water, which rises and
falls, and whispers a crooning song to the gleam-
ing line of sand.
Or the city ; poor lovers when it is the city. It
is hard to find nooks for lovers in the city. In the
big, kind country, when the night falls, we have
the wide miles and each other. Yet even the busy
streets become dear when we have trodden them
side by side, and the glamor falls over all that is
prosaic and humdrum. But the marvellous charms
of those secret meetings, the hair-breadth escapes
from discovery, the dear jeopardy into which Love
leads us, they are something you can never know,
happy lovers. Does not the very openness and
smoothness of your path render it commonplace !
Do you value the prize so easily obtained ? Do
you really know what Love is ? But what true
lovers have not believed that to them alone has
been revealed the length, depth, breadth, height
and vastness of Love ?
We used to think we knew it all; even though
we might not confess to each other, there were times
when we believed we had reached the perfect
happiness. Life had been cruel, would continue
cruel, but Love had given all, and you whispered,
“ If Death would come now ! ”
But he did not come, and it has all gone far
into the past, and we are humdrum middle-aged
people, and have not seen one another for 20
years. If Death had come then, it would have
been better. Poor lovers !
There a K>» of pme»
dock, *“ d "? e whoro the bl.rd. oreaked, and down
the verandah wher t h e B heds where a
the lane so quietly, P fc crao kled under-

was fra g deliberate caution ! Then across
r Sip of open’S white logs lay on the
and one lingered fearfully by stump and
tree past the clump* of saplings where sometimes
u ®ng a grave native bear ; the tall, pale grass on
either sfde stretching away m indistinct
waves Then at last in the shelter of the pines,
the dr'v ground beneath strewn with pine needles,
and the branches above murmuring a song they
long ago taught ships to sing at sea Perhaps the
tall masts were stalwart pines in their youth, and
can never forget the forest-song which sweeps
through the solemnness of a chant, murmuring
dirge requiem, lullaby, then swells into sonorous
peal and blast of mad triumph or despair.
One leaned against an old brown bole with its
warm, resinous smell, listened and started at the
falling of a twig ; across the quiet night came the
far bark of a dog, the whirring of a cricket, the
“ womp-womp ” of frogs in the dam, and blended
with all the whisper and croon of the pines. At
last you would come, your steps indistinct, a
moment of doubt, then coming rapidly nearer.
Then —well you who are lovers know what then.
What a soft cushion pine-needles make ! What
a still, peaceful world we looked out on from the
shadow while we talked of ourselves, and our love,
and the risks we ran, or did not talk at all mid
often that was best. The minutes that dragged
in a very eternity of time while we were apart,
were hurrying into an hour when we were to-
gether.
“ I was afraid you could not come.
“ I could not have stayed ; my heart was beat-
ing in a very madness of longing for you.”
“Do they suspect, do you think?”
“ I daresay. Ido not care mow.”
A light twinkles far off —it is the home of a
newly-wed pair.
“ Ah, my dear—shall we ever What is to
be the end of it all ? ”
“ I daren’t think of it. I only live for the pre-
sent, and from one meeting to another.”
“ Did you hear my step?”
“ Yes—far off. At first I thought it was only
the pulse in my ears, but it came nearer, and you
are here.”
* * * *
Well, it is all past now; hot clasp, passionate
kisses, clinging arms. Judge not too harshly,
happy lovers. We break our hearts, you see, and
must lie and play the hypocrite, and meet by
stealth ; and we try to do what is right very often,
but, though you may not believe it, there are cir-
cumstances in which it is impossible to tell what
is right. And Love is stronger than anything but
Death, and perhaps it is stronger than Death also.
Some day we shall know.
Meanwhile we meet by stealth, and some of us
do our best in a hard place, and some of us don’t;
and when we suffer we wonder vaguely why Love
and morals are so often opposed. Yet, if you are
severe to those whom Love tempts too cruelly,
you must also give credit to those—and they are
not few—who withstand, and break their hearts.
But, as they are probably broken either way, it
does not matter much.
Vic.
MARS.
The Medium of Exchange.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Money? Not much!
That ain't my kind."
This is a story of warder Kinnealy, who is an
Irishman. All warders are, for that matter, but
warder Kinnealy is at once an Irishman and a
financial genius. The combination is not a
common one. The history of his house-furnish-
ing, truly told, is an object-lesson in finance, and
the trail of the broad-arrow is over it all.
The Briton boasts that his sovereign is current
all over the world, generally at a premium ; for it
is a coin containing twenty shillings’ worth of
gold, workmanship thrown in for nothing. But
in the community where warder Kinneally holds
sway gold is rarely seen, though money in the
true sense of the word is in common use. A
handy tobacco currency meets all requirements;
for purposes of small change wax vestas are always
available. There the value of a sovereign is quite
problematical, being estimated according to the
ease or difficulty with which it can be converted
into tobacco.
Dead Bird had a friend who did not mind carry-
ing money to him, but drew the line at a bulky
parcel of tobacco. So Dead Bird’s share of the
fiver his mate sent him resolved itself into two
sovereigns, and he was at once confronted with
the problem of obtaining a market for his rare
commodity.
The capitalist class was well represented in his
world, and he made his first application to a book-
maker commonly supposed to have a plant of
tobacco which taxed the holding capacity of a
kerosene-tin. The man of money had no objection
to acquiring “two jim,” but pointed out that he
was a long timer, and would have to keep them
planted for some years. Even then he could not
be sure of taking them out with him. The
offer he made Dead Bird seemed ludicrously in-
adequate to that hero, who refused it with scorn,
and went on to the next shop. He found he had
gone farther to fare worse ; sovereigns were a
drug in the market, and tobacco was as far away
from him as ever. Here he consulted the swine-
herd, who had earned the name of “ Partic ” by
dodging the weekly bath for many months. Yes,
Partic had a friend who would get them a pound’s
worth of tobacco for the two sovereigns ; his own
commission for furnishing the introduction would
be half the “ weed.” These conditions seemed
hard, but Dead Bird could do no better, and the
bargain would have been concluded if Partic had
not demanded the money. Dead Bird was too
old a fowl to be caught with such chaff, and
negotiations fell through.
That night warder Kinnealy spent ten minutes
in whispered conversation at Partic’s trapdoor ;
and, next morning, he conducted Dead Bird to a
vacant cell, with a curt order to strip and be
searched. After he had put him through some
gymnastic exercises, and examined his back teeth
carefully, he went through his clothes, and found
a sovereign between the upper and lower sole of
his boot. Dead Bird worked in the bootmaker’s
shop.
“ ’Tis well y’are dealing with an aisy man,
Dead Bird,” he said. “ Three months would the
magistrate give ye for this. Where’s the other
wan ? ”
Dead Bird said there was no other one.
“ Thin I must do my dooty.”
But the Drisoner was obdurate, and warder
Kinnealy had to retire with only one pound. He
was observed to make for the governor’s office;
and, as he entered, his face might have been seen
illumined by a new and tender light. Warder
Kinnealy had an idea.
Ten days afterwards the prisoner Williams,
alias Dead Bird, appeared before the governor’s
Court, charged with having in his possession
articles not issued to him by the Penal Depart-
ment.
Warder Gorman gave evidence that on the pre-
ceding Saturday he was on duty in the bathroom
of D division while the prisoners were bathing.
The prisoner Williams, who was having his bath
was seized with a violent fit of coughing,
during which the warder heard something
fall upon the floor. This proved to be a
sovereign (produced), which, in the warder’s
opinion, had fallen from the prisoner’s mouth on
to the floor. On being asked what he had to say,
the prisoner answered that he had found the coin
on the floor of the bathroom ; and held it in his
hand to give to warder Gorman when the fit of
coughing caused him to drop it. It had only been
in his possession a few seconds.
The governor said that, incredible as the
prisoner’s story sounded, he had reason to believe
that it was true, as warder Kinnealy had lately
reported to him that he had lost a sovereign on
the station. The prisoner would be cautioned,
and the coin handed to its owner.
So warder Kinnealy got “the other wan,” and
Dead Bird still languishes for tobacco.

DOAN’S BACKACHE KIDNEY PILLS
The woman who has to “ whip her-
self to every task ” is not a healthy
woman.
A little bit of work ought not to
tire a woman so. It wouldn’t if she
were a healthy woman.
There’s just the trouble. Seems as
though many women were doomed
never to know what it means to be
healthy.
The backache, which comes after a
little exertion, they think comes from
fatigue. Sometimes it does ; but. when
it comes again and again, it’s more than
that. It means deranged or diseased
kidneys. The diseased kidneys, instead
of filtering the diseased tissue from the
blood, allow it to remain. This c,i s
all kinds of troubles—Headache, back-
ache, irritable temper, dizziness, general
weakness. Will every woman who has
backache write us for a little book on
this subject ? It will be sent free.
And will you read what a Sydr ey
woman says of Doan’s Backache Kidney
Pills ?
Mrs. JAMES SHIELDS, living at No. 1 Holt-street, Surry Hills, says :—“ I formerly had no
feith in medicine of any kind, but was persuaded to try some of Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills.
I hey gave me relief, and after I had taken the whole of them I felt I was entirely cured. Before
this my state was miserable enough, as I could scarcely attend to my household duties through weak-
ness. To walk was a terrible exertion, and when I did so would have attacks of giddiness and
faintness. After sitting for any considerable time it was difficult for me to stand erect. I spent two
months in the hospital, being treated there for Bright’s disease, but to no purpose. I say without

Jack Wilkie.—A True Bill.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
The Hornby shows his sectors, seaward, landward,
white and keen,
But fails to light the darkness of the Board mis-
called Marine.
They are comic-opera sailor-men —they made their
biggest mark
And stopped their learning of the sea when Noah
beached the Ark.
They are dotty in the intellect—they stutter in the
skull,
They've lost their standing rigging, and they're
working off their hull ;
They're a joke upon the fo'ks'le —they make wild
amusement aft,
And they'd steer a steamer broadside, and they'd
rig the sprit abaft ;
If a ship has but a lazarette, though she's broken in
the knees,
They'll stifle the enquiry when we're sucked up by the
seas.
* • * *
By the staiths of the A. A. Co.—by the rattling
Plattsburg skips—
Where the coals from Earth’s intestines come to
freight the deep-sea ships.
In the taprooms by the markets ; in the bar of
Charley Lane’s;
By the Oyster Bank and Nobby’s ; by the scream-
ing Stockton cranes
This tale is told by sailor-men—a yarn of three
pound odd,
And how able seaman Wilkie went to sea to meet
his God.
The staith hath borne, the crane hath filled and
holdward falls the coal
With hammer blows that strain her planks and
vibrate to her soul;
They have filled her up for ’Frisco, the crazy com-
posite tank,
As leaky a tub as hogs afloat from Howe to the
Dogger Bank.
But filled she was with a sight of coal, and she
bent to her anchor chains,
For the weight of the freight within her gave
her several sorts of pains;
With eighteen hundred ton in her lap, and her
ribs and each deadwood knee
Cried out to each other to let it go and give the
coal to the sea.
And she cried in her pain as Jack Wilkie came
from the Quay to her buckling deck ;
And he said, “What a sight for a sailor-man !
why, I’ve shipped on a blasted wreck ! ”
Jack Wilkie was a proper man at drink or love, or
graft—
With thews like young eccentric-straps—and an
eye like a sunlight shaft;
He had lumped in the Townsville lighters where
the hot air has a taste,
He had sweated all navel-naked ’tween oil and
greasy waste,
He had trimmed—he had fired—he had held his
trick —for versatile was he,
He had done whatever a man may find to do with
his hands at sea.
And now as motherless and stiff as ever was sailor-
man,
He had shipped as able seaman on the coal-ship
Ghazee Khan ;
He had signed for a month’s advance note, and the
same was three pounds odd,
And in working off his old dead horse Jack Wilkie
met his God.
“You’re a sloven, you’re a touzle,” said Jack
Wilkie to his ship ;
“ Your stomach’s overloaded and you do not like
the trip,
When a waggonload at your forehatch knocks,
your nerves are moved to jumps,
And while the fulls and empties march your crew
is at the pumps ;
You’ve got no pride in your labor —and whether
it’s ship or hand
That’s got no pride in his labor—he’s makin’ for
the sand—
You could float home on your timber if you were
filled with Baltic deal,
But your oakum’s dead and spewing and you’re
sick from above your keel;
You’re tired of carryin’ over-loads—your repairs
ain’t been too free,
And you want to sleep on a South Sea reef an’ be
done with your walks to sea.
You look like a fourpenny Lizzie, and you
ought to look like a bride;
But your bonnet’s off, and the tops’l clews at your
mizzen are rove wrong side.
No ! You’re wantin’ an order to lay you up ; an’,
but for that three pound odd,
You should go to sea by yourself,” said he, “ and
not with me, by God !
But Circumstance—he steers this trick, and he’s
combined at me,
An’ he’ll take me down, an’ he’ll make me drown,
and he’ll laugh as I go to sea—
You may drink ashore, you may pay your score
with the pier-head jump and gait,
You may win from quod, by your fine, by God,
but you cannot baulk your fate !
An’ if I stayed ashore for fear of that Thief of
Men —the sea,
I might fracture my skull on banana-skins, or a
cart might run over me—
For the world is a great big shambles, and, whether
on sand or shell,
Every man must find the bottom slime, and the
decks of Heaven or Hell! ”
And he wrote to his girl at Ultimo, and he told
her his heart was sore,
And he said : “Don’t you fret, tho’ I believe I’ll
never come back no more ;
The Ghazee Khan’s pear-rotten, an’she’s makin’ her
funeral trip,
An’ I’m one of the hired mourners, an’ I’ll maybe
die with the ship ;
For I can’t desert—l’m motherless broke—an’ my
luck has set the course,
An’ I go down to the sea to drown with the ship
an’ my own dead horse—
For Circumstance has waited all these years to
make a catch—
He’s dropped me from the royals, he’s slung me
down a hatch,
And Circumstance has now combined—he’s forged
the final link —
And I die for the sake of three pound odd that I
squandered in meat and drink.
But don’t you cry, my pretty, maybe old Hard-
face Fate
Has left the final rivet out, and I’ll see the Golden
Gate ;
And I’ll write to you from ’Frisco, if so be that
we win in,
And if we don’t, my pretty, you’re saved from a
man of sin. ”
(He blackened himself as the man in love does
ever with sad delight,
His mottled soul he makes as tar, and lier’S
becomes more white)
And Jack was as good as they make ’em, tho’ lie
wrote “ I’m worse than you think,
I’ve seen Chalkers at Fremantle —I’ve been mad
with fight and drink ;
And the only think that ever I did that was good
and straight and true
Is the thing that is breaking my heart to quit—
the clean white love for you.”
* * * •
Past the Oyster Bank and Nobby’s the tug towed
the Gliazee Khan,
And she groaned in her bellyfulness like an old
and collicky man;
Her masts shook till her tops’ls filled, and the tug
had cast her free.
And she labored oilily along to the heart of the
open sea,
Leaving back at Newcastle wharf Jack’s Satisfied
Circumstance,
And the folks who’ve never been fft sea, mouthing
the sea’s romance.
Ten days at sea, and she belted on—queer choking
sounds arose
From her tortured hold to the listening deck, and
the breasthooks of the bows,
Floor, and futtock, and crossline —trenail, and bolt
and wale,
Ran their long agonised gamut from the rudder
to the rail.
Haze had followed the calm—underswell followed
the haze —
The sun in a dingy smoke had set for half a dozen
days ;
Like a candle ill-snuffed and smoking it glowed as
the dark came out,
And frightened all before and aft but the mate
with the twisted snout—
Practical, red, and little, the kind constant at
work and meals—
And he laughed when a sap-green comber stamped
aboard on iron heels—
A sea that was vagrant and friendless, but it
weighed two hundred ton,
And the gush ’twixt her bones and her metal
showed the damage that sea had done.
Under a goose-winged maintops’l, and her star-
board knees adrift,
While through her shifting cargo the waters roll
and sift ;
Straining and working off her frames, and the
seams mouth wide and grow,
And at every lurch they said that all of the star-
board side would go.
Like a man half-asleep under blankets, the sea
turned, rolled, and drew,
And rose in its awful hugeness to the ship and the
sentenced crew,
Long, winding heaves from the stale, dead sea
came slowly from below—
New noises out of the dropped mast-foot, from the
stern-post and the bow.
And, after the deadness, a small gale rose—’twas
nought to a well-found ship,
But it made the Ghazee Khan to wail and her
inner skin to drip;
Her broadsides plunged, and her sheets soaked
grey, and her copper gleamed the stars,
And she rolled her failing hull like a cask and
sighted the sea with her spars.
And her sweating pores gaped wide for the sea
that filtered through her coal,
And the men at the pumps ran an awful race for
the prize of the Life and Soul;
Windmill and hand and donkey, from the sea to
the reeking hold,
From the hold to the splashing scupper, while the
settling vessel rolled;
By steam and hand and gale they pumped ; they
pumped for the right to live,
With an ocean behind the smutty stream that
they sucked through their floating sieve.
The sea was behind and pressing hard in scorn of
their grit and gear;
And heaven was nearer than the land, and hell
was very near;
And, weary of struggle and worn with fight, their
strength and sperm gave out,
And they left the pumps to Jack Wilkie and the
mate with the twisted snout.
“Better to drown,” said they, “at ease, and
lazily yield our breath,
Than be water-blown after the work at the pumps
has wearied the soul to death.
Out with the boats ! Steer west by north, and
back to Sydney Bay !”
And lo! there were boats enough for some, and
some men had to stay.
Jack Wilkie stayed—he had kept the course—ana
the fate of the three pound ten,
And Circumstance and Hardface Fate, and the
scorefold skein of men,
Had hauled to two blocks on an oily sea, in a ship
all groan and creak—
The oak-wale crying for its life, and the growl of
the drowning teak.
They loaded the boats as the stars came out the
head of the Night to crown—
And the ship moved round like a full-fed dog
making ready to lay him down—
Moved round and round, and sank straight down,
as if she tried to kneel —
For her leaks were all over impartially, and she
sank on an even keel.
And her whimpers changed to music as the sea
took the weight of the hold,
And her coals slipped out of her cramping lap, and
the sea wrapped her fold on fold ;
Like a battered fourpenny Lizzie, sick of tramp-
ing the ocean streets,
And glad to be in hospital, and die in clean, cool
sheets.
• * * *
So West and North, the boats steered forth, and
brought this tale a-land
Of Wilkie’s Luck and Circumstance, and blind-
eyed Fortune’s hand —
The chain of Fate that was incomplete without
the three pound odd,
That sent Jack Wilkie to the seas a-squaring yards
with God !
You may booze ashore, you may pay your score
with the pierhead jump and gait—
You may win from quod, with a fine, by God —but
you cannot baulk your fate !
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
